The Red page
George Moore
It is time for George Moore to take his
own medicine; and Susan L. Mitchell neatly
administers it in George Moore in Maun-
sel’s “Irishmen of To-day” series (per Syd-
ney Book Club). Susan writes with a
pretty Irish wit; and George Moore now
has the privilege of feeling how his victims
in his great trilogy, Are, IS alee, atque Vale,
must have felt as he maliciously vivisected
them. She mentions that of the innumer-
able George Moores depicted in that author’s
literary confessions, there is only one George
Moore that he denies, “the little Catholic
boy of that name who went to confession.”
He whose whole literary life has been a
series of confessions, objects to the confes-
sional. Susan suggests that perhaps it was
the privacy of the confessional that
affronted him, “that so much good copy
should be wasted.” When his biographer
met George (in London) “he had' the rosy
face and innocent yellow hair of young
virtue, kindness was on his lips,.though his
eyes were not quite so kind, a little slow in
following his lips... .A man of middle
height with an egg-shaped face and head
a stout nose with thick nostrils, grey-green
eyes, remarkable eyes, a mouth inclined to
pettishness, lips thick in the middle as if a
bee had stung them. He had champagne
shoulders and a somewhat thick, ungainly
figure But he was the only man in Dub-
lin who walked fashionably.” Other people
call him “an over-ripe gooseberry,” “a great
big intoxicated baby,” ‘a satyr,” “a boiled
ghost.”
Susan devotes a whole chapter to George
Moore’s paintings. Here it is: “Nobody in
Ireland has ever seen any of Mr. Moore’s
paintings, except LE,’ to whom he one
shyly showed a head, remarking that it hac
some ‘quality.’ ‘ M’ remained silent.”
But it was his pathetic inability to pain;;
that made him write so gloriously about art
“No lover ever poured his unrequited lov
in sonnet with more passion than Mr. Moor
in his essays has sung his unrequited love o:
oil paint.” And it has been said of him tha
he had enough credulity to make him
bishop, but that he met Manet before h
met Christ, and that to Manet he has given
all he knows of worship. Being a woman
Susan does not believe in Moore’s confes
sions about females; she quotes with appro
val the remark of “the wittiest woman
Dublin”: “Some men kiss and tell, Mr
Moore tells but doesn’t kiss.”
Jt
Susan charmingly confesses: “Women are
only intermittently self-conscious, because
they live only occasionally in their minds a
all. Therefore no woman has risen up to
write a book containing the whole wisdom o ’
woman, and I for one pray that such a one
may never arise to profane our mysteries
For the most part, we look to men to revea
us to ourselves. Man is our logos, articu
late on our behalf. Women are curious, pry-
ing creatures, seeking mind. I can easily
explain to myself Mr. Moore’s ideas about
women and why they are not so offensive to
women as to men. There is often a wreck-
age of women about the lives of prominent
writers or thinkers or any men who stand
above their fellows. They attract women as
the lighthouse attracts the birds, and the
Wheeling creatures die of exhaustion, upable
to reach the light and unable to leave it.
Mr. Moore, perhaps, makes the common error
that it was his person and not the light they
fancied on his brow that lured them." There-
fore perhaps he and they are quits. The
sexes perhaps are always quits, and when
Mr. Moore says such and such a one was or
was not his lover, we women are unmoved.
We think him rather an idiot to talk so much
about it....We make him a present most
cheerfully of any little pleasure he gets out
of fancying we are in love with him while
we crane eagerly over his shoulder to read
what he writes about us. His main theme in
his novels is 10ve.... Funny person. Women
know instinctively all he knows about love
and more also. It is intellect we are after.
The intellect he brings to bear upon love we
wash out of his novels as carefully as the
miner washes the gold from the clay. In Mr.
Moore’s continual occupation with love' and
Jovers I find him less unpleasant than many
of the English novelists. There is a less
adhesive quality in his coarseness, and I
think it is because he has never been able
even to simulate passion Passion would,
perhaps, have spared us his tiresome pre-
occupation with what one might call milli-
nery and confectionery in the love adventures
in his novels. But ho-w absurd I am! Given
passion, the novels would never have been
written.”
Moore’s younger brother. Col. Maurice
Moore, is pilloried mercilessly in the trilogy,
but Susan shows that in this case and that
of other characters in the great book, George
built up their characters to suit his dramatic
purposes. When, after the publication of
the trilogy, Moore met Sir Horace Plunkett,
he was surprised to find that he had pictured
him falsely t ; then with a smile he said, “How
fortunate it was I jyrote my book before I
knew him!” An artist, you see, must not
g et too close to his subject. And of Moore’s
yoilSger brother, Susan neatly says: “George
Henry iVloore [their father] possessed a fine
honesty and frankness whir’ ’ ' >queathed
to his sons —tks hoffSety to Maurice, the
frankness to George.**
This biographer retails many delightful
anecdotes of George. Moore's conversion to
religion, she says, was conceived in the mood
of light comedy. “His conversion was as in-
stantaneous as St. Paul’s, and no doubt his
experiences enabled him later on to under-
stand the Apostle who is the hero of The
Brook Kerith. It is-possible that the light
which fell from Heaven on Moore was in the
nature of a literary inspiration, and he saw
as in a vision the book which he, a Messiah,
should write about an Apostle. Ave, Salve
and Vale occupied him at the moment, and it
was needful that The Brook Kerith should
be postponed for a few weeks. But the wise
litterateur, while working at one book, will
prepare his life for the next, and will be
collecting experiences.”
George Moore’s visit to the Holy Land, to
cull local color for The Brook Kerith, affords
Susan the chance for a series of delightful
comments. When Moore was searching for
a monastery sufficiently romantically situated
for his great story, his gaunt dragoman pro-
vided him with a small pony, the Arab him-
self being mounted on a lean roan. Plump
George saw himself cast for the part of
Sancho Panza. He would not depart on the
great errand of his life in such a role. lie
insisted on returning to the stables and get-
ting a gaunt Arab draught horse. When at
last, after, for him, awful adventures, he
found the sort of monastery he was looking
for, he subsided with the cry, “My God, my
bones, my bones!” “So tired was he that
when he saw a woman there among the
monks he did not even inquire what she was
doing there! As for the rest of the acts
and adventures of this apostle, are they not
written in The Brook Kerith?” But Susan,
mercifully, does not mention the story of
George Moore when gazing at the place
where the woman was taken in adultery.
Probably because George has told that anec-
dote so often himself.
War
Charles de Souza's “Third Phase” of
Germany in Defeat (Paul, per Dymock’s
Library, 65.) covers the period from the
first battle of Ypres to the German failure
at Verdun, taking in, of course, the chief
battles and movements upon the other
fronts. The author, with a lucid method
and a clear grasp of military strategy,
deploys his arguments with logical precision
Germany was defeated when, after the
Marne, she found herself in a state of siege
with all her machine-made military schemes
awry. According to de Souza, ever since
that defeat Germany has been engaged ii
keeping up a big political bluff, in the hope
that the deceived Allies would weary of the
war and propose peace. Germany’s aim has
been to impress the world with a sense oi
German invincibility. The operations since
ipres have therefore to be considered from
the political standpoint, and the various
offensives and attacks made by the Germans
on all fronts must be viewed in the light of
purely defensive action.
De Souza refuses to accept the much-
advertised view that the Russian retreat was
due to the shortage of munitions. That dis-
aster he considers due to the faulty strategy
of the Grand Duke. Having smashed Aus-
tria he determined to finish Austria, leaving
Germany to be dealt with later. The cap-
ture of the Carpathians seemed to the Rus-
sian commander more important than an
offensive in the north. “With such a pivot
:in_ their possession, the Russians, it was
said, could take Germany in flank from the
south; Hungary would be at their mercy;
the Serbs would be definitely relieved; and,
finally, Austria would be compelled to sue
for a separate peace.” So the Grand Duke,
thinking he held Poland securely, plunged
into his bold and daring mflffement, and
thereby lost the campaign. Russia cap-
tured the Carpathians—a brilliant feat of
arms, but had perforce to leave the rest of
her front weakly held, mostly by reserve
troops,. and so. allowed Germany to retain
her initiative in the north. For Germany
cleverly led the Grand Duke to believe that
she was in strength in the Carpathians,
Wh -A e s ® cretl y concentrating at another point
De Souza is clear on the point that it
was not the alleged shortage of munitions
that caused that calamitous Russian retire-
ment. For by the capture of Przemysl
Russia had acquired over a thousand guns,
small arms enough to equip two army Corps,
and enough munitions to carry on the whole
war for two nfonths. Russia’s retirement
was due to the enemy’s counterblow at a
spot where the Russians were the least pre-
pared to meet it, and de Souza assures us
that, even on this section no shortage of
munitions has been mentioned. It is. true
that eventually Russia ran short of material,
but. this was not felt until after the strategic
crisis was passed. There Is this to be said
for de Souza-s contention: such a blow de-
livered at the vital spot in the Russian line
is sufficient in itself to account for the Rus-
sian retreat. And if there is such an obvious
strategic reason for the retirement of the
whole Russian line, why should there be
any necessity for the wail about lack of
material . . For that outcry we must look
or a political reason. May it not have been
considered better for Russia to proclaim her
insufficiency of munitions than the tragic
mistake of the Grand Duke’s grandiose
strategy? A strategy, by the way, that it
is quite conceivable Russia will carry to a
triumphant conclusion next Spring.
De Souza describes Gallipoli as a venture
entered into on somewhat unsteady grounds,
and devised by experts who were prone to
subordinate strategy to politics. “Gallipoli
proved the barren grave of an army of
heroes, of brave men who had rushed in
thousands from the very Antipodes to fight
the Germans, but who ended miserably, if
gloriously, on the soil of Turkey It can-
not be said that the Dardanelles movement
exerted much, if any, influence on the centre
of affairs, except, perhaps, in a detrimental
manner to the Allies; for Britain, whose
means for land warfare were at the time
much limited, squandered a good deal of her
strength on the remote peninsula, and the
glory she acquired scarcely compensated for
the number of useful lives she lost.”
After pointing out the British failing of
making the least noise about their victories,
and the Hun’s cleverness in advertising even
his defeats as successes, de Souza comes to
the last card that the Huns had to play,
Verdun. “They had to choose between this
and ignominious collapse; or what would
still be worse from their own point of view:
unconditional surrender on the soil of the
enemy.” For to the West Front the Allies
by their various offensives had drawn rein-
forcements and reserves which Germany had
meant to use against Russia; these could not
be transferred to the east without leaving
the line in France dangerously weak.
Joffre’s nibbling tactics had scared the Hun ;
he never knew when a nibble might be the
beginning of a big bite. Germany deter-
mined to use these otherwise useless reserves
on a last desperate gamble. “These on-
slaughts which the enemy prolonged and re-
newed till the end, may be fittingly described
as the agony of the German Empire. To
make them, the Kaiser’s generals.... threw
into the roaring and blazing furnace the
major portion of the manhood of their
nation; they sacrificed wholesale, in dailv
holocausts, what remained of the flower of
the German race.” But de Souza affirms
that the Hun knew he couldn’t get through
at Verdun; his plan had a political, and not
a military, end in view: he wished to im-
press and discourage France and force peace
upon the Allies through her. The job had to
be done in a hurry: Britain was getting
ready. So the. last act of the drama, was
opened. “Joffre held them now in his mas-
terly and deadly grasp; he had been egging
them on all along He had forced the Ger-
mans to undertake a new offensive with no
military prospects for them whatever; and
they were now condemned by their own
action, and Joffre’s persistent refusal to
attack them, to squander the rest of their
forces in a mad and hopeless endeavor.” In
support of his view the author calls atten-
tion to the purposeful inactivity of the
strong British forces during the Verdun
battle. “Joffre knew his own game. Untimely
action on the part of Haig would have
forced the Germans to adopt the defensive
everywhere, and thus would have prevented
them from wasting their strength as they
were doing at Verdun.” But, against this
fact, it is stated now—too late for inclusion
in de Souza’s book—that the Somme Push
was prematurely begun in order, for one
thing, to relieve the strain on Verdun,
Germany in Defeat is a useful antidote to
the pessimism that pervades the Allies: our
pessimism must be nothing to that of the
Central Powers. And the recent hectic and
hurried efforts made by the enemy for an
immediate peace strikingly confirms de
Souza’s arguments.
Neil Lyons is welcome with A Kiss from
France (Hodder and Stoughton, per Sydney
Book Club; Is. 6d. here). His soldiers are
real Tommies —so real that nobody would
mistake them. for Billjims. This collection
of sketches is slight, but written by an
artist. Listen to little Wauehope, the man-
killer: “Yon was such a great, stout, beery
chap wi’ a beard; an’ me such a little chap,
and me to kill yon!” he thought as he found
his German. “Och, aye! But I killed him
sure eneuch. He made no proper stond at
all. He held his weapon that onhandy I had
no even tae counter his point. It were no
braver than fechtin’ a sandbag to fecht
yon. He licked hia fat lips, an’ looked at
me wi’ his eyes, like a frechted sheep. Ah
pit my weight behind the lunge, and said ma
wee bit prayer, for Ah’ll no do wilful
murder, ond Ah twisted it into him, the way
the drill-book tells ye. And he saidna’ any-
thing but jest ‘Oh!’ And he fell at ma fit;
him and his big boots and his beard and cross
and a’. He saidna' anything at a’, jest‘Oh!’
.... Ah seen then as he was no a young
man. He micht ha’ been ma faether. Ond—
Ond—-Ah, well! Wha wad fecht his
faether or wha wad fecht a sheep?”
Then there is Pte. Smiff, whom, with his
mates, Lady Stumper entertains at her coun-
try residence. To that good lady’s astonish-
ment Smiff objects to Aunt Sally—everywhere
they are invited they are expected to play
that game. Smiff tells his hostess he doesn’t
mind being invited out. “We likes to keep
the people cheered and happy If I can do
anythink to keep a civilian in good ’eart, to
keep a civvy’s spirit up, to keep their minds
away flttsm thinkin’ childish about this war
—why, Ladyship, I’m glad to do my bit.”
And Mr. Smiff expounds the old soldier’s
theory of foreigtf'Amaking, in the delight-
ful story of “The Belgese Girl.” “I’ve found
’em all alike. All friendly, all agreeable, all
female. .. .And I say distinct as no little
difficulty about languidge ever stood in my
way—or theira. Chinese, Burmese, Maltese
or Belgese, it has always been the same to
me. I grant ya this, guv’nor, mind —there’s
talent on both sides. I got a gift for it, mind
ya. I can talk foreign. I can’t talk Chinese,
nor I can’t talk Burmese, nor I can’t talk
Maltese, nor I can’t talk Belgese; but I can
talk foreign. And how he talks foreign to
that little Belgese girl, “two sandbags high,
she were,” makes the most humorous yarn
in this particularly bright collection.
Some Novels
John Buchan writes the usual novel of
adventure and mystery with almost a liter-
ary sfyle. His latest, Greenmantle (Hodder
and Stoughton), is crisp and up to date.
Two British officers and a benevolent, dys-
peptic “nootral” are sent on a special ser-
vice mission to Turkey, with instructions to
discover what Germany is up to among the
Mohammedans. Rumors of the advent of a
new prophet and a holy war, backed by the
Hun, and the usual mysterious clue, com-
prise all their information. Each of the
three men takes a different disguise and a
different route to reach Constantinople. That
of the narrate” '"'•fle him through Germany
at war. Consequently, there is always some-
thing doing. And when the three rendezvous
at Constantinople, they learn that their
troubles are merely beginning. The finale
takes place at Erzerum, and of course it is
due to the three heroes that the Russians
are able to capture that city. The mys-
terious female is introduced, but fortunately
doesn’t marry the hero. Brisk adventure
and a breathless plot, with heroes that can
be depended upon to possess charmed lives.
Algernon Blackwood has come to the end
of his remarkable imagination. The Wave
(Macmillan) is a short story tiresomely
expanded into a long novel. The theme is
big enough, but it is an out-size for the
trivial story it is meant to fit. “While some
think evolution moves in a--straight line
forward, others speculate fancifully that it
has a spiral twist upwards. At any given
moment the soul looks down upon a passage
made before—but from a point a little
higher. Without living through events
already experienced, it litera'rily lives them
over; it sees them mapped out below, and
with the bird’s-eye view it understands
them.” So Blackwood tries to convince us
that his three characters merely live over
again a tragedy enacted by three inhabitants
of ancient Egypt, But Blackwood fails to
make out his case that the triangular con-
flict between two men and a woman in
Egypt is motived by the compulsion of the
past. It sounds just like an ordinary love-
affair. There is no need for the author to
drag in the Ancient Egyptians. Those blokes
were probably quite like us; their life must
have been no more interesting than our
lives; there must have been Ancient
Egyptian grocers and Ancient Egyptian
navvies and Ancient Egyptian wowsers and
Ancient Egyptian flappers—rather like the
modern variety. And Blackwood’s long-
advertised climax, his hero’s dream of a
wave that overwhelmed him and two others,
is merely a brief and bathetic sand-wave
caused by a desert whirlwind, Poor stuff.
Exit Algernon.
Jt
J. Store* Clouston, author of that delight-
ful extravaganza, A Lunatic at Large,
keeps up his humorous vein in Two's Two
(Blackwood), a farcical treatment of the
Jekyll and Hyde theme. A respectable
baronet has the theory that every ’man con-
sists of two or three different men rolled
into one, and he is in himself conscious of
two sides of his nature that are acutely
contrasted. One side of him is a philan-
thropist and a social reformer; the other
side suffers from lapses, especially where
females are concerned. He determines to
experiment in dissolution, with the serious
intention of sorting but the serious side of
his nature from the irresponsible side. In
order that his better self should survive the
test he prepares himself by reading all the
most stimulating parts of Paradise
Regained, the Order of the Morning and
Evening Services, and selections from
Hamlet. To his annoyance he discovers that
he has become two men. He has merely
his lighter and his more solid
half. One of him is a charming youth, the
other is an austere wowser. Archibald, as
the youth calls himself, for the reason that
ho has never known an Archibald who wasn’t
a successful co-respondent, embarks upon a
joyous career of irresponsibility, and his
adventures, especially with women, are
irresistibly humorous. The wowser half
also compromises himself, especially with
the girl he loves. The complications that
result are highly ingenious and diverting.

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Little Song.
Night’s cloudv counterpane is tucked about
the sleepy town.
By two and two with blinking eyes the little
stars look down.
I wonder if they see us here together, you
and I,
Alone, my lips upon your cheek —and if they
wonder whs^?
The golden rosebud of the moon is drooping
wistfully
To hear the song the low wind sings above
the shining sea ;
A little, crooning song of Love that comes,
unseen and shy,
Into our hearts awhile, and goes so soon —
we know not why.
Your little hand is warm in mine, your voice
I cannot hear.
The world is very far away, and you are
very near.
O love that lives but in a kiss, and breathes
but in a sigh!
To-night life flings our answer back to
Death’s eternal “Why?”
N.S.W.
F. S. BURNELL.
Callao.
All day the surf springs roaring on the sand,
The silver sand a-swoon beneath fierce
skies,
And gorgeousness runs riot through the land,
And nowhere is there rest for weary eyes.
For eyes that ache with tears that may not
fall,
For white clouds scudding o’er a town high
flung
Above a bay where grey gulls wheel and call,
And all the earth is green and morning
young.
And brave eyes frankly meet a neighbor’s
gaze,
Unlike the shifting eyes I meet each day
On whose dropt lids some shrinking terror
weighs
To hide the dreams long crucified away.
I would that I might feel the gusty sweep
Of high, cold winds where hill streets sky-
ward lean ;
I would my eyes their bitter ache might weep
In salt tears for the old-time things and
clean.
Had I done prison penance for my deeds
(God, will that surf no instant cease its
boom!),
Sweet hope, maybe, had sown some little
seeds
That could have flourished to a perfect
bloom..
The ravelled ends of life to some fair scheme
With patient fingers I had worked anew,
And she of whom by day and night I dream,
With threads of gold had run the pattern
through.
But no one here is what he might have
been— -
O haunting faces with the eyes of gloom!
0 hands befouled that never can be clean !
God, will that surf no instant cease its
boom!
N.S.W.
JOHN EGAN.
Wet Weather.
Pools upon the pavement, round as pallid
moons;
Sobs within the .doorways, tears upon the
pane!
High up in the housetops the cool wind
croons;
The dim streets of Wellington are musical
with rain!
The tramcars of Wellington go drooning
through the hours,
Waking fountains fi-om the rails where
rinkling rivers race;
And each is like a brown bee amid the dewy
flowers,
For each is like a brown bee with dew upon
its face.
And the tramcars of Wellington, the little
weeping cars,
Are filled with wagging round heads like
peas within the pod ;
And the wee streets of Wellington know
neither sun nor stars
When Wellington is hidden in the flowing
robes of God.
But the wet folk of Wellington go laughing
to and fro —
Oh, every heart’s a merry heart that’s shel-
tered from the rain ;
And a grey phrase whispers of the storms
of long ago,
And a gay lip is singing that the wind will
swing again.
The garden of the Cityside is breaking into
bloom —
Shop fronts are tulip beds, and some are
daffodils;
And lights like early primroses are showing
’mid the gloom
Behind the swaying curtains, above the
window-sills.
The swift winds of Wellington may swing
into the West,
The clouds o’er Terawhiti may break within
the South;
The rain-song of Wellington will linger in
my breast,
For the moist kiss of Wellington is music
on my mouth !
MAORILAND,
BOYCE BOWDEN.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 11, 1917.
AUSTRALIA AND WORK—OR PARTY
AND SHIRK.
Agitated partisan barrac-kers and assorted
job-hunters continue to snuffle that it is the
one duty of the Federal Parliament “to pro-
secute the war.” What they really mean is
that Parliament ought to do nothing at all.
which is what it has been doing for the last
three years. The interests of Australia in
this matter are not, however, confined to
watching performances on the political tight-
rope or making bets on the balance of party
groups. To limit the work of Parliament in
the way suggested is equivalent to utilising
the war as a means to an end, that end being
the perpetuation of the anomaly presented by
the Hughes Ministry. And that anomaly
can only be perepetuated by putting party
considerations' first, ignoring the constitu-
encies and cutting out of the political pro-
gramme matters of vital concern to the
nation. A Cook-llug ties understanding, or
whatever it might be, could no doubt sustain
a Ministry in office, for a time, at any rate.
But it would be more or less under the heel
of the Senate, and it would inevitably be a
Ministry conducted on let-it-slide principles,
filling the air with loud talk about prosecut-
ing the war and the infamy of the Germans,
and under cover of an uproarious patriot-
ism and yells about the 1.W.W., solemnly
doing nothing that mattered for Australia.
Its great justification would be that it was
verbally keen on the war, and kept the
Labor crowd off the front seats. A Ministry
for which no more than this could be said
would be as terrible a revelation of what can
happen to a democracy as the existence of
the Grab Government in N. S. Wales. That
lot of quick-change artists have the war
flag at the masthead, too. They have dis-
franchised the electors in order to carry on
the war with greater energy. And as they
have no more to do with the wax’ than with
the King of Spain, they are hanging on to
their jobs and assisting the Allies by spend-
ing millions on something else, while making
a few disjointed remarks and finding easy
billets for Gregory Wade and John Henry
Cann.
* * * *
It is only true in a restricted sense that
even the first duty of the Federal Parliament
is to prosecute the Avar. It would be childish
to pretend that the contract for defeating
the German absorbs so much of the
time of members that everything else
must be excluded. There is other work in
greater profusion than ever for Parliament
to tackle. There are problems arising out of
the war, conditions created by it. Are all
these to be allowed to go drifting and un-
attended to? Are the multitudinous public
men infesting Australia going to keep on
sticking their heads in the sand, incapable
of even attempting to grapple with the chaos
they know to be piling up around them?
The Australian tariff clamors for review.
The nation’s debts and the borrowings by the
States and the interlacing of State and
Federal taxation are subjects that only
coAvards would avoid coming to grips with.
The strike disease is iflpe for treatment, and
the arbitration system clamors either for
repair or for the scraplieap. The financial
responsibilities of the nation are reaching a
point where anything may happen. To leave
these subjects alone—to disregard the de-
mented nature of the Federal arrangement in
relation to the States —means that when
peace comes to the world Australia will be
struggling up to the neck in muddle, heavily
weighted by the consequences of political
ignorance and shirk and idolatrous worship
of the party joss.
* * * *
What the general aspect will be when the
Avar is over no man knows with certainty.
But it is not necessary to rely on guesswork
to get an idea of at least some of the scenery
that will be opened up. The public debt will
be enormous, making demands upon the tax-
payers which, if prophesied a few years, ago,
would have sounded incredible. The national
borroAving and spending will stop abruptly.
Wages will have to come out of something
that is not a p.n. Either the volume of pro-
duction for export and of manufactures for
domestic consumption must be greatly in-
creased or the bill will have to be paid by
a people ground to a state of poverty. . What
preparations does the Federal Parliament
think should be made to meet these inevitable
circumstances? Or does it imagine that it
has discharged its duty to Australia by going
into recess and blithering about prosecuting
the Avar, when all the prosecution possible
at this end consists in the capture of an
occasional recruit? An excuse that Aus-
tralia was unprepared for peace because dur-
ing the war Parliament did nothing but
confine itself to conducting the struggle and
accepting holidays would have a mighty queer
sound to people who know how abject the all-
round shirk has been. Nor will it be possible
by phrase-making about the Empire and
secret juntas and Germans for the Federal
Parliament to hide from the people that in
the realm of public and private expenditure
the grossest waste of the national strength
has been encouraged at a time when such a
thing is criminal. Australia is importing
goods at a greater rate than ever. It is a
scandal that Parliament should alloAV this to
go on —that so much of the money spent from
loans for war should be allowed to leak aAvay
for foreign-made goods. Such conduct on the
part of the State amounts to economic can-
nibalism and a prostitution of the tariff.
Australia is actually paying foreigners tAvo
millions a year for lollies and chewing-gum
and the gaudy boxes they come in. All this
is waste of what every other country on
earth is struggling to save. That it should
be going on is a mockery of the nation’s
necessities. And the tragedy is that the
Federal Government’s own record as a
Avaster of the public estate is so appalling
as to invite derision of any suggestion that
it should stop the wastage perpetrated by
the private individual. There is here alone
opportunity for Parliament to get busy on
something that would count. t This cry
about concentrating on the war at its best
means more shirk, and that is something
Australia cannot afford to have much more
of. At its worst it represents the degrada-
tion of a patriotic impulse for the ignoble
purposes of political pigmies. Australia
never Avanted a National Government so
much as it does to-day, but a Government
that called itself National and baulked at
the nation’s problems would be an enemy of
the Commonwealth.
THE BLOCKADE AND THE BER-
SERK NATION.
When Morgan, at the head of his 1100
pirates, crossed the narrow part of America
to attack the rich Spanish city of Panama,
he ought, bv all the rules of the game, to
have been beaten by the journey before he
got within sight of the plunder. The men
who followed him were starving and in rags,
and had suffered agonies of thirst through
the thoroughness with which the Spaniards
had poisoned all the wells. Things were so
bad that they had eaten strips of leather-
bags for lack of anything better to chew.
Yet their purpose never wavered. At last, as
the scarecrow army approached through the
open country, Panama sent forward 4000
lighting bulls to smash it up and utterly
annihilate it. The result was different from
any that might have been calculated on.
Morgan’s famished men saw beef; and that
settled the fate of Panama. The idea that a
nation can be starved into a feeling of bro-
therly kindness for its enemy was thus shown
to need qualification. Austria —which is not a
nation, hut a collection of States of varying
race and language chained together in an
artificial fashion —may throw in the sponge
when the cupboard is sufficiently bare ; but
to pin our faith to the belief that Germany
can be broken without blows is horribly un-
safe. What the war was really about and
who is responsible for it are dark secrets
which the German people have never
been permitted to know. The tendency
of the blockade, however, is to make
the issue clear to every German mind.
England, France and Russia stand between
the people of the Fatherland and their fat
feeding. To win is to eat to the full. To
be defeated is to continue more or less hun-
gry. This brings things down to the bed-
rock of simplicity; and.the rush of a people
united in starvation may have any sort of
result.
An examination of the forces which first
made scattered trines into a nation indicates
that the blockade by stronger combinations
has usually had a lot to do with it. Two
sets of people who have joined together to
achieve victory are not likely to be nearly so
closely knit together in the years to come as
two who can boast that they starved in
unison when the wheat was burned and the
last cow faded away. More than that, the
pride of filling the mouths of the hungry
is the highest pride of all. The warrior wl.o
goes out to slay doughty cnampions is quite
small potatoes' compared with the liope-
bringer who strikes a blow to win bread for
the women and children. There are big prin-
ciples involved here—principles which have
operated all through human story and which
are likely to operate again even in an age
of machine-made warfare. The force of the
blockade of German ports, with the conse-
quence of a shortage of sausage in Berlin
and Dresden, must be to harden the Ger-
mans into a more solid nationality. The
offer of a peace conference has been turned
down. It was impossible that it could be
considered seriously while the burglar of
Europe still had hands and feet on the
plunder; but, in turning it down and main-
taininer the severity of the blockade, the
Allied nations have to look for developments.
Mastering all other thought and dwarfing
all more highly-colored ambitions, the great
desire for something to eat may rouse the
German people to the point of fervor at
which they will hurl themselves berserk
against all their foes. In that possibility lies
the Allies’ best chance, and their gravest
danger. Meeting on equal terms, a well-fed
man ought to be able to hammer one who has
been starved; and a nation with breakfast
still in its system should smash a nation
whose sparse bread is adulterated with saw-
dust. But the danger is that the berserk
fighting may not be on equal terms. Poison
gas. liquid fire, the enslavement of con-
quered peoples and the murder of non-com-
batants have all been employed by the
Teuton to keep up his end when he was only
moderately pressed about the stomach and
had big ideas of winning. To what other
and more awful expedients might he not
resort when as a nation he goes berserk to
grab the sausage or die in the attempt? A
hunger-maddened Germany might well be for
a few months the most terrifying proposi-
tion any combination of nations has ever
been called to face. Knowing that, and
being prepared for it, all may be well. Fail-
ing to recognise it, the battle of the Somme
might easily enough have a sequel like the
splendid landing at Gaba Tepe.
Having thus very probably hardened and
consolidated the enemy, what have we done
to meet the danger? Britain and France
are, without question, now putting forward
all their energies. Australia, on the other
hand, has not yet wakened up to. the fact
that a Avar is going on in which we are
more than gravely concerned. A number
of widows and stricken mothers know;
but the politicians cannot yet be per-
suaded that the northern controversy is more
than a large-sized picnic vvith firework
accompaniments. It is partly for this reason
that, on all appearances, our children are
likely to be compelled to pay for a good deal
more than we achieve; and a bad bargain
made in. a war-time when the public suffered
nothing is likely to result in imposing some
of the natural war-time conditions on the
long succeeding years of peace. What Aus-
tralia had a right to demand AA T as good value
from the foe for all her expenditure of cash
and life; but before she could make such a
demand she had to be in earnest about it.
And so, with the whirligig of time working
its old vaudeville turns, we are forced to the
belief that Australia’s own hungry years will
come Avith the cooling of the cannon, and
that when the people know the price they
are paying they will recognise that the return
got on the battlefield for our mild entrance
into the war made the bargain a bad one.
It might be worth paying our last shilling—
if Ave could chase the elusive coin to its lair
—to land the full force of our blow on the
Kaiser’s pride; it is an open question
whether less than our full force is worth
considering as a punch at all. But the
lean years to come may have the effect of
welding us into a nation. There is hope
there at least. As the blockade may simplify
the war outlook in Germany, so, when we
discover the fact of our own political block-
ade, the meaning of a lot of other things
will be clear. For Australia is blockaded
and narrowed in by small schemers and
shallow inefficients to a point that threatens
the baby’s bottle and Cissy’s ice-cream.
Some day we, too, may go berserk.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Spree that Total Prohibition Is
Wanted For.
The man who has been paid off from a
Government job and is looking for another
somewhere else is becoming fi’equent in N. S.
Wales. “Starvation and distress” are being
spoken of. And at the Trades Hall violent
language is being used about the Ministry.
The point of this condemnation seems to be
that the Government is not keeping the men
employed by borrowing more cash to do it
with. But the real misfortune is that the
Trades Hall did not long ago damn the
borrowing it wants to see more of—that even
now it cannot realise how ruinous this
demented business is to the wage-earners and
the State. It is all very well to blame Minis-
tries for men being out of work —the politi-
cians richly deserve all the harsh things that
may be said of them—but Labor itself cannot
escape responsibility. It has persistently
encouraged artificial labor conditions. It
lias been the most reckless pawner and
the most profligate spender among all
the political parties Australia has known.
Partly because of blank ignorance, very
largely on account of laziness, and
to some extent because of a habitual
preference for the glib tongue, organ-
ised Labor has accepted from Parliaments as
a substitute for work the frequent visit to
Cohen. And with blind devotion it has fol-
lowed politicians who had nothing better to
offer the people than bird-lime. Of course, it
all had to come to an end some time; and
if the end of the section is close —well, Labor
will have the pleasure of knowing that while
it had been playing the fool, Fat has got
away with the proceeds, and that the situa-
tion is a little worse than it was originally.
That men should be unable to find employ-
ment in Australia is dreadful. There is work
to be done far beyond the capacity of the
present population to perform. But so long
as the public policy consists of putting a
plaster of debt over the carbuncle, industrial
development, will be deferred. It Avould be
more than a good thing for Australia if its
numerous Parliaments could never borrow
another stiver. Then a nation’s problems
could be worked out honestly, and the people
by their brain and muscle achieve what too
many of their public men are shirking.
The Returned Soldier.
Between the politicians, parsons, miscel-
laneous persons who want to get into the
limelight, the newspapers nosing round for
something to yell about, and the administra-
tors of various funds, the returned Australian
soldier is beginning to look like the first
stage of a nightmare; and the prevailing dis-
order threatens to collapse into still another
heap of chaos. The fact that an immense
amount of effort has been expended in order
to meet the case of the returned soldier, and
that the public is afflicted with an almost
pathetic anxiety to play the game towards
the man in khaki, makes the position at the
moment no better. Nor will it prevent the
situation getting worse, and that is so
because (1) of the failure to systematise the
business in hand and (2) of the wildly
extravagant talk and fantastic promises that
filled the air when the Australian army was
being raised by the cursed “voluntary” sys-
tem of disguised coercion and bribes.
Where are all the “farms” that were talked
about, and the supplies of cash to start
“businesses or factories” ; where is all the
machinery that was going to be put into
operation to see if men were “fit” to be
cockies, and to instruct them and weed out
the “unfit” who clamored for farms and do
all the rest of it? This paper does not know
and cannot find anyone able to explain. But
it does know that thousands of men have
been deluded into thinking that there was
something more in it than hot air, and
this, combined with the absence of any clear
understanding of Avhat a soldier is entitled to
look for from the Government, has led to
developments a long way from creditable.
Meanwhile The Bulletin wants again to
say that there are returned soldiers and
returned soldiers, and in its judgment the
variety which matters is the one which is
incapacitated—that the sick and wounded
man, the orphan and the widow have the first
claim to public cash and consideration. It
is a crime against these people, a misapplica-
tion of a trust, that any part of the relief
funds now in existence should be diverted to
men as able as their neighbors to fend for
themselves. Such men when they attempt
to prey upon these funds are suckers, and the
brutal truth is that the more opportunities
there are to suck the more suckers there will
be. It is a fair thing for us to remember
that there are Australian soldiers dying in
the trenches and hospitals still, that men are
being maimed and broken for life, and that
our responsibilities to them are not likely to
be adequately recognised under first-come-
first-served principles. This paper has no
time for the ex-soldier who, fit and well,
hurries round in search of a dole. It is the
other fellow who ought to be looked after,
even if his groans are not so loud; and Aus-
tralians ought not to forget it.
Immortality.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The wandering winds that blow about the
world
Are filled with voices, ardent whisperings—
The long-drawn sighs of valiant bugle-calls
That rally lone patrols to deathless death.
The last, stark courage of uncounted men
Goes like a thrilling sigh down streams ot
air;
And sunsets bleed like an heroic wound,
And all the void is quick with selflessness,
Disbodied, and faith breaks from stems that
flower,
And inspiration breathes through twilight
grass.
Oh, fields of France, heights of Gallipoli,
And Alpine passes lifted to the stars,
And Macedonian hillsides, and the wastes
Of bitter Poland, and the meadow lands
Of Flanders, evermore as holy ground
Ye shall be known and named! The flower-
ing grass
Has drawn its life from their immortal dust
Who keep forever the stark heights they
won ;
And the iEgean, mourning in her caves,
Croons of the courage, and the martyrdom,
And of the sweet sleep of the mighty dead.
The shining glaciers are white altar-stairs
Whereon the naked soul of man was raised
To the mute heavens. The blood-sodden
earth
Of France and Flanders and the Polish
steppes
Is of such dear, yet awful, sanctity
That whoso in the years to come shall pass
At sunset by ploughed fields that now are
red
Shall surely, with a leaping heart, behold
Ranked armies, shoulder unto shoulder set;
Their lifted faces lighted as with flame,
Their tattered banners laurel-wreathed, their
blood
Sprung from the soil in roses, and the sky
Above them gold—an arch unspeakable.
The wandering winds that blow about the
world
Are filled with voices, ardent whisperings—
The long-drawn sighs of valiant bugle-calls
That rally lone patrols to deathless death.
N.S.W.
DULCIE DEAMER.


Holman’s Tote.
When Premier Holman was still a Labor
Premier he opposed the introduction of the
totalisa-tor into N. S. Wales on the ground
that he had “conscientious objections” to the
acceptance of revenue from a gambling
machine. As Red Tape was busy collecting
income tax from Ikey, all this was obvious
nonsense and it looked no better when Hol-
man made obscure remarks about the “dubi-
ous reasons” which influenced advocates of
the machine. Most of them were as honest
as he, at any rate. It must be said, how-
ever, that if Holman’s objection was against
a totalisator system such as he became con-
verted to immediately on throwing away his
Labor rags, there was reason behind him.
But when people spoke of introducing the
totalisator they usually meant a totalisator
worked in the interests of the public. The
thing he accepted is nothing like that; and
it is just about up to him to offer some
explanation of the “dubious reasons” that
impelled him to father the Act authorising
the totalisator which is now on the statute
book. When the Bill came forward this
paper protested against its rotten character,
and further inspection confirms this view.
The State is not getting the totalisator The
Bulletin argued for, or that any sane per-
son asked to be supplied with. As the thing
stands now the country would be infinitely
better without it. The net result is going
to be much the same as if, when a Conser-
vatorium of Music was asked for, the Min-
istry had liberated 7642 nimble-fingered
organ-grinders and an equal number of
mischievous monkeys on the public high-
ways.
Maoriland and the War Bill.
Unless the Government manages to have
everyone gaoled in the meantime, there is
going to be a loud noise in Maoriland about
the local profit-monger. By the twin process
of borrowing from J. Bull and getting high
prices for exports, the landowners and their
commercial camp-followers have since the
war broke out been coining money. The rest
of the community has been struggling with
the cost of living, paying increased taxation
and drifting further and further into debt.
And now it is beginning to turn sour. On
the one side there is much distracted talk
about “the duty of the population,” the cost
of producing wheat and mutton and butter,
the wisdom of supplying J. Bull with food
at bargain prices, “placing all our resources
at the disposal of the Imperial authorities,”
and so on. On the other side, there is fierce
protestation that the farmers and others are
entitled to their revenues in the same way
as other traders. In the end, there is great
uncertainty as to what all the talk is about,
and another illustration is given of the in-
capacity of the average Maorilander to get
down to the bedrock of his problems.' Obvi-
ously it will not make the local wage-earner’s
income any more useful to him if the Gov-
ernment collars all the meat on the pre-
mises and sells to J. Bull at half the price
paid by the British consumer for the rest of
his chops and steaks. The result of any
such transaction, extended over the whole-
field of exports, would be (1) to surrender a
large slice of the national earnings; (2) to
make the country less able to meet the cost
of war; and f 3) to increase the burden of
existing taxation. The absurdity of asking
the Maoriland producer to take less for his
supplies than is given for similar commo-
dities in the U.S.A. and the Argentine and
Australia is surely clear. That would mean
penalising Cocky for the mere sake of doing
it. The system that is being followed now
penalises everyone else but Cocky for the
mere sake of letting him fill his pockets at
the expense of the less fortunate part of the
community. The way out is not to waste
time blithering about the prices paid at the
other end of the world for wool and other
things, but to get busv on the problem of
paying for the war. It is outrageous that
Maoriland, in receipt of an immense revenue
from exports, should be borrowing from the
British Government and converting a large
part of her income by local borrowing into
permanent debt. It means shifting on the
debt to the next generation, which can be
confidently relied upon to have quite enough
to do without paying for the wars and eva-
sions and mouthing loyalties of the men of
to-day. It is idiotic to grumble because
the country’s earnings are high. What
can be avoided is the economic mis-
use of these earnings. A land-tax with-
out exemption to cover the cost of war,
make decent provision for the men who
are being conscripted for military service
and for the maimed and bereaved afterwards,
would sweep up most of these war profits,'
hurt no one, keep the country out of a bog,
and bring relief to the crowd of wage-
earners now nursing the baby.

Eureka.
“Playbird” for the defence:—
“Die Mihi,” in his remarks concerning the Eureka
rebellion, claims that he is a perfervid Australian
and knows his country’s history. Well, it must hav,e-
been a squint-eyed Tory history he has been reading
this time. Perhaps Gyles Turner’s petty and false
account of the Rebellion.
“Gold by the law of Britain was a royal metal.
The gold did not belong to the diggers. It was the
property of the State then as now.” What sort of
argument is this? Did the State ever make this
claim? Even in the bitter political fight of ’55, the
aftermath of the Eureka rebellion, the autocratic
Hotham and the squattocracy of a nominee Parlia-
ment were not so loose-headed as to make that argu-
ment part of their pleading.
“The State asked £lB per year for a license to
pick up gold in slugs on the surface.... Surely 30s.
per month was not too much to pay for the privilege
of gathering it in chunks and hunks.” Firstly, in
’54 the license fee was much more than 30s. per
month; but let that pass. Take the “Commonwealth
Official Year Book” down, and these facts appear.
In 1854 the gross value of the gold won from the
earth in Victoria was £8,873,932. A very low
estimate of the diggers in Victoria in ’54 is 100,000.
This gives average annual earnings of £BB 14s. lOd.
That’s what “Mnu” calls picking up chunks of gold !
No doubt many diggers made fortunes; bu,t by far
the. greater number did not make tucker and were
glad to get back to their trades, thus laying the
foundation of the bright, busy, industrial boom in
Victoria in the ’6o’s.
But what is meant by “the State” ? The diggers
and the many tradesmen they supported formed 75
per cent, of the population and provided probably
the same proportion of the revenue. Yet aot one
had a vote. Not one man represented them in the
Parliament: The Legislature was a close preserve for
the squattoeraey and was ruled by an all-powerful
Governor. Of course England at the time was in
much the same state. But the governing classes of
England had education and whatever refining influ-
ences birth and breeding give, whereas the ruling
class of Victoria was an ignorant one, whose only
claim to superiority was the possession of many sheep
and vast areas of land which had never been paid
for.
True, in the heterogeneous cosmopolitan diggings
were assembled good, bad and indifferent from the
four corners of the earth. But the great bulk had
come from Britain, men with the love of adventure
in their veins, men whose great-sires had followed
Drake and Raleigh, whose sons faced the privations
of the Westralian gold rushes, whose grandsons met
the Turks on Gallipoli. And these men were treated
by the handful of squatters as dirt, as scum, as
refuse. Thousands of them had been Chartists in
’4B (practically every point of the Charter they
fought for is on the law books now, both here and
in Britain), and were these men with right and
might on their side going to submit to the domina-
tion of a handful of ignorant sheep-rearers whose
only claim to power was that they had come over
a dozen years earlier and “taken up land”?
‘ Dic’s” final remarks re the Boston Yankees show
the conservative, early-Victorian kink in his mind.
If ever there was a just war in the world it was the
American War of Independence. Every reputable
modern British historian admits the justice of the
American Colonies’ claims..
Here are the real causes of the Eureka rebellion:—
(1) An exorbitant license fee, levied on all resi-
dents of the diggings, no matter whether they were
diggers or not.
(2) The brutal methods adopted by the police in
their so-called digger hunts.
(3) The absolute impossibility of getting justice
from a corrupt magistracy. The shocking abortion
of justice in the Scobie-Bentley-Dewes case is air
example.
(4) The refusal of the Parliament to give the
diggers any representation, i.e., they claimed “No
taxation without representation.”
And the results of it:—
(1) The first attempt to form a Representative
Parliament in any of the British colonies.
(2) The taking away of all the autocratic powers
of the Governor.
(3) The purification of the magistracy.
(4) The fair and equitable adjustment of taxation.
The Eureka Rebellion was only a small affair, but
its effects were momentous. From it sprang the
democracy of Australia. When our country swings
fairly away on its great path of nationhood and
years allow the past to be viewed in true perspective,
Eureka s 3rd of December will become one of our
great days of the year, and the slopes outside
Ballarat will be recognised as holy ground where
brave men died that Australia might be free. And
more—where by their death they did actually make
Australia free.

The Habit of Waiting for the Flood.
The disaster in 'Queensland which has
resulted in so serious a loss of life has made
a whole continent hold up hands of shocked
amazement that intelligent citizens should
be content to place their homes where they
are in danger of destruction the moment
tlie weather conditions are a little beyond
the normal. There had been warning after
warning of the real nature of the peril, and
most of the people of the doomed township
quite frankly admitted that something ought
to be done about it; but nothing was done
about it, and the flood came along at last
to verify all prophecies and hand out the
due punishment to those who staked their
lives against the chance of the weather keep-
ing dry. The result may be an effort of
some sort. to get other townships and even
bigger cities into defensive shape against
their own special dangers; but it is not
likely that the warning will be taken in
dead seriousness in as far as it applies
to things of larger and more far-reaching
significance. Thus it is quite possible that
Sydney may wake up to the fact that usable
fire-escapes are seldom attached to the build-
ings where they are likely to be most needed
and that fire is quite as sudden as water
when it is out to ravage; but the far more
startling fact that a huge portion of the
weatlh of Eastern Australia is crowded upon
a small peninsula between Woolloomooloo
Bay and Darling Harbor, and within range
of the guns of an enemy well out at sea, is
not likely to force itself on the intelligence
of the person who brays of the value per
inch of George-street frontages. Congestion
such as . that which Sydney continues to
endure is about as dangerous as living
under a cracked reservoir dam at the end
of a dry spell. It invites calamity, and it
is likely to keep on inviting it until the
ferocious animal actually arrives. The same
is true of the importing policy which under
all the patriotic talk still has a vigorous
life in the souls of those who intend to use
it for all it is worth once the war stops,
b or .a nation to live under the great cracked
retaining-wall of an importing policy is
obviously to take risks of a terrifying sort;
and some day Australia’s best hopes may
be entirely swirled away in the flood of
cheap things made by the cheap foreigner.
But Clermont can’t teach lessons like these.
The national imagination works in narrow
circles, and a separate calamity is wanted
to impress each point in the' rhetoric of
logic against the sophistry of Don’t-Care-a-
Damn.

The Miraculous Element in British
Victories.
British victories by land and sea have
generally had something of the miraculous
about them. Look at the Napoleonic wars.
Nothing doing for years until St. Vincent
won a victory at sea. Then Providence
took a hand, and sent Nelson to smash the
enemy through neglecting to do what he was
told! The Nile fight, which left Napoleon’s
army stranded in the East, was in the same
category. You can easily prove it now on
the map that the British fleet couldn’t have
licked the French in the position they had
taken up. Later, in the Spanish peninsula,
John Moore got an idea. That, looked a
miracle, too; for there hadn’t been an idea
in the British army since Marlborough left
it; and here was a General actually thinking
a thought that burst up the whole French
strategy for months! Anybody who lias read
knows that Waterloo was a miracle. The
thing simply couldn’t be done. Napoleon’s
strategy was as good as ever: the big Prus-
sian army was retreating; and the stand at
Waterloo was a joke. Providence now has
its chance again. There has been any
amount of fighting; and the bulk of neutral
opinion suggests that, while there may be
plenty more of an even worse sort, nobody
can win straight out. About here the
miracle ought to happen which ends in the
band playing “Australia Will Be There” to
an astonished Berlin. And then the solid
arrogance of the Briton will be able to assert
itself to the annoyance of all foreigners for
another century. Providence seems to like
that sort of thing if you carry it off with an
air of religious solemnity

An important part of the excuse advanced
by the Holman Government for the
Norton Griffiths deal was that the shin-
dykit had a vast quantity of available cash
which would obviate the necessity for trans-
actions with an ordinary Cohen. The
terms of the agreement provided for
£4,000,000 being spent in the first two years.
That period expires in November next. Up
to date about £3,y00,000 has been spent;
but it is now announced that as “a con-
cession to the Government” Norton
Griffiths will not spend £2,500,000 during
the next 11 months. That leaves something
to be explained. Part of the weird arrange-
ment was that a battalion of Norton
Griffiths officials should ue placed on the
State pay-roll, and they are there now,
reaching down a good healthy allowance per
month. Thus, although the shindykit’s
millions are not to be spent, the State’s
wages bill has been pushed up by thousands
per annum. What are these superfluous
persons doing? What are they wanted for?

The Earl Granville who has been appointed
British Diplomatic Agent to the Venizelos
Provisional Government at Salonika —a
ticklish job—is grandson of the Granvllle
who was longtime Gladstone’s Minister for
Foreign Affairs. A talent for tact is said
to run in the Granville family. The
present Earl is well up in foreign languages,
anyhow, and at 44 is still young enough to
be alertly suspicious. In Greece these times
a diplomat must be always looking for
treachery, and able to recognise it at first
glance. There is something pathetic in the
recollection of the late Kitchener’s “mis-
sion” to Greece 20 months ago. Con-
stantine the King seems to have hood-
winked him by merely professing to be frank
and open. “We talked together as soldiers
and soon came to a complete understanding,”
said the Kaiser’s slippery brother-in-law. A
very one-sided understanding it was. Con-
stantine got to know all about K. of K.
and his plans, whilst the Englishman got
nothing but lies from the Dago. It may
be supposed that Earl Granville is at least
a professional unbeliever with a misleading
smile.

This is taken from a New Year sermon by
an Australian bishop :
We who cannot fight shoulder to shoulder with
the men in the trenches should endeavor to assist
them by coming into touch with them through our
supplication. We should pray for justice, truth,
mercy, honor, peace 'and love amongst mankind.
Episcopal and other exhortation to pray for
peace is having a confusing effect on the
minds of sinners and the righteous alike. In
the allied and enemy countries the Church
is busy encouraging soldiers to fight, blessing-
banners and proclaiming that Jehovah is a
Man of War. And while the young and
the strong are urged from the pulpit ito bog
in with a bayonet and achieve victory by
slaughter, the old and the very young who
remain at home are told that they must
pray for peace. This mixture of bellicosity
and humility seems to imply the existence
of clerical doubt as to whether Providence
can be most impressed by vehemence of bash
or volume of supplication. Certainly an in-
stitution which urges people both to pray for
peace and to fight suggests that it is more
firmly persuaded about the expediency of
backing a winner both ways than convinced
that reliance on prayer represents a sound
national programme.
Chief Sec. Donald McLeod, of Victoria,
after publishing his definite intention to
appoint a few policewomen, and receiving
90 applications from parties willing to
clothe themselves in a short skirt and a
little brief authority, has changed his
elderly mind and decided that the time isn’t
ripe, after all. This was his attitude when
he first became Chief Sec. Then he deter-
mined to try a couple. Then he reached
the conclusion that he wouldn’t. Before
long he announced that he would, and his
latest decision is that he won’t. The reason
for this final won’t and for rejecting the 90
applicants is national economy; and it seems
a pity that the Minister wasn’t able to be
economical before wasting a lot of printing,
advertising space and departmental time, as
well as a share of his. own expensive brain-
services. Still, it has not yet been made
clear in exactly what way the' latest decision
is economical. Efficiency and economy go
hand in hand ; and you can’t get either by
putting on a man to do what is essentially
women’s work —it doesn’t matter whether it
is card-indexing, looking after young o-irls
or using a bushel of tact where the he-
policeman provides about 2oz.
“Canuck” : Here is the military history of a
young friend of mine. He volunteered IS
months ago, and was rejected on account of
deficient chest measurement. Twelve months
later he had another try, but was still half
an inch short. He was, however, accepted
for Home Service on the understanding that
he should go to the Front if he put on the
needed half-inch. He was trained in mod' ra-
tion for a few weeks, and then sent to
another camp on police and other duty.
After the Referendum he assisted in dismant-
ling that camp ; and then, having put on more
than the required half-inch of chest, he was
invited, with others on Home Service, to
volunteer for “permanent guard-duty.” They
all declined, and were promptly told to hand
in their kits and go about their business.
There were six half-trained lads'thus dis-
charged that day. Last week my lad made a
new—his third-attempt, and was promptly
accepted for active service. If the military
chiefs want men as badly as they say they do
that lad should have been in France by now.
Another chapter of his story calls loudly for
investigation. When he and the other five
were discharged they were given orders on
the Red Cross depot at the rear of the Mel-
bourne barracks, where each was handed two
pairs pvjamas, two pairs underpants, two
flannels and two pairs socks. These articles
bore the labels of up-country Red Cross
branches; all were misfits, and one sock con-
tained a kindly message from the knitter
wishing the recipient “a charmed life and a
safe return.” I wonder if the kindly woman
who knitted those socks —they came from
Ararat-—dreams that they were given to a
lad on his discharge from Home Service, fit
and well. Have the Red Cross people more
goods than they need for those actually fight-
ing, or is this sort of thing plain—what?
Fruitgrowers in the coastal districts of
N. S. Wales are having a melancholy time
this over-wet year, thanks to the prevalence
of brown rot, which turns stone fruit putrid.
If there are a few diseased peaches in a case,
the whole lot will rot in a few days. In
some of the orchards half the fruit is mil-
dewed and rotting on the trees. Yet 80 per
cent, of the damage could have been avoided
if fruitgrowers had adopted scientific
methods. Spraying at the right time with
lime sulphur gives these fungus diseases a
horrible bump. But because the State does
not make the grower spray, he mostly doesn’t
do it; and the efforts of those who do are
neutralised by the neglect of the others. An-
other folly is that tons of fruit have come
into the Sydney market in cases reeking
with contagion; and nothing being done to
cleanse or sterilise these boxes go back to
the orchard, or into some clean district,
carrying the disease spores with them.
Frequently, also, decayed fruit is picked out
of one grower’s case and thrown into an-
other’s, and thus contamination is spread.
It is a ruinous business and badly needs
cleaning if there is ever to be a hope of the
public getting cheap fruit, and the orchard!st
a decent living.

He was an Australian, and one day while
reading about what happened at the Front he
found a notion sprouting under his hat. By
sundown he figured it out that if this sprout
were planted in France many lives should be
saved. So he wrote to the British War
Office and asked what it thought of his
notion. The reply was prompt—a printed
form stating that the thing was useless.
Further information convinced the man
that the War Ofliee was —well, wrong.
Therefore, after a while, he wrote to
the Inventions Board, then just estab-
lished. Back came a letter intimating that
an investigation would be made. Later on
a lot later on—there was more corres-
pondence, in which it was explained that the
idea had been examined and the verdict of
the Board was that it could not be adopted.
Then, later still, the Australian read in
English papers that trials were being made
at the Front of something which was the
same as the affair he had schemed out; and
when he subsequently found that it was
being extensively employed he commenced to
wear a worried look. Incidentally it has
been explained that many lives have been
saved by the idea. Which seems to imply
that many were lost by regarding it as a
joke in the first place. But nobody at Head-
quarters is going to be hanged with his own
red tape for the murders.

“Southern Cross” : The question of “cruci-
fixion” (Field Punishment No. 1) was
brought up at the recent Inter-State Labor
Conference in Melbourne by T. D. Mutch, a
N. S. Wales delegate. On his advice it was
decided to ask “Jupp” Gardiner, leader of
the Labor party in the Senate, to get a select
committee appointed to inquire into the
iniquity. Officers who have sat on courts-
martial say that the regulations allow up to
JO days F.P. No. 1, and that the full extent
of. the penalty is freely inflicted in the
British armies. Australians rarely get more
than 28 days; in fact, in most cases of
breach of discipline for which F P No 1 is
handed out to English troops, Australian
officers prefer to inflict C.B.—“Confined to
Barracks though English officers shake
their heads at the “leniency.” In the Com-
mons some weeks ago there was brought to
fight a case in which a soldier got 90 days
1.1. No. 1 for driving a waggon too fast in
Prance, thereby endangering the lives of
children on the road. In addition to physi-<
cal punishment his pay was stopped for' 90
days and Ins wife in England, who had
children to support, lost her allotment for
that period. Forster, of the War Office
admitted the facts, but said the Government
could not interfere with the sentence of the
court-martial. But the 0,000,000 voters who
are in khaki will do a whole lot of “inter-
fering” when the war is over.

The Prospect before General Outlook.
HoW much longer General Muddle is going
to rule the roost in Greece has not yet been
explained. He has had a fairly long innings,
anyhow, and if there is anything except
ehabS to show for it ail it has been kept
a profound secret There is just a dim pos-
sibility that Muddle is a Strategist df great
guile, who, when he has manoeuvred the
Situation into a state that makes it opaque
to everybody else, is going to produce a
handful of trumps, throw them down, and
emerge a winner. Pending consummation of
ali this, the events iil that quarter are as
suggestive bf an early victory for the Allies
aS the continued activities of the submarine
are. Sefvift, Montenegro and Roumania
have been over-run, and the Greek appears
to be so much impressed by the Allied army
at Salonika that if he wants to do anything
at all it is to get ill On the Side of the
German. There has been a riot of
writing, conferences and explanations, and
the net result is a collection of confusion
that looks like the wildest romance. The
German’s greatest ambitions are in the
direction of Asia via ther*®alkans. Defeat
on his own frontiers would mean destruc-
tion of those ambitions. But he has yet to
be defeated there, and the size of the job
can be to some extent measured by the
battle of the Somme and the inescapable
fact that during the past few months the
German grip in the Balkans has been
tightened. Neither side is in a frame of
mind to knock off, and it will do Australia
no harm to remember that the fight for this
country’s freedom from a menace more real
than anything it ever dreamed of is a long
way from being over. The yarns about Ger-
man food shortage mean nothing. It is
the armies in the trenches that count —and
judged by them Germany is still strong,
defiant and resourceful. That is the sort
of eneijiy we are facing. What are we going
to do about it? Say “I beg your pardon—
I misjudged you,” and own ourselves licked,
or go into this business as if our life and
happiness depended upon the result, as
indeed they both may?
.
Joe Cook knows that the Hughes Govern-
ment has no right to be where it is—he
knows it is a barnacle and a usurper. Let
him speak for himself —*
Our attitude is shaped by the great overwhelm*
ing issues which are being determined in other parts
of the world, and by the need which exists here to
do everything we can to assist the Motherland to see
this war through to a triumphant conclusion. How
else would it be possible for a great party, such as
ours is, to swing behind a party which is a much
smaller party ? How else is it possible that we
could be to-day keeping in power, office and respon-
sibility a number of men p:ho, on every democratic
principle, have no claim whatever to be in their pre-
sent positions ? On no party or political considera-
tion could they be permitted to stay there for one
minute. .. .Neither on account of the war nor on
account of the principles of democratic freedom and
democratic responsibility can it be defended in its
present aspect.
No stronger condemnation of the Liberal
conspiracy to keep Hughes on the back of
Australia like an Old Man of the Sea could
be framed. If Cook and bis friends cannot
run a Ministry—if they are afraid of being
stabbed by Hughes —there is nothing to pre-
vent them assisting Tudor to win the war.
That at least would be understandable. For
it would be the rankest misrepresentation to
allege that Hughes is capable of stirring
Australia to any greater effort in the war
than Tudor, tin the contrary, it is abso-
lutely certain tnat, so long as Hughes re-
mains in power, large rummers of men will
stand aloof from every war-movement. They
will do nothing for which Hughes could
afterwards take credit. If “democratic free-
dom” and “responsibility” are outraged by
the existing arrangement, the last words
have therefore been spoken, and Cook will
never be able to condone his participation in
this offence by hifalutin’ about winning the
war.

Died in Adelaide, in the nineties, Thomas
Caterer, the last of a family which began
to teach the S. Australian youth as far back
as ’54. Like brother Fred, who died some
years ago, he was no scholar to speak of,
but they both passed on a good, practical
commercial education, and their schools had
a marked influence on Adelaide. A son,
Ainsley, is acting headmaster of St. Peter’s.
He can’t be appointed permanently because
he isn’t an Anglican parson.

Auditorium “Where Are My Children?”
“The Woman,” staged at Melbourne
Royal last Saturday, is a hotch-potch of
previous Yankee telephone dramas, sour in
character and mechanical in movement. The
colorless young son of a wealthy political
grafter wants to marry the daughter of a
respectable politician who was ruined by
the grafter and his party. Practically the
same trio occur in “The Lion and the
Mouse.” In this case the grafters are up
against an “insurgent” politician who is
blocking one of their railway jobs in the
House of Reps., and whose respectability
threatens to resist all attempts at blackmail.
But before he has brought their dirty Bill
to grief their boss—the Hon. Jim Blake —
discovers that the pattern of morality has
been guilty in the past of enjoying one
blissful Saturday to Monday at the seaside
with a girl who ought to have been his wife
but wasn’t. Jim Blake’s secret inquiry
agents learn everything about Matthew
Standish’s week-end indiscretion except the
identity of the mysterious Woman. So
James adopts simple theatrical devices to get
on the track of this missing Woman by means
of telephone clues. And all the time she
“THE HOUSE THAT JACK BUILT,”
AT HER MAJESTY'S, MELBOURNE.
Harry Roxhury as
Sammy.
Arthur Stigant as
Dame Barleycorn.
was his own dear daughter and the adored
wife of another legislative grafter. The
telephone girl knows this. The audience
knows it. Yet the grafters to a man are
ignorant of the fact that Jim Blake’s one
ewe lamb had formerly been engaged to Mat-
thew Standish and had lived with him as his
wife for two days, after which she refused
to marry the gentleman on the ground that
she didn’t love him any more. The differ-
ence between the Woman who went astray
with Matthew and the girl who was tricked
into indiscretion in “On Trial” is that the
one didn’t want to marry the bachelor,
whereas the other couldn’t marry a seducer
who was married already. Both girls get
into a terribly awkward fix as the result of
having kept their similar secret from hubby.
Also in this drama there is a severe cross-
examination of the telephone girl, which
begins when the curtain is falling on one act
and is continued before the curtain rises on
the next act. It is a repetition of what hap-
pened in “On Trial.” One way and another
the mechanism of “The Woman” is always
appealing to this scribe’s recollections of
other dramas which were less unconvincing.
Every “strong” theatrical situation in it
rings untrue. It is all ’phone and falseness.

the plot thickens, owing partly, perhaps, to

The Royal Comics have safely hitched
themselves to “The Cinema Star” at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney). It’s n bright show,
the brighter for Minnie Love’s work.
That money-spinner for the Bailey-Grant-
Duggan co., “On Our Selection,” is busy
again at the Royal (Sydney). It’s the
draw of the co.’s season as usual.
Bert Bailey has made arrangements to
change over from Sydney Royal to the Cri-
terion for Sumner Locke’s comedy, “Mum
Dawson, Boss.” It is a wise move, as the
compact little theatre is better suited to the
closely-played type of farce. The energetic
little author, besides striking a novel vein of
Australian comedy, has built several charac-
ters that defy stage tradition, and have their
human moments. And the absence of slap-
stick caricature should be a welcome feature.
Sydney Criterion audiences are throwing
stones at “The House of Glass.” Some of
the bricks must have hit the box-office, for
the last nights are already announced.
When will the Firm discover that it can’t
keep on feeding American crook plays to
Australian audiences?
“Peg” is still pegging away at Sydney
Palace, though the end of the thunderstorm
season is announced. It will happen next
week. Incidentally the Palace is to be
internally recqnstrueted in part. The Taits,
who have a long lease of the theatre, will
stage “Very Good Eddie” there on Feb. 10.
The brightest turn that has come to the
Tivoli (Sydney) for a long time arrived this
week in custody of the Togas, Japanese. If
anybody has seen cleverer juggling and more
artistic balancing The Bulletin would like
the address of the performer. Fine pro-
gramme this week —greatly helped, of course,
by Sydney James and Madeline Rossiter.
The White City (Sydney), under the
inspiration and management of T. H. Eslick,
aided and abetted by the heat which is so
helpful to open-air entertainment these
nights, is doing very well, thank you. This,
season the big show has been made more
attractive and it’s more convenient. To-
night there is to be a festival, marked
“special” on the calendar.
Since the recent success of the sextette at
the Garden Theatre (Sydney) the Dandies
have been making a feature of part-singing.
Leaving aside the fine blending of the voices
the team has apparently dropped on a
secret deposit of good numbers. Daylight
saving has driven the management to fix the
opening hour at 8.30.
