At Melbourne Princess’s there is no more
“Richard III.” for the present. The man-
agement must have come to the conclusion
that bad King Dick was hot stuff for warm
weather, so on Saturday there was a revival
of “As You Like It,” and the advt. laid
stress on the coolness of the theatre. Allan
Wilkie’s production of this pretty Shak-
spearean tissue of absurdities and popular
quotations is the same artistic success as
before. The performance has been bettered
by some alterations in the cast since the
first time the same Rosalind and Orlando
met in the same “desert inaccessible” where
there are lions on the loose and sheepruns
and farmhouses for sale. The incredible
Forest of Arden will spread its melancholy
boughs for six nights only. On Saturday
comes Oliver Goldsmith’s “She Stoops to
Conquer,” not staged in Melbourne for 25
years or more.
The panto, known as “The House That
Jack Built” flourishes eight times a week at
Melbourne Majesty’s. Presently the orches-
tra will subdue itself sufficiently to let the
gentle voices of the lady principals give
their song words a chance. Meanwhile the
ballet-dancing remains sweetly audible above
the flowing accompaniments, every word in
the poetry of motion being feelingly recited
by Maggie Dickinson’s limbs. Also every-
thing in the garden (including standard
roses) is lovely at Tulip Time, and Sammy
continues to supply the Dame with droll
reflections when she gazes into the missing
looking-glass.
“The Accidental Honeymoon,” tried by
the Kate Ilowarde Co. at Melbourne King’s
on Saturday, is a farce of ancient pattern
that hides the light of its authorship under
a merciful anonymity. The unknown author
takes the usual pair of eloping couples, and
having dumped them in a waybaek tavern,
leaves them to revel in a series of comedy
situations that were beginning to look de-
cayed in the era of our grandfathers. But
the trouble with the playwright is that,
when he had run the whole gamut of plagia-
rism, making his quartette father and son
and mother and daughter, and also involving
them in fraudulent matrimony to increase
the complications and give spice to their
adventure, he leads them into such a
dramatic impasse that he can only rescue
them with any appearance of decorum by
the desperate expedient of bringing down the
curtain and letting the characters escape in
the confusion. Comedy of that sort, which
mostly substitutes horseplay for humor and
brightens the dialogue with flying missiles,
is very easy thinking, but hardly deserves to
be spoken aloud. However, a fair house
gave the piece a respectful hearing on Satur-
day, and received the cast with some
applause. Lesley Adrienne takes the part of
Millie Match, a small lover of romance, who,
having tasted the imitation in boarding-school
novels, almost bolts the reality in the com-
pany of Cuthbert Claxton. Millie’s child-
ish sentimentality is doomed to extinction by
Miss Adrienne, who merely appears as an
animated doll made vocal by a soprano with
a cold in it. Austin Milroy, as Cuthbert, her
excessively effusive admirer, almost bursts in
his endeavor to grow lyrical in a large, high
collar. Olive Sinclair does better as Millie’s
mamma, but she is almost youthful enough to
reverse the relationship. Enid Bowman, as
the tavern mistress, has enough acid to keep
her sour for the evening. W. Maloney and
Lester Carey figure as a coon car-conductor
and a pathetically inept policeman, and Elton
Black is a rough-and-ready waiter whose feet
were made for disaster and whose paws were
shaped for tips. Practically every member
of the co. talks Amurkan fluently, exclaims
“Gee!” says “foirst” for “first,” and forges
and utters similar perversions in a manner
which would make Hale Hamilton swear
they were Broadway born. Next Saturday
Allen Doone reappears with “Happy-go-
lucky O’Shea.”
There is little else than Follies and Fred
Bluett at Melbourne Tivoli, and the Follies,
at least, are quite as good as they used to
be. The inclusion of Daisy and Sydney
Yates has given the medley a touch of refine-
ment. Moreover, a new field of industry has
been opened to some of the moderately
musical* choristers. Four couples of them
venture into the d.c. and the girls dangle
their legs over the parapet whilst their
respective young men hold them in an
“THE WOMAN.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Five scoundrel politicians of the modern ’Murkan
kind ;
A ’phone girl who is cute beyond her years;
A wife whose lawyer-husband seems ridiculously
blind
To the patent cause and reason of her tears;
A chain of situations incidental to the fact
That the rotters want to learn a Woman’s name,
And a mushy ’Murkan ending to the third and final
act
Where the Woman makes confession of her Shame.
A stink of foul corruption in the story of the play,
An odor that is wearisomely’ stale;
A son who loves the ’phone girl in a callow ’Murkan
way;
A doting Dad who glories in blackmail;
A secret which the audience can fathom from the
first
Re a giddy week-end happening of yore;
A bubble complication which, of course, is bound to
burst,
And —a play composed of dramas gone before.

"THE PASSING SHOW,” AT SYDNEY
TIVOLI.
Graystone, James , and Connie Milne in an
opera burlesque.
The Dandies at St. Kilda (Vic.) broke
out in a pimple of “Faust” last week, and
also combined their voices in a mysterious
composition called “A Syncopated Boogie.”
In the second part Brock’s troupe spent an
hour at “The Ship Inn” in a series of mostly-
nautical disguises. The company seemed a
little tired, but became a glee party when
Joe Brennan was about; for Brennan’s
natural gaiety as an ac-tor is immensely
infectious.
The film-spectre of Tyrone Power will
cease to ask for his children at Melbourne
Auditorium after Friday night. On Satur-
day E. ,T. Carroll promises the first screen-
ing of “Ramona,” which portrays in 10 sec-
tions and 1000 scenes Helen Hunt Jackson’s
story of early California. The film will be
shown twice daily and will be “cued” to a
specially-written score performed by a sym-
phony orchestra.
Hoyt’s have a stoushing parson on view
at Melbourne Olympia this week. The
pugnacious pilot is dealing out opiates to a
group of festive cowboys in “On the Night
Stage.” A more peaceful subject running at
both of Hoyt’s theatres is “The Light of
Happiness,” from which Viola Dana wfisc-
fully peers as the bedraggled daughter of a
boozy parent. The third feature introduces
the new “find,” June Caprice, who turns out
to be a clever imitation of Mary Pickford
with some of the smile rubbed off.
* * *
Adelaide, which does not originate much
in the dramatic way, has dashed in with a
candid syphilis drama—on the film. It is a
practical story, showing the jolly station-
hand, the bad company he gets into in town,
and the usual consequences-, ending in sui-
cide after his sweetheart has married his
brother. As a warning it beats all ser-
mons, and the Chief Secretary allows it on
condition that children are not admitted.
The play was worked out at Buckland Park
and in Adelaide by Cyril Mackay. Fitzher-
bert, Marie Dalton, Mabel Dyson (Mrs.
Coppin) and other clever people, and the
films are drawing crowds to Wondergraph.
Matthews was author and producer.
#• * * *
The large section of Adelaide that stayed
away from “While the Billy Boils,” at the
King’s, missed a good thing. Its gentle
Robertsonian humors blend well with the
up-country atmosphere, and the melodrama
dragged in is the only weakness. Kay
Souper and lan Maclaren, Tilton and Can-
nam, are all good, but Walter Cornock stood
out from the lot. At the Tivoli. Anderson
has had the happy idea of reviving the old
Warner-Pettitt success, “Hands Across the
Sea,” and the Majestic has a good well-
balanced programme (twice a day) with the
Eurasians and the juggling Littlejohns as
its top notes.
Novelist H. G. Yv'ells, in an article telling
the U.S.A. about a visit to some of France's
big military men. breaks into parentheses:—
I had but recently had an encounter with an im-
ported colonial statesman who was being advertised,
like a soap, as the coming saviour of England....!
met something surrounded by touts, that was dully
trying to “come it” over me.... One jcould as soon
have talked with one of the statesmen at Madame
Tussaud’s.- The effect of these French commanders
upon me was quite different from my encounter with
that belated adventurer.
Now, who was this that Wells met?

THE PICTURE-CENSOR.
[For The Bulletin.]
Maoriland’s recently-appointed picture-censor in
the first seven weeks of his dreadful job examined
640,000 feet of film.
Poor wretch! His tortures ne’er shall cease —■
Oh, fate both dire and grim !
For he must view each, fresh “release”
Till death releases him!
VICTORIA. E. T.
“R.Y.S.”: It’s a rum life that of the
mummer. I met one of the profession the
other day—one • who has done notable work
on the Australian stage in various kinds of
parts, his versatility and all-round ability
being known to all playgoers —and he aston-
ished me by saying that he had been out of
work for a twelvemonth. And yet the mana-
gers, saying they are hard up for talent, are
bringing inferior artists from America by
every steamer!
“Bob D.”: Daylight Saving is giving
open-air picture-shows a rough passage, in
Victoria especially. During December, Jan-
uary and February it only starts to get
dark at about 8.30, and for that reason the
al-fresco picture shows at St. Kilda do not
begin until half an hour after their roofed
rivals. Darkness doesn’t really descend
until 9.30 now, so the proprietors are taced
with the alternative of closing up and pray-
ing for an early winter, or showing only
half a programme and chopping their prices
in two. Meanwhile they do their best to
darken the earth from 8.30 to 9.30 by
descriptive profanity.
Shaw’s latest. A new piece of his, “Get-
ting Married,” was about to be produced in
N’York. He wired across to the producer,
W. Faversham, to postpone it because he
“did not wish to divide attention with the
American political situation.” Faversham
replied nastily that there was not the slight-
est fear of a collision.
Comment of the N’York Morning Herald
on the Writers’ Guild, newly formed in
Melbourne: —-
The Writers of Australia have organised and will
seek the passage of a law to inhibit the importation
of American and English books, in competition with
native talent. Can you blame thorn? What need
has a country rich in art, in literature and in
science for the literary output of other countries.
By the way, who are the Australian writers known
beyond the borders of that country ? Do you recall
any of them, or one of them? Neither do I. Per-
haps they are the talented sons of the ticket-of-leave
men who settled Australia.
Perhaps they are. And the American
dramatic writer seems to know a good bit
about the police, too.

Beaumont Smith is the latest movie pro-
ducer. The young Australian dramatist
has hidden himself away in the foothills of

“Up Stage” : Now that tire stage-technique
cyclone has blown over, a few facts may
help “Private Boxer’s” claim that stage
craft is somewhat necessary to “balance”
the local drama into a playable groove. An
authority like Charles Froliman declared
that not 1. per cent, of accepted MSS. were
produced as written. Charles Klein had
many failures until he became a stock actor ;
then having acquired some knowledge of
the craft he wrote “The Lion and the
Mouse,” “Third Degree,” “Gamblers,” etc.
Bayard Yeiller, author of “Within the Law,”
was a press agent, married to an actress.
Eugene Walter, “Paid in Full,” was a press-
man ; his wife a well-known star. Ganthony
hawked “A Message from Mars” around
for two years until Charles Ilawtrey saw
the idea and got him to rewrite it. Pinero
was an actor before he settled down to play-
writing. Hartley Manners, “Peg o’ My
Heart,” dropped the grease-paint years ago,
and so far hasn’t known failure. Old
stagers affirm the success of “Silver King”
was due to Barrett, who straightened out a
tangled script and stage-techniqued it into
the money-maker. Plays that count from
the box-office point of view are nearly all re-
plete with stage craft.
“E.T.” : Letty Lind, the ex-Gaiety dancer,
well-remembered in these parts as a member
of the burlesque troupe headed by Nellie
Farren and Fred Leslie, has taken to play-
writing. In collaboration with Major Row-
land Gibson, she is responsible for a piece
which the all-pervasive Grossmith and Lauril-
lard have acquired. It is a Chinese sub-
ject ; title, “The Dragon and the Throne.”
George Grossmith, by the way, is Letty’s
brother-in-law; he married sister Adelaide
Aster, who also wak at one time proud to
be known as a Gaiety girl.
A recent revival of “Silver King” at Mel-
bourne Royal was accountable for an
astounding piece of misinformation in the
Argus :
There are four melodramas amongst the huge
avalanche that has flooded the stage during the last
quarter of a century which are always sure of an
audience by their legitimate appeal to our loyal and
common humanity, and one of them is “The Silver
King,” which was produced on Saturday night to a
full house at the Theatre Royal. The other three
pieces are “Our Boys,” “Struck Oil” and “East
Lynne.”
None of those plays was avalanched on the
flooded stage in the last quarter of a cen-
tury. “East Lynne” in melodrama is about
sf) years old, “Silver King” is 35, and
“Struck Oil” and the almost-forgotten “Our
Boys” are a little over 40. Moreover, the
charge of “melodrama” levelled at the last-
named play is a reckless accusation. H.
J. Byron’s “Our Boys” was a farcical senti-
mental comedy, in which the plot was sub-
servient to the dialogue, and the central
character was a retired butterman whose
errors of speech became the popular jokes of
the period.
“Midchannel”: “Ho” (B. 28/12/T6) is
anxious to know what sort of mole I am.
I regret I can’t supply the information.
When I said (B. 7/12/T6) that “we haven’t
had a single play whose scenes have been
laid in Sydney or Melbourne,” I meant the
genuine artistic play, not the distorted
attempts and caricatures that —with _an ex-
ception or two —make up the long list fur-
nished by him. I include “The Waybaeks,”
“On Our Selection.” etc., in the charge. The
fact that any of them have made money for
their authors is immaterial.
“0.K.” : One of the most interesting pro-
ductions of the Incorporated Stage Society
(London) last year was “The Toy Cart.” a
free adaptation of an Indian work. Only
two members of the co. were colored persons.
Two others —Arthur Wontner and Rose
Edouin—were well known to Australia, and
they played principal parts in the sombre
proceedings. Rose Edouin (Mrs. G. B. W.
Lewis) is still a recognised leader of the
“old legitimate” school in Britain, and gets
great notices for her occasional “creations”
of strong parts where age and artistry are
equally essential to realism.
A yarn is going the rounds about a leading
lady who refused to attend rehearsal, on the
ground that she had played the part so often.
It recalls the similar trouble of one of the big
managers in New York. “She won’t come
to rehearsal, eh? Umph ! She’s very pretty
and. of course But wait till the
wrinkles come!”
“Jean S.”: According to an article in
Melbourne Argus, the J.C.W. wardrobe

“8.D.”: An enterprising Australian lias
cornered about 30 thousand ibis on one of
the Vic. rivers on which his selection fronts.
The birds are breeding on a string of islands
in the centre of the stream, and when he
spotted them it dawned on him that a mov-
ing picture might be interesting. He went
to Melbourne and demanded £lOO for the film
rights, but couldn’t get even a fiver when
he came down to that. The picture firm
declared that it would send one of its own
men to get a negative for nothing. He
disappeared wrathfully on the first train
home, and the camera man who followed
three days later discovered that the cocky
had secured an option over every floatable
craft for 10 miles on either side of the
birds. Beyond the 10-mile limit the stream
was too shallow for a boat to pass. The
movie man came back disappointed, and the
cocky made a little pile by taking Christmas
visitors out to the island to see the flock.
“Nulla Nulla”: “Floater” (B. 4/1/T7),

(7/12/TO), failed to mention Enid Bennett,

and came here at a very early age. The

why not count House Peters, who also came

"Stargazer”: The cinematograph having
made plain spectacle on the stage look kke
three half-pence, the theatre seeks to appeal
to the human eye more and more through
color. Presently some enterprising scientist
will perfect color stuff for the screen, and
then the theatre may have recourse to the
final expedient of appealing to man through
his brains. Two big shows in Melbourne
just now, the pantomime and the Follies, are
color-schemes for tripping up the people’s
nimble shillings, so far as they are anything
at all. The panto, is merely an extravagant
smudge of futuristic embellishment and a
fantastic dress-parade; its humor is crude
and useless, and there is not a single good
voice among the principals. Of the “Follies”
much the same may be said —it is expens-
ively and quaintly arranged, there is a Jap-
anese prodigality of color, and things go with
such verve that you are almost persuaded
the incidents are really great events. But it
is all sawdust, and only differs from the
popular German fare in the fact that it is
exquisitely-tinted sawdust.

MAGNESIA FOR ACID STOMACHS.
TAKE A LITTLE IN WATER AFTER
EVERY MEAL.
Never mind the old-fashioned advice .to
try this or that remedy for your indigestion
or dyspepsia. Be guided by physicians and
specialists who have devoted years of study
to these troubles, and have learned what it
is that causes flushed face after meals, heart-
burn, flatulence, etc. They know that in
nine cases out of ten these symptoms indicate
acid in the stomach and food fermentation,
and so, instead of prescribing drugs or arti-
ficial digestants, they recommend the use of
half a teaspoonful of bisurated magnesia in
a little water after meals. This neutralises
the harmful acid instantly and also prevents
all possibility of food fermenting in the
stomach. This bisurated, magnesia is of
greater value than drugs or medicines, for
it attacks the root cause of the trouble,
whereas drugs merely deaden the nerves and
disguise the symptoms. The genuine bisu-
rated magnesia can easily be obtained from
high-class chemists everywhere, but care
should be taken not to use the citrates,
oxides and sulphates, as well as crude mix-
tures of bismuth and magnesia. Some of
these are very similar in name to bisurated
magnesia, and are apt to confuse the unin-
formed, hence the importance of asking the
chemist very distinctly for bisurated mag-
nesia.— Advt.

SOCIETY
“Vagabond III.”: I came from the Far
Back of the Never Never to Townsville and
after passing three different doctors in Cam-
ooweal, Townsville and Brisbane I was made
a full-blown sodger, and after inoculation
and vaccination I was within two days of
going to have a bit of the sausage nation
when I was chucked out for being over the
age limit. Well, perhaps I am, but my dad
has been fighting just about since the war
commenced and is fighting in France to-day.
Some of the vets in Queensland are great
guessers of age. It took them 90 days to
guess my age, and then the nearest guess
was 11 years out —just because I rubbed 'my
eocoanut with a flourbag. You see, I
helped to dip a sergeant-major in the horse-
trough. He was dirty and wanted a bath.
He would sooner have had it in beer,
but the horse-trough was nearer. Dad never
did any of those naughty things, and that is
the reason he is fighting in the trenches to-
day and I’m too old. Is that a square-dinkum
go for a poor back-blocker? It isn’t what
we call a dinkum go outside. So I’m put-
ting a roll in bluey to tramp straight back to
the Never Never, where I can go and dig
yams and live in peace. If another war
breaks out they need not send me any of
their cards to fill in. They can send for
daddy. He’s the youngest.

“B.B.” : It’s right about the casual Aus-
tralian. He exists. Last week, at Bunbury
(W.A.),a cat fell overboard from a steamer
and forgot to leave its address. This worried
one of the coal-trimmers, and three days
later he decided that the mystery would have
to be cleared up. So he gave notice of his
intention: “I’m going after that cat, and
not all the sharks in the Indian Ocean will
stop me.” He evidently thought that puss
was at the bottom, for that’s where he went
looking for her.
“C.P.”: Apropos the early kick against
“crucifixion” in the Australian army, this
is from my New Guinea diary (3/10/’l4) :
Major made an. ass of himself to-day. He
had occasion to deal with two men caught looting,
and in passing sentence of 28 days’ gaol included a
couple of days’ Field Punishment. We have no guns
here to chain the men to, so the Major gave orders
for the erection of chains in the main street and for
the men to be fastened there to-morrow. The rank
and file thought that to have white men, especially
soldiers, chained up in a black man’s country, with
Germans and natives to jeer at them, was over the
odds, so we kicked and told the color-sergeant that
if the two men were chained up we would hand in
our rifles and demand arrest in a body. The Naval
Reserves are with us. Color-sergeant has gone up to
tell the O.C.
There was no chaining up.

“Melaleuca”: Concerning “crucifixion.”
Even the French Foreign Legion —Legion
Etrangerc —has abolished the equivalent of
this barbaric punishment. General de
Negrier wiped it out as unbecoming a regi-
ment of gentlemen. It is still inflicted, how-
ever, in the unhappy Bats. d’Aff., the com-
panies recruited for service in Algeria from
Frenchmen who have been convicted before
their time came to do their military service.
A man may be sent to the Bats. d’Aff. if he
has received a sentence of more than three
months’ imprisonment, and in France a
youth may be sentenced to three months or
more for insulting a gendarme. The officers
of these disciplinary companies are picked
for their severity. You’ll get details in Yere
Shortt’s novel of the Legion, “Lost Sheep.’
“A.H.R.”: A week-old recruit had cold
tea served out to him one morning lately.
He raised such a row that the sergeant and
the cook rolled up. “Look ’ere!” he said,
“this blanky tea is cold. How do you ex-
pect a chap to drink that stuff? Put your
blanky finger in it and feel.” “Oh, there’s
no need for me to put my finger in it, ole
sport,” said the cook. “I know the blanky
tea is cold. It’s been waiting for you for
two and a half years !”

“Wart”: A case of “Damaged
Goods” within my personal knowledge.
X, aged 15, in the absence of any
instructions from teachers or parents,
was initiated into the mysteries of
sex by a servant girl, and infected with
syphilis. His father, ashamed to take
him to the family doctor, had him
treated by a quack, who worked a
“cure” in six months. At 24 X was
married. At 34 he was the father of
five children. When the youngest
child was 12 months old X began to
get what the doctor described as “nerve
troubles” and was ordered a rest at
the seaside. After a fortnight he
came back to town with a rash, and
the doctor treated it for erysipelas.
Cured of the rash, he found every
scratch on his skin would fester, and
several little ulcers formed in his
mouth and throat. As soon as the
doctor heard of the last trouble he
asked, “Did you ever have syphilis?”
“About 20 years ago,” said X. “Um!
The secondary stage. It may invade
any organ or tissue, or take the form
of almost any disease known. I am
going to give you a severe treatment
for at least a year, and hope to avert
the third stage.” “But, Doctor, how
about my wife and children?” “We
can only hope for the best and watch
them closely for symptoms; mean-
while” Meanwhile X is having a
foretaste of Hell, and he sees in every
scratch or pimple on the wife and
children the first symptoms of the
disease.
Several churches, with a valorous parson
leading, have established good records for
enlistment; but few have done better than
the little Methodist chapel at Dithgow, the
synagogue wherein Joe Cook was a local
.preacher in the days when the Labor party
was coming out of the shell. Its pastor,
R. H. Pettendrigh, put on khaki early enough
to die at Gallipoli as a corporal; and when,
recently, his widow unveiled the roll of honor
in the church, the names of 89 of the mem-
bers of the congregation were upon it. Of
those II have followed their leader into the
shadows.

“Chevron” : The dogs that have their day
at Duntroon are in a class by themselves.
They have no time for civilians, but would
risk their lives for a uniform. Any newcomer
who is in doubt as to the road to a small
store near the officers’ training-school has
his difficulty settled by a four-footed guide
whom no civvy can lure from his lair under
the store’s floor-boards. But let a uniform
heave in sight, the pup does everything but
present arms, and rushes out to escort the
soldier to the door. The three dogs, how-
ever, whose names
will be oftenest re-
called in distant
bivouacs are (or
werel Platooney,
Squadrille and Comp
(short for “Company”
—and good company
he tried to be —and
died to be). When a
recent “finished” de-
tachment left the
school to return to
their several States,
the dogs followed
them 24 miles to Can-
berra. Here the lads
entrained for Quean-
beyan, seven miles
. away. Whining their
protests at being left,
the pups set out in
pursuit of the train.
Comp is believed to
have fallen into the
Molonglo River from
the railway bridge
which is an undecked
structure; but Squad-
rille and Platooney
apparently leapt from
sleeper to sleeper, and
reported for duty 10
minutes after the
train reached Quean-
beyan. Platooney was
limping badly. A sol-
dier, too Australian to
desert a mate, hopped
out and took Squad-
rille into the carriage.
Platooney, heart-broken, slunk under the
train ; but another good mate shouted, "Take
both or leave both,” and the second dog
went, too.

“Maire”: The Maoris have responded well
to the invitation to join in the Big Scrap ;
but there is still much good material unem-
ployed. Therefore Defence Minister Allen
with a military retinue lately paid an official
visit to the headquarters of the Waikato
tribe, at Mercer, to solicit the aid of “King”
Kata in stimulating recruiting among his
followers. The monarch was, unfortunately,
detained with State affairs elsewhere, but
he sent his Prime Minister to represent him
—an affable, much-tattooed and grey-whis-
kered old gentleman who limped painfully
in a pair of new boots he was wearing for
the occasion. In the resultant korero Allen
hinted that conscription was applicable to
Hori and his white brother alike —-an aspect
which the Prime Minister promised to duly
place before his Majesty. But on that
point his Majesty may put the Defence

“Buccleuch” : Though rejected three times
I feel, and more strongly than ever since
the other men and their wives decided on
October 28 to let Australia back out, that
there must be much that I can do. But
for the dunderheaded military rule that no
man ineligible for infantry is any use for
anything, I would have, been “somewhere
in Egypt” nearly two years ago. A year
ago I tried to convince the chief medical offi-
cer that clerical work and other backline
jobs can be quite efficiently performed by a
man with a reasonable amount of physique
and education (I am a Melb. Uni. graduate),
even though he may possibly be unfit for
infantry. It was no use. Later in the
year, feeling I must do something, I
obtained a place on the field staff of the
Y.M.C.A., but that work faded away as a
result of the general closing of camps two
months ago. Meanwhile I had again hopes
of A.I.F. work, nourished by an A.M.C.
major at the October examination, but shat-
tered five weeks later. At present my only
hope, so far as I can see, is to look for
some way of earning a passage to Britain,
where at any rate most of the population
Know there is a war on, and that a man
should be used for what he can do rather
than spurned and discouraged because of
what he cannot. If there 1 is any way in
which I can make myself acceptable for any
corner at all in the greatest work of our
time, I hope I may look for some guidance
through The Bulletin.
From an Anzac officer in England :
English officers complain that our men do not
salute, and make nasty remarks about our ideas of
discipline. Yet I know that I get three salutes
from Australians for every two from an' equal
number of Tommies. There are two reasons for the
English officers’ complaints: (1) Our men look for
rank badges on shoulders and miss the cuffs; (2)
English officers are very slack in acknowledging
salutes. Many seem to think that a nod or any
old wave of the flipper is sufficient. In Australia
we are taught to respond rather more smartly than
we receive, but here, on the other side, the compli-
ment is often ignored, and our men only require to
be turned down a couple of times to give the next
English officer the glassy eye. I have myself pulled
up English subalterns on two occasions and made
them salute privates whom they had ignored. Aus-
tralians may not be more popular with those two
gentlemen, but they may see that there are two sides
to the complaints.

From the desert patrol:—
We have seen all the Bedouin farming operations.
The sowing is done mostly by women and children,
who follow a wonderful wooden contrivance, a kind
of plough-harrow which just scrapes the surface soil.
The women carry earthenware bowls on their heads
and look picturesque enough as they listlessly scatter
the grain behind the squeaking plough, drawn
always by a broken-winded, sorrv-looking camel,
driven by a shabby but very dignified patriarchal
person white of beard and of turban. His age
makes it quite all right for women of all ages to
go unveiled before him. The threshing is done by
means of a circular train of camels, five old spirit-
less crocks, who groan and grunt with disgust as,
tied head to tail, they tread out the grain on the
primitive threshing floors. The reaping is done with
a curved, long knife, or the stalks are stripped by
hand. The winnowing is a pretty sight. Women
and girls stand in the middle of piles -of grain,
which they lift in earthenware bowls, and by a
slcight-of-hand trick many . thousands of years old,
send it flying upward and outward. As most of
them are graceful they look fine in the centre of
the swirling golden cascades. The fact is we are
living through a series of living pictures taken
from a well-illuminated Family Bible. On the
whole, these lady agriculturists are not badly
treated, no one but the Sheikh having the right to
punish them in any way; but they are a solemn,
proud and somewhat disdainful lot, and no Ginger
Mick would care to “word” them. To see them
setting off to the wells at sundown you would
reckon from their airs that they were all Zenobias
or uncrowned Queens of Sheba. They’re not, but
they have glorious eyes and superb figures, and
their domestic habits might well be copied by some
of our European women. They are ideal and most
devoted mothers, and (seemingly) most attentive
wives. True, I write merely as a looker-on, and
lookers-on at matrimony are frequently deceived.

‘A agabond” : So far I have not seen any
fair description of the “tanks” or “pepper-
pots,” as I’ve heard them rather neatly
nicknamed. The point that most of the
imaginative artists have fallen down and
sprained their pictures on is the caterpillar-
feet arrangement. This does not, as they all
seem to suppose, revolve under cover, and
is not a series of tramping feet. It is an
endless band which grinds round outside each
of the armored fort walls. The band travels
backward on the horizontal when the mon-
ster moves on level ground, turns up and
forward at an acute angle, goes forward on a
line parallel to the ground-floor portion,
turns backward parallel to the rear line,
turns downstairs and backward parallel
to the rear line. This is a some-
what cumbersome description and it
may be clearer if I say that, looked
at sideways, the endless band is roughly
diamond-shaped, with one of its sides on
the ground. The fort part of the body has
a round-shaped front with a couple of loop-
holes like eyes. Amidships on each side is
a bulge like a rounded bow-window, each
bulge having a gun poking out for’ard and
another aft, so as to rake any boarding party
closing in on bow or stern quarter. Although
the ’bus isn’t shaped anything like a tor-
toise,. its movements are most remarkably like
that insect’s. To see him crawling steadily
along over ground churned into one ragged
frazzle of big and little holes by shells is
most amusing, and it’s little wonder the
troops yelled with delighted laughter at him.
He looks absurdly life-like nosing and clawing
at a trench parapet till his fore-feet get a
grip, heaving himself ponderously up until
his fore-eud tips over and he cautiously
crawls down the other side with his hind feet
waving in the air, lurching sideways into
deep holes, with one side grinding along the
top edge and the other down below, taking
the smaller holes and narrow trenches in his
stride, wheeling heavily and lumbering along
a trench edge and squirting sudden death
down into it. Yes, he’s a funny beast, the
funniest I’ve seen in this war. And yet the
Hun doesn’t see a single gleam of humor in
him.
* * * *
“Croce di Malta” : Much twaddle has been
printed lately about the Maltese. S. M.
Hekald, for instance, says they are of
Phoenician origin. There certainly are
Phoenician (Carthaginian) antiquities in
Malta, also some little Carthaginian blood,
just as there are Greek, Roman, Spanish
and Slavic infusions; but the staple of the
population is an Italo-Arabic compound.
More than half the language is modified
Arabic; the rest is corrupt Italian, with a
flavoring of Spanish. The local peerage—
of Austrian, Spanish, Genoese and Papal
creation—is fully recognised by Britain, and
the “premier noble,” Baroness Diar-il-Bniet
(Arabic), officially attended the coronation
of Edward VII. The present Governor of
N. S. Wales is Conte della Catena in the
Maltese peerage. Finally, the Maltese
people—writer has lived amongst them —
are good-looking, good-humored, good
workers and good British subjects. Surely
that fills the bill.

“T. Messmate” : The other night we were
talking to a couple of girls at the pub, and
got on to war-time fashions and reforms.
Someone mentioned the Daylight Saving Bill ;

the middle of the talk the Son of the House
came out and caught the trend thereof. '
“Wot’s this I seen in the paper the other
day,” he butted in, “about a bloke getting
married by proximity?”

“Three Stripes”: Owing to the airs which
it gives itself, the V.A.D. is not excessively
popular with the Australian army nurses'
in England; and the irritation has been
increased by the latest order, which forbids
the nurses to be seen abroad with Austra-
lian privates in uniform. The restriction
does not apply to the Voluntary Aid dam-
sels ; it is aimed only at the nurses, who,
being amenable, to military discipline, can-
make no effective protest against a ukase
which debars them from associating with
their own brothers if these latter are mere
privates with their khaki on. More or less
apropos, a British officah lately found among
the guests at an Australian hostess’s dinner
party one of these same dreadful privates.
Though it was the hostess’s brother, and
though the dinner was given in his honor,
the belted snob explained that if the private
remained, he, the officah and gentleman,
must retire. The officah and gentleman
retired.

“The Old Soldier”: “Blighty” is «n
Indian corruption of the Arabic “WilayaHe,”
meaning “foreign” ; but as there, is no “w”
in Sanskrit or any of its derivatives, natives
of India change that sound into “b,” just as
Frenchmen turn it into “v.” Thus “Wil-
ayatee” becomes “Bilayatee.” As Tommy
came from a “Bilayatee” part, he naturally
assumed that “Blighty” was Hindustani for
Whitechapel or thereabouts.
EQUAL SACRIFICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Our claims to equality of service,” concluded the
Bishop, “are a damnable lie. We who cannot fight
shoulder to shoulder with the men in the trenches
should endeavor to come into touch with them
through our supplication to God.”
“tt hat did you do?” the battered soldier said.
“What sacrifice was yours when murder wallowed
In seas of pain amid the mangled dead,
And we the faint, far light of honor followed ?”
His arm hung limply at his twisted side,
Six medals on his shabby tunic glittered.
“Where were you when the doors of Hell were wide,
And all the ways with agonies were littered ?”
The saint looked upward with a _holy smirk.
“Scorn not,” he said, “the humble tasks of
wowsers.
The equal sacrifice we did not shirk.
Behold the dread condition of my trousers!”
He waved six pairs of ragged pants on high.
“These deeds were done to serve and save the
nation.
I could not fight—l do not like to die.
/ wore them out in earnest supplication !”
N.S.W.
PAT O'MAORI.
EPHEMERA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was a rorty young pierrot,
In panniers you were a vision.
What was your name I may not know,
But we were born in realms Elysian
Where music raved with fine emotion,
And not one serious, worldly notion
Possessed the mad inhabitants,
Born but to laugh, and love, and dance.
What were we ere your lips touched mine
I know not, and it doesn’t matter.
Existence stretched for us from nine
Till five next morn, ’mid merry clatter,
In iridescent colors shining 1 ,
With hands, and often lips, entwining.
Within those hours we had our day,
Lived, laughed, and loved, and passed
away!
I speak of you and I, dear, yet
It was not I but some blessed creature
That left me thoughts I can’t forget
And not another mutual feature.
W T hat came of you, my day-fly tender —
A glorious death in all your splendor?
Far better than my wretched fate,
Reverted to the larva state!
VICTOBIA.
SILAS SNELL.

Returns of Insurance Premiums Re-
quired from Persons, etc., Insuring
with any Insurance Company Not
Registered in New South Wales.
Pursuant to the. Fire Brigades Act 1909 and the Fire
Brigades Amendment Act. 1910.
ALL persons, firms, companies and asso-
ciations, being owners, trustees or
/gents for any goods or property within
the Sydney Fire District, and every other
Fire District in the State of New South
Wales to which the Fire Brigades Act 1900
and the Fire Brigades Amendment Act 1910
have been applied, in respect of which
premiums are paid to an insurance com-
pany not registered in the State, are
hereby notified that they are -required to
send to the Board of Fire Commissioners of
New South Wales returns showing the
amount of such premiums paid by them in
respect of the said property to such insur-
ance companies during the year 1916.
The time appointed for receipt of the
returns is on or before the 31st January,
1917, subject to a penalty not exceeding £1
per day for every day of default.
NOTE. —Returns are required only of the
proportion of the premiums piopeily chaige-
able in respect of the Fire Risk during the
time such property was. in any lire District
under the said Acts.
By Order of the Board of Fire Commis-
sioners of New South Wales.
11. M. WEBB. Secretary.
213 Castlereagh Street. Sydney.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Irvine Coulter, one of the oldest inhabi-
tants of Ballarat, has now two D.S.O.’s in
the family. It is a remarkable crowd alto-
gether. Major Leslie Coulter was given a
gold medal by the directors of the Mount
Lyell mine for bravery in rescue work when
the great disaster occurred there some years
ago. When the Kaiser let his barbarians
loose in Europe Leslie helped to organise an
Engineers and Miners’ Corps at Mount Lyell
for service at the Front, and was awarded
his D.S.O. some months ago. Lieut.-Col.
(then Private) Graham Coulter won the
Queen’s Medal, witli clasps, in the African
disturbance. Subsequently he became one
of the leading oarsmen and footballers of
Ballarat, and when war broke out he steered
straight for the recruiting goal. He took
part in the Landing at Gaba Tepe, remained
on the Peninsula till the evacuation, and the
latest list of D.S.O.’s contains his name.
Captain S. Coulter, the oldest of the pack,
stopped one of Kruger’s bullets at Bensburg,
and the grass is growing over his grave in
South Africa. Johnny Turk finished another
brother, Sergeant Jason Coulter, at Gal-
lipoli.
FINLAYSON,
M.H.R. (Q.),
listening to a joke.
“Spanwire” : Tom Glynn, editor of Direct
Action, who has been sentenced in Sydney
to 15 years for preaching the wrong brand of
politics, served in the S’African expedition
with a Victorian contingent. When the war
was over he joined the Natal Mounted
Police, served during the Bambaata dust-up,
and was suspended from duty for refusing
to shoot a Zulu hoy during a drive of the
rebel blacks. lie was imprisoned, and put
back for a court-martial for . disobeying
orders. The court-martial never eventuated,
for Miss Colenso, the Bishop’s daughter,
heard of Glynn’s case, and he was released.
He never returned to the Police, but went
up country to Jo’burg, where he got work on
the trams, became interested in the radical
movement, and was the mainspring of the
first great tramway strike. He got a spell
of gaol for that. He edited a few issues of
the Voice of Labor, which was run by
Archie Crawford, one of the deported union
leaders in 1014. Following this, he tracked
to Galway, Ireland, and then made for the
States, which he toured hobo fashion. After
a wheat-harvest in Calgary, he went down
below among the Makura’s fires and worked
back to Australia. He is a profound
admirer (what Galway man is not?) of
Robert Emmett, and could be induced in
convivial moments to give a first rate recital
of his hero’s historic speech from the dock.

who used to be parish priest of Richmond
(Vic.). Some years ago there was another
soldier-priest in Richmond, Father Mclniry,
who was through the American uncivil war.

Alex. Murdock, who died lately, aged 57,
was the N. S. Wales detective who with
lioche and Rochaix kept a brotherly eye on
the King and Queen —then Duke and Duchess
of York—on their Australian pilgrimage,
lie retired in 1911, as the result of, an

Captain R. -R. Harper, who can wear the
D.S.O. and the French War Cross nowa-
days, is a son of Dr. Andrew Harper, head
of the Presbyterian College at Sydney Uni.
When the Devil was unchained a couple ot
years ago, Harper was doing a medical
course at. Sydney, and had won his double
blue” by playing Rugby with the first 15
and rowing in the inter-’Varsity eight. He
got into khaki in time to spend five smoky
months at Gallipoli, where he was wounded
in the head. The D.S.O. and the French
decoration came to hinj for leading Ins Bill-
jims in a successful attack on a Hun trench
at Pozieres. Wounds received on that occa-
sion have sent him back to Australia while
the patching process is being completed, but
he reckons on being in the final round.
On present appearances the A.W.U.,
greatest of Australian labor organisations,
will throw W. G. Spence out of the pre-
sidency and instal F. W. Lundie. ‘Old
man” Spence practically created the A.W.U.,
and he is as good a unionist as ever he was;
but because he didn’t curse conscription, not
being required to do so by any pledge lie
had ever taken, he is led to .the scaffold.
Lundie is of a very different type—a long,
lean bushman, full of fight as a bull-ant.
lie followed Barnes, now Senator. as
A.W.U. agent in the West Darling, and has
for a good many years past been raising
Cain in Adelaide.
“TJloola” : The youngest Billzac? Perhaps
this is he. He enlisted at Sydney in July.
1915, aged 15 years and 10 months; had his
10th birthday in Liverpool camp; embarked
for Egypt Dec. 22, ’l5; had two months m
the desert between Cairo and the Canal
(“Bully beef and biscuit and a quart of
water a day,” he reported) ; arrived in
France April 3, ’l6; was at the Somme and
is now at Ypres. He went away in the
infantrv, but in the re-shuffle in Egypt was
transferred to the Pioneers. He was an
infant sft. Uin. tall when he enlisted, with
a chest measurement of 33. expanding to 35
inches. He has so far escaped wounds and
hasn’t had a day’s sickness —nor has he done
a single grumble. I am proud of him: he
is “Uloola” jun.
The 86-year-old widow of the late Francis
Peter McCabe, who died the other day, was
the eldest daughter of the wealthy pastoral
Osbornes. Her father, Henry Osborne, came
from Tyrone to N. S. Wales in the very
early ’3o’s, settled in Illawarra, raised a
family of 13, and made all his 10 sons
squatters. F. I*. McCabe was one of the
earliest surveyors in N. 8. Wales; he was on
the staff of the Theodolite-General as far
back as 3841.
The new Bishop of Ballarat, whose name
was Smith and is Gumbleton, comes with a
reputation for straightening church finance.
No doubt, his motto is “Watch and pay.
Australia has found it a lot easier to leave
the “r” in.
Add W. H. Moule, of the Victorian Dis-
trict Court, to the judges mentioned last
week who have lost sons in the war. This
judge’s boy was a lance-corporal who went
away with the first Light Horse and was
killed by a shell 'explosion in Gallipoli on
August 7, 1915.
Cecil Ilartt has got into the London
Times. Invalided to England, the Sydney
hi ack-and-whiter produced a portfolio of
humorous war drawings which, the Times
says, “show both good draughtsmanship and
humor and real power of characterisation.
We do not know,” it adds, “which is the
truer —his type of Australian or his type of
English soldier.”
Pat Fraser, who has found his way into
Massey’s bastille in Maoriland for .saying
nasty things about the Compulsory Service
Act, comes from Inverness, and owns a
tartan-plaid accent and a belief that juvenile
smoking should be prohibited and national
whitebait factories established. Fraser has
been prominent in Auckland and Waihi
labor circles, and struck trouble in the ’33
strike for saying something allegedly sedi-
tious. For a long while he has been the
organising secretary of the Social Demo-
cratic Party, and his energy has kept that
lot from taking a trip to the cemetery. Pat
has a quick tongue. Once when he was try-
ing to organise a railway construction
workers’ union in the Poverty Bay district
he was making a speech previous to taking
the names for the union, when somebody
chimed in with “Rot!” “Just wait a minute,”
said Pat. “I’m not quite ready for your
name yet. Tell me again when I’ve finished
talking.”
Master F. Radford, of Parkham (Tas.),
reckons up his years and makes the sum
101 —or 102 on March 11. When his
first cry piped around St. Pancras’, London,
the smoke was just clearing off Waterloo.
At 28 he left England in the Lord Auck-
land, which took six months or so to reach
Tasmania. After farming awhile at Exton,
he cut a track into the bush and made a
home in what is now a respectable village.
The humpy was pitched seven miles from
the nearest track, and bushrangers were his
nearest neighbors. His youngest was
killed at Gallipoli; whereupon another
promptly dropped his tools and went to ask
Abdul about it.
W. J. ROBERTS, Chairman Melbourne
Stock Exchange.
Died at 80 ex-Detective Superintendent
Joe Brown, of Vic., who shepherded King
George when he was here with Prince Albert.
Brown’s mate at the time was O’Callaghan,
who afterwards became Chief Commissioner.

must add to the list of those who use the
mitre otherwise than “only on their note-
paper and their spoons.” “A. Nutter Adel-
aide” has been wearing his in public for
three years.
LOUIS EBSON, poet
and playwright, now
on his way to A merica
to try his luck.
Among the New Year honors was a Mili-
tary Cross for Captain W. C. Scurry, a
North Melbourne officer whose record is
worth having. Before he was 21 he sur-
rendered his commission in the Essendon
Rifles and enlisted in the Ttli Battalion.
He was in the thick of Gallipoli, where he
won„ the D.C.M.. mostly for inventing a
device for tiring rifles automatically. This
trick was played at the evacuation, and
deceived Abdul into believing that the
Anzacs were still in the trenches, from
which they had withdrawn an hour or two
previously. Given a commission by General
Munro and mentioned in despatches, he
was transferred to the 58th Battalion, and
as a captain in the 59th won the- Military
Cross in France. He is now with one of
the Mortar Batteries.
Adelaide, which is accumulating statues
at a great rate, is to honor Flinders next.
The early navigator never set foot near the
city for whose site Light was responsible
later, but he sailed about the Gulf, and from
Kangaroo Island gave the present name of
“Mount Loftv” to the highest point in the
range just behind where Adelaide now is.
Also he left at Port Lincoln a tablet, some
fragments of which remain in the Adelaide
Museum, to commemorate the drowning of
some of his crew. Premier Crawford
Vaughan, at the recent annual commem.
lunch, said he thought the State’s obligation
to Flinders was even greater than that to
Light, and his Government will give a pedes-
tal and other help if a statue can be financed
by the usual enthusiasts.
“Albatross”: The cable tells us that
Archgaiters Riley, of Perth, was aboard
the transport Ivernia which was torpedoed
in the Mediterranean last week. I most
respectfully suggest that Dr. Riley be re-
quested to do his archbishing in some super-
drought country where a small boy could not
find enough water to float a paper boat in.
So far his Grace’s Jonah record runs this
way:—-
(1) On his first trip across the Atlantic the ship
caught fire. He told file this himself.
(2) He was a passenger on the small steamer
Macgreg'or When she broke her tail-shaft, and a dis-
tress rocket recurved and killed one of the seamen.
Burns, Philp can vouch for this.
(3) He was a passenger on the Rob Roy in one
of the worst ‘storms experienced between Albany!
and Esperance. I was second mate.
(4) He £ was a passenger on the Bullarra when
she struck a rock off the Nor’-West coast of Aus-
tralia. I was second mate.
(5) Now the Ivernia !
I’m glad to hear my old friend, is safe ; but,
in view of his “hoodoo,” would it not be a
fair thing if the authorities put him some-
where where other people can be reasonably
safe?
A PRIVATE GRIEVANCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“A new order forbids Australian nurses to be
seen abroad with Australian privates.”— ‘•Phyllis,”
in The British Australasian (London).
Our Billjim takes his walks abroad
Beside his latest mate,
And were his cobber twice a lord
He would not change his gait ;
He strolls around with any she
Who does not seem averse,
Save one —old Billjim is not free
To walk abroad with Nurse.
In hospital he is allowed
To call the lady “Sis,”
And sometimes she is not too proud
To let him snatch a kiss.
She answers if he rings the bell
Whilst he is bad, or worse;
But when the patient’s getting well
He may not walk with Nurse.
Perchance she feeds him with a spoon
Whilst he is in her care ;
She brings his cup of tea at noon
And combs his towzled hair ;
But when good Billjim, fat and strong,
With money in his purse,
Feeds girlie at a restaurant
She must not be the Nurse.
VICTORIA.
O. K.
JONAH, A.B.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A dirty man and he stank of tar,
He came from Hell or the Evil Star ;
I-Ie cursed the ship and he damned the crew,
And blasphemed over the rum he drew.
He spat on the pig and he spat on me;
“To hell,” he said, “with the rotten sea,
For doomed we are, we’re snugging down
For Port o’ Hell ’stead of Sydney town.”
So followin’ Scripture’s law, d’ye see,
As a salt full o’ Christian piety,
. F kicked at his hands one night in a gale
And sent him back to his whale!
N'.S.W.
JOHN BARR.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Federal Treasurer is asking the co-
operation of employers and employees to
make the new War Loan a success. Circu-
lars have been issued to each section, as well
as to the general public. To these last the
Treasurer says
Patriotism and self-interest alike demand that
we maintain the fight with all our strength. The
provision of funds is just as necessary as the pro-
vision of men, and most urgently are you appealed to
for assistance. You are asked to exercise self-denial
and to contribute as much of your funds as possible
towards the war loan. You are asked to realise
that on you depends something. To some extent at
least the proper maintenance of the fight depends
upon you. If you do not give for the purpose of
the war as much money as you can give, then you
cannot say to yourself that you have done as well
as you should have done. The terms of the loan
have been so arranged that comparatively poor per-
sons may subscribe. Those who cannot afford more
most probably could in 10 months find £lO, paying
£ 1 per month.
Employers are asked to collect instalments
from their employees, to whom the following
appeal is made :
No doubt you have yourselves pondered deeply
upon the matters at issue, but may I point out that
not only are the lives of our soldiers imperilled,
but on the result of the war depends the liberty of
every individual in the British Empire? Personal
freedom and the right to make laws relating to con-
ditions of life and employment, even employment
itself, are at stake.
On every ground of patriotism and self-interest
I ask you to enter upon a period of self-denial.
Some, of course, cannot out of wages do more than
provide for themselves and their families. There
are, however, many who can, either out of savings or
out of current wages, provide £lO or more. Please
do all that you can.
The payment of ss. per week for a period of 40
weeks will entitle the subscriber to a AVar Bond of
£lO, on which interest amounting to 7s. 6d. will
be paid on December 15, 1917, and thereafter inter-
est will be paid half-yearly at the rate of 44 per
cent, per annum, until the date of the repayment of
the loan in 1925. Your employer will, if authorised
bv vou in writing, do all that is necessary to pur-
chase for you a bond or bonds, retaining the proper
sum from your weekly wages.
There is no donbt whatever that, if we bad
a National Government, embodying the
strength of all parties, the appeal wonkl bo
more sympathetically received. But let us
remember that we do not punish Hughes or
Pearce or Cook or any other politician by
starving this loan. We punish the men at
the Front, and through them ourselves and
Civilisation.

Business has not shrunk during the war,
whatever it may do afterwards. Notes and
bills in circulation, both small items, have
shrivelled a little; all others except bank
premises are larger, including deposits,, liquid
assets and 'advances, which are the import-
ant lines. Here are the figures as at
November, 1918, at the same date in 1916
and 10 years ago :
1906. 1913. 1916.
Free deposits .. . . £287,252 £333,765 £355,739
Fixed deposits .. .. 240,745 328,004 326,476
Other liabilities .. 76,830 26,499 18,211
Liquid assets .. .. 201,373 198,589 248,608
Advances, etc. . . . . 567,775 689,371 714.238
Premises and sundries . . 28,209 28,617 24,923
“Other liabilities” are smaller because the
bank no longer issues notes, that being now
a Commonwealth monopoly. The Common-
wealth notes which it holds are, of course,
included with the liquid assets. These assets
represent a reasonable 35 p.c. of the liabili-
ties ; but it doesn’t allow T much of a margin,
and the bank couldn’t offer any more accom-
modation to speak of on its present
resources without unduly pinching its coin,
etc. But if the National is using every
penny that can safely be used, creditors can
take comfort from the fact that there is a
very good margin of cover so far as they
are concerned. The bank owes altogether
£700,426. Deduct liquid assets, and there
remains £451,818 of debt, which is repre-
sented by non-liquid assets valued at
£739,160. There’s good, untroubled sleep
for depositors in those figures.

*Subject to remarks hereunder.
It is a fine big gamble. The co. chanced to
have for its chairman S. W. Copley, the
Westralian sporting man who knows a lot
about “consultations” and other flutters;
and soon after the war started he saw his
chance. Going to England, he plunged in
underwriting, and here is the result: —
Net Loss Expense
Premiums. Losses. Expenses. Rate. Rate.
1913.. £1,790 £22 £874 1.2 p.c. 48.8 p.c.
1914.. 3,519 770 3,429 21.8 „ 97.4 „
1915.. 52,061 17,424 16,074 33.4 „ 30.8 „
1916.. 180,716 116,763 40,118 64.6 „ 22.2 „
They are remarkable figures, How much of
them the co. is going to hold after the war
remains to be seen. The nature of the
business is pretty well indicated by the
expense rate. It consists largely of marine
underwriting and reinsurance.
The profit is “subject to remarks here-
under.” In plain words, the directors don’t
know and we don’t know how much is real
profit. It certainly all isn’t: of the total
reserves no less than £39,500 is to the credit
of the unexpired risks and contingencies
fund. Last year, for instance, allowing that
all old business was renewed, there was
£128,655 of premiums derived from new busi-
ness. This new business was no .doubt
spread over the whole year, so that, as an
average, the risk had several months to run
at the end of the co.’s year. In ordinary
circumstances it is reckoned that a co. ought
to have a reserve for unexpired risks (or
unearned premium reserve, as some call it)
equal to 40 p.c. of the year’s premiums; and
in this case that would mean £72,000. But
that seems excessive where a large part of
the business is probably in risks for single
voyages. When you take a new fire risk in
June, you almost invariably do it for 12
months, so that you have half the risk lo
run at the end of December. But with
marine insurance the risk taken in June may
expire in August. It is the same with
many of the “riot and civil commotion”
policies which were issued during the year.
Most of them didn’t run more than six
months. But while this co. doesn’t thus
perhaps need a 40 p.c. reserve, it is much
more certain that it needs some; in other
words, some of the profits shown above are
not true profits. Indeed, the management
doesn’t claim they are.
In view of the remarkable growth of busi-
ness some managements would have been
enticed into plunging into dividends much
fatter than 6 p.c. But the co. does the right
thing in keeping them at 6 p.c. until it
knows just where it is ; and it can’t know
with this very big contingent liability on
hand. Ordinarily we expect an insurance
co. to have funds equal to at least 140 p.c.
of its year’s premiums. That would be
£250.000. But the total net assets were only
£lO5 064 before paying the last dividend.
The position, therefore, needs careful nursing
—just as much in the iiiterests of share-
holders as of policy-holders; for every one
of the 240,000 shares issued carries a liability
of ss.

The directors of this exceedingly affluent
concern evidently think that the least said
the soonest mended; so the report is the
mere rattling of a few dry bones done as
inconspicuously as possible. Poor old John
Bull, fighting for his life, is, of course, mak-
ing most of the purchases out of which in
two years the co. has netted confessed pro-
fits almost equal to one-half of its capital;
and it appears also to have written assets
down very generously. As at November,
1915, the co.’s six or eight works were valued
at £282,328; 12 months later they stand at
only £273,234. The most amazing thing is
that, though war has so obviously poured
money into this co.’s till, and though war
profits are admittedly indefensible, the co.
paid last year hardly any more in rates and
taxes than it did in years of peace. In 1996,
following a year in which it made a profit
of only £15,074, rates and taxes absorbed
£1962. In the past year, following a profit
of £53,044, the outgo on the same account
was £4781; and no doubt rates on the
numerous new properties accounted for much
of the increase. It all amounts to a scandal,
because the money will have to be got pre-
sently, and it will then be collected not from
those who have benefited by the war, but
equally from those who have suffered bit-
terly by it.
Chiefly because the co. has been very
moderate in its dividends no matter how
fat its profits, it carries on its very large
business without leaning heavily upon the
bank. Here are the principal figures:—
1906. 1915. 1916.
Liabilities .. .. £97,920 £111,2(12 £92,421
Works and fixed assets. . 267,783 282,328 273,234
Stocks 25,010 55,145 76,212
Book debts, etc. . . 25,526 114,193 105,507
Between 1906 and 1916 the co. has got in
£25.142 of capital ; but it has reduced its
liabilities by £5499, added £131,183 to its
semi-liquid assets, and paid for all additions
to its works and fixed property —and how
much that amounts to we can’t even guess.
It certainly would be more than enough to
buy a packet of peanuts.

That is, if everything turns out all right.
But the balance-sheet shows among the
assets “Interest due by borrowers, £24,407,”
as against £11.012 two years previously;
and it looks as though a good deal of this
may have to be written off. The managing
director explains:—
The considerable amount of interest in arrears is
to a large extent caused through clients of the bank
having enlisted, and in consequence we' have not
pressed for the payment thereof.
In various respects the bank isn’t run on
strict commercial lines. For instance, though
the bank sometimes has to foreclose, it puts
the security into cold storage, and “gives
clients an opportunity to redeem their hold-
ings should their circumstances improve. It
is not the policy of the bank to realise unless
the position is hopeless.” Probably because
the bank wasn’t to be run on commercial
lines it wasn’t thought necessary to organise
it on commercial lines. Hence —
In our last report we suggested that either the
accountant of the Lands Department have full con-
trol or that the Bank do so, as under the present
arrangement duplication of work exists. ... Divided
control of the accounts has been far from satisfactory
so far as the Bank is concerned.
It is obvious that the whole thing wants
reorganising. And while they are about it
they had better turn it into a business con-
cern pure and simple. They will do much
more good to the farmer that way. If the
bank gives charitable assistance it can do
it only at the expense of the other clients;
and most of them are young stragglers who
are not in a position to do much helping just
yet.
From time to time various improvements
have already been made in the constitution
and practice of the bank, with the result
that a very much larger number of settlers
have of recent years consented to take
assistance. This shows various movements:

The repayments appear to have been very
slow the last couple of years, and how much
principal is overdue there is no telling. So
far only £783 has been written off and pro-
vided for; but 42 properties which have been
foreclosed upon are still in the hands of the
bank. Curiously and improperly, these fore-
closures don’t appear in the balance-sheet
either. The only assets are —advances fo
borrowers, £1,186,895 ; interest due. £24.408;
furniture, etc., £7O. Apparently the fore-
closures are included with the advances;
and there is no distinction between advances
overdue and otherwise. These are flaws in
the accounts that ought not to be repeated.

£3,800 £57,539 £61,339
That is to say, the total profit, exclusive of
what it is necessary to contribute to a sink-
ing fund for the repayment of capital, is
£3BOO. No dividend has been paid to the
State, which supplied the capital; but, of
course, interest has been paid—somewhere
about 3f p.c. In all, the State has advanced
£250,000, of which £7050 has been repaid,
in addition to some temporary loans.
The coal property cost the State nothing;
and there were no promotion expenses or
anything of that sort. Yet, up to now, the
proposition has barely paid the State its
3| p.c. and provided enough to cover depre-
ciation and feed the sinking fund. Of this
sinking fund, by the way, £39,730 is in
Government stock, £10,760 in cash and the
balance apparently is “invested in the busi-
ness,” to employ the usual cant. The sooner
it is put into Government stock the better.
And there ought to be a public trustee to
hold it.
An amount of £10,760 put by each year
and improved at compound interest will
wipe out the existing capital debt in about
16 years;.and, all going well, the mine will
last many years longer than that. Coal
produced during the past 12 months was
452,798 tons, and in the previous year
567,056 tons. The manager says: “While
much useful and necessary information has
been gained, my previous estimate of avail-
able coal reserve in the State-mine area,
approximately 20,000,000 tons, has been
altered only by the amount of coal extracted
during the year.” The mine should thus
have a good many years of life after the
sinking fund has repaid the State.
Meantime the undertaking is severely han-
dicapped by the restrictions put upon it by
the Leg. Council. It may not sell large coal
to the public; that may be sold only to the
Railways and other Government depart-
ments. The public may be supplied only
with slack. During the past year the cus-
tomers were: Railways, 275,658 tons; other
departments, 12,318 tons; public, 145,546
tons. No doubt the public gets a small
direct advantage out of the working of the
mine, inasmuch as it keeps the private
owners who supply the balance of the rail-
way requirements from becoming too rapa-
cious. But the benefit would be much larger
if the Wonthaggi miners took their part
more seriously. “The mine lost 23 days,”
the manager says, “owing to the refusal of
the employees to remain at work while the
new agreement was under discussion, and
other short stoppages were occasioned by
sectional strikes and stop-work meetings,
while employees also absented themselves for
14 days in connection with a fatal accident.”
The workers are all or nearly all Labor
men ; and this is how they proceed to demon-
strate the advantages of State ownership,
which is a leading plank in their platform!

Babcock and Wilcox Water-tube Boiler, so univer-

sidered, is due, no doubt, to the splitting up of the
water into 4in. tubes, which permit of rapid circula-

lin. plates used in the Shell Boiler being done away
with.— Advt.

whereas the six months’ output has averaged
£lO7 12s. (id. on realisation. With the
exception of small quantities for local con-
sumption the whole of the 1917 output has
been sold to the British Munitions Depart-
ment at £l2O per ton c.i.f. London.
It is the costs that are still the weak
feature of the Mt. Morgan position ; and in
part they seem to be due to a still-defective
organisation. For the last six months they
averaged 50s. 10.99 d. per ton as against
475. 10.7 d. for the previous half-year. The
net profit was equal to £3l Is. Gd. per ton
of copper produced. Taking the average
price realised for the half-year’s production,
this means that the cost was £76 11s.
Strictly, it wasn’t quite as much as that,
because, whereas 1875 tons brought in from
the preceding term averaged £94 16s. 5d.,
2196 tons in stock at the end of the term
is valued at £55. In other words, the half-
year benefits by £39 16s. sd. per ton on 1875-
tons and loses £52 12s. (xl. per ton on 2196
tons, assuming that the half-year’s average
can still be got for the metal in stock on
November 30. So the half-year’s accounts
gained about £74.000 from surpluses and
lost about £116,000 by taking in copper at
£55 instead of £lO7 12s. 6d., and the differ-
ence is £42,000. If we add that to the
half-year’s profit we get £183,000, which
means that the profit per ton of output was
about £4O 3s. (id., and the true working costs
(including development and realisation)
about £67 9s. per ton. Unless a co. can
see how it can cut down costs severely to
meet altered circumstances, it can’t feel
very comfortable when it faces costs like
that.
The fact that this co. takes its unsold
copper into account at £55 shows wliat is
regarded as a fair thing in normal times.
And it hasn’t always been even £55. The
world passed through one of its periods of
depression (which generally means a period
of liquidation after boom commitments and
prices) in the earlier ’9o’s, when the market
for standard copper got down to these fig-
ures: 1892, £44; 1893, £42; 1894, £3B;
1895, £.39; 1896, £4l; then rising gently
until in 1900 the top was £79 and the bot-
tom £6B. Whether we are going to have low
prices in the near future this paper doesn’t
know. But, especially so far as metals are
concerned, they must be regarded as more
than possibilities. Among the probable fac-
tors governing future prices these things
have to be taken into account: (1) The
copper-producing countries of the world are
not in the war zone and are producing
largely; (2) no metal-production more
quickly responds to high prices than that of
copper; (3) a considerable part of the
metal now being “consumed” will be avail-
able for commercial purposes by and bye;
(4) high prices (of stores, etc.) have re-
sulted in a diminished output of gold, and
this will tend to gradually reduce prices of
all commodities.
Mt. Morgan mined 156,805 tons of its
reserves last half-year, and it had 4,176,795
tons left. Both sets of figures are exclusive
of low-grade ore outside the reserves; last
half-year 25,117 tons of this latter material
was mined. Assuming that these outside
ores can be drawn upon throughout in the
same proportion there is about 14 years’
work in sight; and efforts to increase the
figures have had poor results. If the co.
gave £200,000 profit per annum the total in
14 years would be £2,800,000, equal to 565.
per share; and he would be a venturesome
man who would say that the figures and
past experience warrant any such valuation.
In addition, however, the co. had at the end
of November liquid and semi-liquid assets
valued at £559,412 after providing for all
liabilities.

Australia’s biggest butter-maker, the North
Coast Co-operative Co. (N.S.W.), turned out
29201 tons (6,555,5011 b.) last half-year, as
against 2036 tons (4,500, SSI lb.) in the latter
half of droughty 3915. For the half-year’s
yield the co. got £425,397. The average
price realised for all grades was 15.57 d. per
lb. Straight out, suppliers got £376,892; in
addition they received a bonus of £3415.
Thus the suppliers of the raw material got
375. 9d. in the £ of what the finished pro-
duct realised straight out. From the
other 2s. 3d., plus incidental scraps of
revenue (ice and buttermilk sales, bonus and
dividend from the Coastal Farmers, who
market the butter, etc.), the co. paid all
manufacturing charges, freight to market,
exchange, etc. It is a triumph of co-opera-
tion and cash trading. Besides its butter
trade the co. realised £77,028 straight out for
bacon and paid suppliers £63,295. The divi-
dend is 0 p.c. on the very modest capital of
£33.012. Plant alone has cost £147,070, but
it has been written down to £62,974.
So far Sydney Ferries, Ltd., has been for-
tunate in escaping attack from season-ticket-
holders on the question of the signing away
of their rights as ordinary passengers. The
person who wants to buy travelling from
the co. wholesale finds it necessary to sign
a document containing these remarkable
clauses:—
The company shall not be liable for loss of life,
or injury, or delay, suffered by any passenger, how-
ever caused, nor loss of, or damages, or injury or
damage to passengers’ luggage, or other property
or chattels, whether such loss, damage, injury, deten-
tion or delay arise from accident or from theft,
or pilferage, or from any act, default, neglect or
error of judgment of masters, officers or crew, or
any other servant or agent of the company.
These conditions shall apply to any renewal of the
said ticket, and the issue of any future ticket to me.
There is absolutely no good reason why the
season-ticketholder should be placed on a
different footing from the occasional travel-
ler ; and it is at least doubtful whether, in
view of all the circumstances, the co. could
establish the position it assumes. But it
would cost a private individual a lot of
money to shift the co. out of that position.
This is distinctly a matter for Parliament.
If the Legislature thinks that the regular
traveller by the Harbor ferries should have
the same rights as the casual traveller, it
can very easily declare illegal such an unfair
“agreement” as the co. now requires its
regular customers to sign.
The Tas. Government proposes to pur-
chase from the Railton-Latrobe Shale Co.
its property, comprising 1500 acres of the
Mersey shale deposits at Latrobe. The
price is £65,000, for which the co. will take
44 p.c. debentures redeemable in 10 years.
The Commonwealth Government has con-
tracted to take at least 8000 tons of fuel
oil for a period of 10 years at £3 15s. per
ton in tanks at Devonport. The Speck Gov-
ernment proposes to spend £150,000 in plant,
development, pipe line, etc., making a total
capitalisation of £215,000. The plant will
have a capacity of 00,000 tons of shale per
annum, which will, on the estimates, pro-
duce per ton 35 gallons of fuel oil, seven
gallons of petrol and two gallons of crude
oil, or a total of 13,000 tons of fuel oil,
630,000 gallons of petrol and 18,000 gallons
of crude oil. Treatment costs, according to
Dr. Wade, will be 14s. 2d. per ton. If the
whole of the products are sold at pre-war
prices it is estimated that the venture will
show a profit of something over £30,000 per
annum. The Commonwealth Government
will probably take the whole of the fuel
output later on, but in any case there should
be no difficulty in disposing of the product.
If present prices are maintained, it would
mean another £20,000 profit. The import-
ance of having a local oil supply for the
Commonwealth Navy is the big factor, and
the Tas. Government is to be commended
for its pluck in developing the industry. The
matter was initiated by the late Labor Gov-
ernment, which had to leave before negotia-
tions were completed, and now the Lee Gov-
ernment, after looking closely into the
question, finds it so attractive, that Minis-
ters are sinking their objection to State
socialism in the hope of providing for the
time shortly when Tassy will lose the Fed-
eral grant and will then have to develop
something substantial or sue for annexation
by the Commonwealth.
* * * *
Silver-lead mining at Zeehan (Tas.) now
depends almost entirely upon tributors, who
are producing a fair amount of ore. Owing
to the fact that their labor counts as a com-
pliance, under the Mining Act, with the lease
covenants, they are keeping in ownership idle
companies to which they pay royalties.
Though the scale is lower now, 30, 40 and up
to 60 p.c. was not uncommon on the field,
and the tributor in a good many cases had
no agreement and had to trust to the sweet
will of the owner, who raised the royalty as
the ore improved. Last session Mines Minis-
ter Ogden, who knows all about the game,
introduced a Mining Bill which (1) cut out
tributors’ labor as a compliance with the
covenants, and (2) provided for a firm agree-
ment between owner and tributor, with no
royalty chargeable until the tributor has
earned £2 per week. The former provision
was eliminated in the Assembly, and the
latter passed. The Bill was not dealt with
by the Leg. Council last session, but was re-
introduced and passed by that Chamber this
year, and will pass the Assembly before
Parliament rises. The Council actually
raised the subsist allowance" to £2 10s. per
week, nut refused to agree to the proposal
regarding tributors’ labor, which would have
forced companies to spend some of their own
money or forfeit their leases. The tributor
will, however, have the protection of a firm
contract, which must fix area and depth of
ground, royalty and other conditions, and it
also secures him in the case of change of
ownership.
Pioneer (Tas.), with plenty of water for
sluicing, had a good year, and announces
a Is. 6d. dividend, which costs £12,000.
The output is from 500 to 600 tons of black
tin a year, and average costs are below £4O
per ton, which compares very well even with
Tongkah Harbor. “Prohibitive labor con-
ditions” and all thrown in, the Pioneer is
producing oxide assaying 73 p.c. tin at a
lower cost than most Siamese and Malay
cos.
* * * *
Briseis (Tas.), having its head office in
London, had to pay Britain 4s. 6d. in the £
on the £15,000 which it divided last month.
The previous dividend, in June, was taxed
at the rate of 2s. Bd. in the £. Briseis is
loaded with 600,000 shares, and its capital
is nominally £600,000, so its £15.000 divi-
dend is a modest 2| p.c. But shares are
rarely over 55., and on that basis a sixpenny
spells 10 p.c. The profit earned is about
£7OOO a quarter, which is equal to about 35
p.c. of the value produced.
* * *
Lefroy (Tas.), which has been in a state
of suspended animation for the best part of
20 years, is preparing to have a good time
on the strength of the Back Creek Deep
Leads. A report reads: “Struck heavy
water-worn cement wash, giving prospects
of coarse gold. The bottom is dipping to
S.E. and very smooth.” Shares took a
jump from one to five shillings. The Back
Creek is the last survivor of a number of
companies floated in Launceston, and only
a grant from the Government has enabled
it to survive. There is no telling what may
happen; certainly the lead until it dipped
under the basalt was about the richest piece
of dirt in Tasmania.
* * * *
Broken Hill Prop, is making a fine success
of slime treatment by the Bradford process.
It produced 1188 tons of lead and 3302 tons
of zinc concentrates in four weeks. The
feature of the return is the average assay
of 53.93 p.c. lead and 79.420 z. silver in the
lead concentrates.
* * * *
The Associated smelters at Port Pirie are
making improved practice. A while back an
extraction of 75 p.c. of the zinc from con-
centrates was good work, but an average of
80 p.c. is now usual. When spelter passes
£4O the men recelye a bonus pronortionate
to the extraction wffien it is over SO p.c.
* * * *
We can be proud of our metallurgy, any-
way. At the end of 1916 B. H. South was
recovering no less than 90 p.c. of the lead
from crudes averaging 14 p.c. Silver recov-
eries have been low for years, but the new
slimes plant is producing a concentrate con-
taining 58.7 p.c. lead and 47.80 z. silver.
From a 2i p.c. copper ore Mount Morgan,
with the help of the mineral separation pro-
cess, is recovering 93 p.c. of the copper and
79 p.c. of the gold. Sintered concentrates
assay 7.09 p.c. copper and 16.34dwt. of gold.
Wallaroo and Moonta is also doing remark-
ably well with low-grade copper ore by
mineral separation; and Hampdeh-Clon-
curry has been persuaded by that success to
provide itself with a similar plant.
* * * *
Kampong Kamunting is one of the not-too-
numerous Malay ventures that don’t do vio-
lence _to their estimates. Last month its
working costs (two dredges) were 2.87 d. per
cub. yard and _the working profit 6.58 d.,
equal in all to £5266. But even at the Ivarn-
•pong there is a considerable discrepancy
between boring results and recoveries. No.
1 dredge worked ground which the bores said
was worth .891 b. per yard. It actually got
out 1.121 b. On the other hand, *\o. 2, which
