theory, found it good for only .731 b. in
practice.
T> W\ A. Freeman, who has the Austral-
Bouvian tin proposition in hand, is on the
way to the United States, where there are
two or three financial groups that want to
know something more about it. War-time
regulations in Australia made a satisfactory
dotation here doubtful; so an American co
is probable.
Mount Oxide is to be connected by rail
with Mount Elliott’s smelter at Selwyn and
refinery at Bowen,- and a bill authorising a
State line is being engineered through the
Queensland Parliament. The sooner high-
grade and payable copper mines are linked
up with the railway system the better, for
John Bull is almost at the mercy of
America and Spain for his supply of red
metal. The extension to Mount Oxide
means 85 miles of rail.


MAKE YOUR OWN COUGH MIXTURE.
A pint of the finest quality remedy for
coughs, colds and throat troubles —equal to

Sydney, January S, 1917..
My Dear Arini. —
There was no Governor-General this year
to put a head on the local Scots’ New Year
gathering. The State Governor came to
town for the skirl, and veteran Dr. Mac-
kellar —rejuvenated by the mineral wash of
Moree—was amongst the old inhabitants at
the sports. Dr. Storie Dixson, Currie Elies,
Colonel Burns and a hefty' gathering of cosy
citizens with long tags of tartan dangling
from their buttonholes, foregathered from
morn till niclit. Which somehow reminds
one of Harry Lauder. For him this wasn’t
a happy first-footing, for an only son —the
one Lauder chick —was lately killed in
France.
The New Year’s concert filled the Town
Hall with much the same crowd as attended
the annual ceremony of Tossing the Caber.
But there were added a few old ladies,
dragged from urban retreats, who might,
barring the crinolines, have stepped out
of “Bunty Pulls the Strings.” One vener-
able dame had her back-hair caged in
a thick brown velvet net. The weird thing
was almost heavy enough to catch deep-sea
fish, and the tartan bow that finished the
coiffure was a small Macgregor’s gathering.
Colonel James Burns took the centre of the
front seats with his party and settled down
to a fearsome procession of talent. Eighteen
items, doubled by encores, didn’t exhaust the
perspiring Scots. Mrs. Verbrugghen, a
comely figure in brocaded silk, sang so
sweetly of Leezie Lindsay and other long-
dead charmers that her items were a regi-
ment of repeats. Richard McClelland, not
long back from England, was the star male
singer, for Phil Newbury was not in his
best form. John Lemmone and his magic
flute assisted. Manager James Chalmers,
of Farmer’s, was the hon. conductor of a
Highland Choir —much depleted, for most of
the lads are away at the war. And the heat
isn’t good for such withered sprigs as “The
Laird o’ Cockpen,” anyhow.
School-marms come and go. In the State
mill for grinding the Young Idea there are
some whose going, however long deferred,
will be regretted by many of the mothers of
the present generation. Amongst them is
Miss Elizabeth Hurst, who may retire
within the next 12 months. Starting as a
pupil-teacher in St. James’s C. of E. school
in 1867, and passing later as a trained
teacher, Miss Hurst taught Windsor infants
in 1872, and three years later went back to
St. James’s as head-mistress. Balmain in-
fants next supped of the three R’s at her
knee. She was mistress t>f Lower Fort-
street School in 1886, and then moved to
the Stanmore Girls’ School. From that until
now there have been no more changes. A
strenuous 50 years of active service in the
education of Cornstalks will be com-
pleted on July 1 of this year. For five days
a week during the past 31 years Miss Hurst
has walked up the same steps, rung the
same bell, and healed and comforted the
same sorrows that befall the younger
scholars. She’s too shy to send her photo—
even with so fine a jubilee in sight.
Governor Strickland took eldest daughter
Mary to Melbourne to see her aboard a boat
for the Jam Island. The busy girl hasn’t
been up to her top form since a minor opera-
tion a little while back. So she is going to
stay with the Chief Justice Cullens on the
hill beyond Hobart. With the indefatigable
Strickland, who wanted an official chat with
the Victorian Ex. Stanley, went the tactful
Secretary Budge.
The Law is moving hither and thither to
seek the iced breeze on mountain tops. The
Adrian Knox couple went to Hobart, and will
be able to foregather with the legal Cullens
at Mount Wellington.
Old squatter Charles Burcher, whose will
was proved at £168,000 the other day, was
uncle Charlie to many nieces and great-
nieces. Some of them were handsomely
remembered by the stern old relative with
the Daddy Christmas beard and beetling eye-
brows. He belonged to the pioneer type of
prosperous bushmen, and never spent a
penny on social fluff, but knew how to dip
into his moneybags when there was a solid
call for help. At a* Sydney show he generally
made a beeline for the sheepsheds—a kind
of lesser heaven to the old shepherd kings
from the edge of the sunset.
Mrs-. Chris Bennett, the much-liked State
missus of the N.S.W. Voluntary Aids, is in
mourning for her just-dead husband. He
was the Chris who followed the genial Frank
as managing-proprietor of the Evening
News and Town and Country Journal.
A London correspondent mentions the dis-
tinction the silly old War Office draws be-
tween women and ladies. If the new female
invasion is tolerated it is not exactly en-
couraged. Hence in the book in which
clerks sign their names there are two head-
ings. One is “Temporary Ladies." The
other is, “Permanent Women.” The “volun-
taries” are, of course, the ladies. Still, if I
had to choose, I think I’d rather be a per-
manent woman than a temporary lady.
When the war was mouths and months
younger The Bulletin told the story
which sounded like a double-barrelled
romance of an exalted Russian naval officer
named Domerschikoff. Though lie had fled
to Australia on account of political trouble,
he nevertheless decided when the big trouble
began that he must go back and chance the
consequences. His grateful country, on his
reappearance, promptly eourt-martialled
him, and lie was actually on his way to
Siberia when the pleading of influential
friends gained permission for him to tight in
the ranks. Since then he has gone gather-
ing promotions and decorations and honors
as easily as you or I might pluck fruit off
a tree. Dive Crosses of St. George, two
gold swords and the Belgian Cross sent to
iiim by the King of Belgium, are among the
tally iip to the date of the last letter from
his wife, who, before she decided to sample
the seething political pot of Europe, was
Miss Fussell, of Sydney. The lady writes
enthusiastically of her adopted country'. She
gives a very loud cheer for its excellent rail-
way systems, and she ought to know-* some-
thing about them, for she speaks of journeys
to Petrograd and Odessa, Baku and Tiflis,
Bessarabia and the Caucasus, as one might
of coming home from Manly by the Spit.
She finds the Russian a very cheery and
polite person, very different from the morbid
individuals who live in the pages of the
Russian novel.
Here is an Australian lass who has a
managing way with her. She is Miss Evans,
the bundle of tact in charge of the Ash-street
Anzac Buffet for returned Billjims. As
Commandant of the Mosman V.A.D.’s, the
capable young woman made a success of the
first Anzac Buffet in Sydney. This was in
the cellar under the old Education Building.
When that haven was carted away in loose
bricks the Mosman girls moved into premises
in Ash-street. There the cobwebs vanished
before clean paint, and for months it has
been one of the returned men’s favorite
lounge rooms.
Justice Ferguson on Saturday reopened
the Domain Anzac buffet under entirely new
management. The boss lady—who isn’t
looking for this personal ad. but meekly
swallows it to help the cause —is the same
Miss Evans. With her team of hard-worked
V.A.D.’s she organises a double shift. The
Ash-street premises go on as usual from
dusty morn to sunny eve —5 p.m. by the new
Ananias time —while the Domain Anzac
will take orders from Billjim during the
hours that stretch between 3 and 10 at
night. If you don’t want that batch of cakes
you’re making, send them to either of the
two Anzacs. It’s up to everyone to help
these devoted women.
There was a busy day at St. Francis
Xavier’s Church, Lavender Bay, last week.
An archbishop and four assistant priests
were engaged tying the usual knot. The bride-
groom was Captain James N. Duhig, of
Brisbane, and the bride Kathleen Mary, eld-
est daughter of J. M. Taylor, who is one
of the Public Service Commissiqners. Arch-
bishop Duhig, uncle of the groom, tripped
specially from Brisbane to perform the
Ceremony; and the Pope sent his blessing
by cable. “Breakfast” at the Wentworth;
then away to Melbourne.
Doris Egerton Jones, who is responsible
for “Peter Piper” and a few other novels,
lias just given her affections into the keeping
of Keg Callaghan, of the Windsor branch,
who is No. X on a gun somewhere on the
cold Front. Doris E., besides being a writer,
can tack LL.B. on to her name, and is an
accomplished person. It was mostly to allow
her to plead at the Bar that the S. Aus.
Parliament squeezed an Act through; and
then she didn't. The local color of her latest
book, “The Cocoanut Planter,” was acquired
on a round trip to Papuan waters, find most
of her fellow passengers figure in the cast.
Mr. and Mrs. John Gale, of Queanbeyan,
had their “diamond” wedding on January 3,
with four generations to join in the congratu-
lations. The old folks have 11 children,. 3!)
grandchildren and 17 great-grandchildren to
hear from. Old John founded the Queanbeyan
Age, which was originally The Goloen Age,
and sat in our Parliament just upon 30
years ago.
At St. Stephen’s Church, Chatswood.
two Saturdays ago. Gertrude Ward, of
Artarmon. was married to Leslie Hamilton
Wright. The bride wore a graceful frock of
ivory crepe de chine, lavishly trimmed with
rose point lace. A court train fell from
the shoulders, and a long embroidered veil was
lent by the bride’s friend Mrs. E. Farleigh.
Bridesmaid Vera Ward was a picture in a
simple frock of white organdie made with
fichu, topped by a pink chi]) hat trimmed
with green leaves and pink tulle. Ernest
Lording "(Melbourne) was next-best man,
and Miss F. Piggott, cousin of the bride,
sang “Until” during the signing of the
register. Subsequently the guests were
entertained at the Wards’ Artarmon resi-
dence, Warrego.
Mountain air claims many visitors this
month. Queensland Judge Chubb and bis
daughters are among them ; and so are Jus-
tice Real and his wife.
Julius Knight is resting arid knitting in
Sydney these days. On the Coogee sand he
comes like a sunburst in pink shirt, purple
tie and green sox; and, later, in sapphire, he
purls in the breakers.
News of note from Maoriland is the
engagement of Nancy Rich, daughter of
Maitland Rich, of Christchurch, to Colville
Smith, from England. It was a sea-catch,
the man being on a convalescing trip. Miss
Rich is a niece of our Professor Ilaswell’s
wife.
Wellington (M.Lv) has done some expen-
sive farewelling to the junior Countess of
Glasgow, who is removing her nursery and
luggage to Kelburn Castle, in Ayrshire, her
seagoine lord having been ordered to another
part of the naval world. The senior Glas-
gow widow was once vice-regal lady in
Maoriland, and her brother, A.D.C. Reggie
llunter-Blair, captured heiress Emily Rhodes,
of Christchurch, younger sister of Ileaton.
M.F.
Melba may go any day to ’Frisco —per-
haps she’s on the water from Honolulu
already. The word was to come from an
overseas friend, Lady Susan Fitzclarenee, a
war-widow seeking solace, perhaps, in a trip
this way as the guest of our Sovereign 'Voice.
If the lady says “Yes” she comes via U.S.A.,
and the Coombe Cottage hostess meets her in
the city where poor old Dan O’Connor niet
the earthquake.
Major Douglas Marks, a Neutral Bay
man, has the picturesque Order of the
White Eagle of Servia to add to his Military
Cross. Before getting into the trouble in
France he had the luck to be of those who
were privileged to be with the Fighting
Thirteenth at tragic Gallipoli. He is a con-
nection, by the way, of Mrs. McDonald, the
sweet songstress who was Kitty Slattery
years ago.
One of the New Year D.S.O.’s decorates
Surgeon-Major Phipps, who abides in Mos-
man in times of peace. Returned men men-
tion his good work on Anzac. Mrs. Phipps
is billeted in Blighty.
Amongst the warmest of the sojers’ friends
are the voluntary cooks in the lied Cross
Kitchens, with Mrs. Holdship as the missus.
They stuck to their ovens over the swelter-
ing holidays, so they’ve earned a Double Plalo
when that decoration is handed out. Near
bv the pickle-and-condiment ladies had to
pull down their sleeves and desert the onion
and the red cabbage. The coal strike hit the
glass-bottle industry, and the hon. cooks and
bottlewashers are snatching a holiday in
consequence.
Lieut. J. E. S. Walsh, killed in France,
was the only son of H. D. Walsh, one of the
Sydney Harbor Trust commissioners. He
was an old boy of The King’s School, and
had just finished the second year of his
engineering course at Sydney Uni. when he
enlisted early last year. Another Sydney
subaltern, F. B. Fitzpatrick, killed in France,
was a grandson of Michael Fitzpatrick, a
one-time politician in the Ma State; his
peace-time job was under the Water and
Sewerage Board. He enlisted as soon as war
broke out, went through Gallipoli from land-
ing to evacuation, and was congratulated by
General Cox for gallantry under shell-fire.
Of three surviving brothers, two are wearing
khaki.
Died at Sydney, G. *F. Vaughan, known
to several generations of Sydney Grammar
boys as “Grumpy.” Vaughan had a first-
class Oxford degree, and was headmaster of
Maryborough (Q.) Grammar School when
dear old Weigall brought him south in 1892
to teach classics at Sydney. In Latin, Greek
and ancient history his pupils were con-
sistent winners of medals at “juniors” and
“seniors” for many years. Vaughan also
started a school “hobbies club,” which did
good work for the boy whose tastes ran to
such things as carpentery and photography
instead of games; and he loitered on the
s ; dewalk of the Inky Way for a while
editing the school magazine.
There’s plenty of sympathy for ex-Chief
Secretary George Black. His wife, after a
health-trip to the other side, and heroic
efforts to keep on smiling, has to give in to
being a very sick woman. She is in a Syd-
ney private hospital.
' Somewhere at the back of the city’s heart
there’s a Medical Mission where the very
poor used to pay “thruppence” for a bottle
of medicine. Its founder was Dr. Julia Fox,
who died the other day. A Sydney ’Varsity
woman, this friend of the needy sick gave
her services in the backwoods of the city for
years, till ill-health forced her to go slow in
her lion, healing work. Wife of Dr. Robert
Fox, of Lidcombe. the late Dr. Julia was a
sister of Mrs. Budden, the Ivillara lady
whose husband is Red Crossing on the other
side.
Manly is getting the usual summer atten-
tion from the squattocracy in the very latest
bathing kit. The Augustus Hooke pair
(Walcha), the A. A. Gordons of Queens-
land, the Phil Tooheys, and the Fenwickes of
Walcha —the place the good polo ponies used
to come from —are amongst the Village’s
surf visitors.
Mrs. Herring, who used to be Florette
Murray Prior, is a proud young matron this
New Year. It brought a D.S.O. to her hus-
band, a Lieut.-Colonel who was in Gal-
lipoli, and is now in France. His wife is
only over the Channel with baby Diana—the
kiddie with the phenomenal ear for music.
Mr. and Mrs. W. E. Shaw and their
daughter have removed from Macquarie-
street to Carnarvon, Bayswater-road. Mrs.
Shaw is staying for the summer at their
seaside home at Austinmer.
Did you notice with what garlands. of
flowers the old year sank into its hiding
place? Never since the days of Dolly
Varden have the Girls’ glad rags been so
botanical. Even the very nicest hats are
stamped all over with miniature gardens.
Saw 'a beauteous young thing the other after-
noon who appeared to be looking out at the
world under a bouquet of tulips. It was the
wide brim of the new umbrella millinery,
stamped with the Dutch blooms as large as
life.
Autumn millinery is already in the air.
That is to say, the willy-wagtail has told
the sparrows on the housetops that silk
beaver hats and immense black velvet head-
gear will blow this way in March. Even
now the heavy-weight velvet covering is seen
on the lately-returned and thankful-to-be-
here Australian woman. Furs —big capes
with high collars —are only hanging back
for the really simmering days of February.
Then they’ll trim the stands in our best
showrooms.
Taffeta silk, the shiny modern relation of
grandma’s glace, has for some reason been a
prime favorite this summer. Possibly its
steady wear and good behavior in not “rag-
ging” has given us its rustle again. The
antimacassar that belonged to the glace
period is, however, still buried with the
horsehair sofa.
The Nurse Cavell calendar is a handsome
local production, designed by Syd Ure Smith ;
and every page of it is a hint that E. B.
Giles, of 4 O’Connell-street, is waiting to
receive even your smallest contribution to
the Rest Home.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

HOW THIN PEOPLE MAY PUT ON
FLESH.
GREAT DISCOVERY BY EMINENT
SPECIALIST.
Judging from the countless preparations
and treatments which are continually being
advertised for the purpose of making thin
people fleshy, developing the arms, neck and
bust, and replacing ugly hollows and angles
by the Soft curved lines of health and beauty,
there are evidently thousands of men and
women who keenly feel their excessive thin-
ness, and it therefore gives us real pleasure
to publish herewith a simple prescription
which, by correcting faulty metabolism and
stimulating the activity of certain sluggish
vital organs quickly produces a marked
transformation in the appearance; the in-
crease in weight frequently averaging from
three to four pounds the first week, and
very rarely less than two. This increase in
weight also carries with it a general im-
provement in the health. Nervousness,
sleeplessness and debility, which nearly
always accompany excessive thinness, all
quickly disappear, dull eyes become bright
and pale cheeks glow with the bloom of
perfect health.
The prescription, which is absolutely
harmless, directs you to take before each
meal a 5-grain tablet of Bitro-phosphate,
such as you may obtain from any chemist,
at a cost of about 2d. a day. Eat all you
want, but chew your food thoroughly.
CAUTION- —Although the above prescrip-
tion is unequalled for relieving nervousness,
sleeplessness and general debility, it should
not, owing to its recognised flesh-growing
properties, be used by anyone who does not
desire to put on flesh. — Advt.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, January 8, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Baronet Rupert Clarke has been laid up
for nearly six months at Salonika. Malaria
is a constant caller on Rupert, and he is
now off to England in a brave endeavor to
drop the acquaintance.
Even if that much-discussed doctor,
Colonel Barrett, misses fire in the British
New Year honor list, he will not face his old
chums with a bare stretch of tunic. The
Sultan of Egypt has handed him a third-
class Order of the Nile. This at least gave
the doctor a chance to get rid of some of his
choked-up eloquence by proposing a vote of
thanks.
ToOrak is talking of the Jim Strachans’
second boy. Twenty-two-year-old Geoff, an
original Anzac, is now a captain. He left
one eye on tlie battlefield, and is having a
set of shrapnel wounds patched up to enable
him to steam back to his proud parents.
Mrs. Jim is a daughter of the late James
Aitken, the fine old Scot who managed Dal-
getys’ for years.
Has the romantic story of the Victorian
soldier-man and the English actress ever
been published? The hero told it casually to
a Melbourne friend months ago while he was
being lionised by the stock-brokers. In his
youthful, stage-struck days he had simply
worshipped Henrietta Watson, the then lead-
ing lady of the J. C. Williamson Dramatic Co.
He dreamed of her as the cleverest and most
fascinating woman in this wide and univer-
sal theatre, the world. When he went from
town to station life, and became a lonely
young dreamer dossing in a hut, he wrote to
her, stating that he could never forget what a
wonderful creature he had thought her, and
how he would like to write again to her if
he might. The actress wrote back saying she
would just love to hear all about his life in
the Way back. In this style they corres-
ponded for quite a long time even after Miss
Watson had left Australia. But he was
beginning to forget all about his early love
when the war came, and off he went to
Egypt.
Wounded in the action where he won the
V.C., our hero (let’s call him Lieut. Thros-
sell, and be done with it) was shipped to
England. As he lay in hospital, feeling very
lonely in his convalescence, he remem-
bered his old-time friendship-on-paper with
Henrietta Watson. One of the London pic-
torials published an illustrated interview
with the lady, and her name stared the sick
soldier in the face. So he wrote to the
actress at the theatre where she was appear-
ing, telling her w r ho he was and how he came
to be in hospital. Presumably Miss Watson
learned from another source that the soldier
was a V.C. man. Anyhow, she came to his
bedside next morning with an armful of
flowers, and made a fuss over the boy who
had interested her with his artless letter-
writing in the last years of last century.
And when her new-found hero was able to
lie moved she took him to her charming house
in the country to stay awhile with her charm-
ing husband and more charming self. And
the long epistles he had written to her when
she didn’t know him from a crow were pro-
duced from a drawer in the lady's private
desk and laughed over by all the family.
Not many soldier men can tell of such a
sweetly-droll romance.
Major Jack Studholme, who lately landed a
D.S.O. in Egypt, owns Coldstream station, in
Canterbury (M.L.). He married a philan-
thropic daughter of the Archbishop of York
the first time, and afterwards annexed a
second mate, daughter of Speaker Charles
Bowen, of Maoriland, who went with hjm to
Pharaoh’s land to do war work thereabouts.
Another Studholme brother, Joe, was once
employed as sort of extra-help in the Hope-
toun household, and married Miss Hersey
Wauchope, a cousin of Lady Jlopetoun. Mrs.
.Toe was a niece of S’African Gen. Wauchope,
too.
Melbourne has welcomed home another of
its V.C.’s—bomber A. L. Reid, son of Mr.
and Mrs. A. L. Reid, of Rutherglen. Besides
his V.C. lie has a D.C.M. and is a King’s
sergeant. His Cross came for repelling
a counter-attack till supports arrived; his
medal was for something extra in a raid on
enemy trenches —both at the Somme. The
surprised young man, as silent about these
things as the rest of them, is an electrical
engineer when he isn’t a soldier, and has
left only 23 years behind him.
Lieut. Neville Fraser, Royal Field Artil-
lery, cables his parents the thrilling news of
his appointment to one of the land Dread-
noughts. He is the first Australian to get
aboard this particular type of lugger. It’s
a life on the ocean wave with the water left
out, by all accounts, as new members of the
crew get exceedingly sea sick as she rolls
into trench and shell crater.
More highly than all the political honors
Mrs. I. H. Moss, who used to be one of the
grand dames of the Women’s National
League, esteems the war medals ol her sons-
in-law—Major Norman Harris, of the
Engineers, who married the elder Moss girl,
Rita; and Acting-Brig.-General C. M. M.
Dare, who is the husband of the younger
daughter, Dot. Dot was the heroine of a
military wedding in Egypt which simply
shone with swords and stars. 'Major Harris
left a job in the engineering dept, of the Vie.
Railways to go away as a captain with the
sth Field Company. He is a Master of
Science, and after distinguishing himself in
sport and learning at Scotch College, finished
up at McGill University, Canada, where
Stephen Leacock, the Canuck humorist and
author of “Literary Lapses,” is a professor.
His Military Cross was won in France. His
brother-in-law, Charlie Dare, won his D.S.O.
in Gallipoli. Charlie was a lieut.-colonel at
27 —possibly the youngest fighting for
Britain. Dick Courtney, whom he succeeded
in that job, described him as a born
soldier who never got rattled. His father is
Monty Dare, managing-director of Brooks,
McGlashan and McHarg, the Flinders-lane
warehousemen.
The Elsternwick Fethers have further
news of elder-son Major Bill. After keeping
his end up with Jim for two years or so,
Major Bill will leave for Australia as soon
as liis wounds permit. He has done his bit.
He will bring a D. 5.0., together with a hole
or two in his handsome head.
Maxwell Homfray Maxwell Gumbleton
(ne Smith), who was turned into a bishop
at St. Paul’s Cathedral on New Year’s Day
as a preliminary to his installation at Bal-
larat, reached Melbourne from London 24
hours previously—on the one morning of the
week when Melbourne is a dull, drab, dreary
city, with lone cabs standing about waiting
a call from the church door. However, the
bishop-elect saw the New Year in with the
Archbishop, so perhaps his impressions im-
proved as the day wore on. The new
gaiters is quite young, not at all hairy, not
particularly distinguished in appearance,
and apparently prepared to regard Australia
as a country that will teach him something.
The Henry Parker knight, who was one
time Chief Justice in Westralia, gets back
to this city for the hot spell, bringing
daughters Dolly and Mildred, who will
squeeze into a Melbourne Mansions flat.
The family has the tripping habit badly.
Justice Holroyd’s widow, having disposed
of Fernacres at St. Ivilda, has crept through
the palings to son-in-law O’Hara Wood’s,
next door. The dame, whose health now
forbids all social duties, was probably the
best-liked matron in society. She had a
knack of straightening out the fiercest cat-
mittee meetings without even scratching the
most refractory cat present. Another Hol-
royd daughter is wife of Van Assche; the
youngest is widow of a young legal light of
the ’9o’s, Charlie Webb-Ware. Two O’Hara
Woods and two Van Assches are in the
trenches: also a grandson-in-law, who got
into khaki soon after his honeymoon with a
Van Assche girleen.
The Bananaland Grahams, who kept the
moths out of the Faulkner-Carse widow’s
South Yarra house whilst she tripped abroad,
have decided to move up to Toorak. where
they will get in a ton of up-to-date tables and
chairs to spread round the parlors at Bronte,
Nesta Clarke’s bricks and mortar, which has
yawned emptily since it was put together
for sister Vanda Hodges and her hubby. The
latter is at war work in the, Fog. Victor
Hood godfathered Bronte, which is named
after his brother, the Duke.
Mrs. Nicholas Fitzgerald has just
returned from England, apparently over-
come by the responsibility of mother-in-law-
ing a duchess. Her son, the irresistible
Majah Percy, you may remember, married
Millicent, the energetic Duchess of Suther-
land. Her daughter-in-law is neck-deep in
all sorts of social activities, and when last
heard from was helping to mend broken and
battered warriors in a French hospital.
The Standish and Bagot at Flemington
saw the first of the amusement tax, and
many nobly paid the backsheesh to the last
desperate fragment of the coinage. One
stout dame had made a valiant effort to
get the 12s. 54d. quite correct. She had it
all in the minor currency, and it looked
as if she had borrowed the cook’s fat-money
or had burgled baby’s money-box. A gent, pro-
bably Scotch, with an unfinished New Year’s
Eve discernible in his eye, was 34d. behind
the advertised charge for admission. He
was an annoyed MacTavish when the ticket
clerk refused to take a tram-ticket and two
poker chips in settlement of the discrepancy.
Some of the Quality adorned the meeting,
but a lot of it was trying to dodge sunburn
and freckles elsewhere. Among the Very
Best present was Prime Minister Hughes,
relentlessly pursued by his faithful Sheppard,
who is shortly off to England and the High
Commissioner’s office. During the afternoon
he kept so close to Billy that he hardly

AVeedon and his wife rambled gracefully on

Several girleens shuddered in their season-
able pink when a sudden rainstorm burst,
and one lady, queerly festooned in green
ribbons, just escaped becoming a pile ot
washing. The deluge happened immediately
before the Steeplechase and almost turned
that event into a water carnival. As on
the final day of the postponed Cup meeting,
numerous sodden flatites charged across the
course to the stand for shelter, and the
expensive crowd was again regaled with the
spectacle of one half-drowned bobby trying
to stem the rush.
Ex-Mint Master Barton and his grey-eyed
wife looked like theatrical stars on a first'
night at their golden wedding the other
Tuesday afternoon. Guests had surrounded
the pair with flowers ; otherwise the affair
bore a war-time aspect. One son and a
son-in-law were killed in the earlier days of
the great trouble, and the elder hope is now
in the Anzac trenches. Just before the
cake was hewn came a load of cabled con-
gratulatory messages from relatives all over
the globe. A brace of benedict sons dashed
down from the sheep country with golden
offerings; another wired love from Cochin
China.
The usual story from Adelaide: —
The Stanleys are due in mid-January as the guests
of the Mount Schank Clarkes. Our own little V.R.
party have gone to Marble Hill for the summer —
Lady Galway left on Wednesday and his Ex. on
the following day. But it isn’t arctic even at Mt.
Lofty.
Herbert Heaton, who has been appointed lecturer
in economics and director of tutorial classes under
the Workers’ Educational scheme, began his career
as lecturer in 1912, when he was assistant to Pro-
fessor W. J. Ashley at Birmingham, and at the
same time had tutorial classes for the students of
the Workers’ Educational Association. He is due in
this State about the middle of March.
The Arthur Hughes couple have bought Lea Lodge,
the Goodmans’ home on Sti'angwavs Terrace, North
Adelaide.
Artist G. A. J. Webb and his wife are leaving
S. Aus. for Melbourne at the end of March. Mr. Webb
has painted most well-known S. Australians in the
20 years that he has lived here. Some of his work
is in the gallery of Federal legislators in Melbourne,
so that’s some kind of an introduction.
South Aus. has a creditable, share in the latest
list of military distinctions—D.S.O. for Brig.-Gen.
Brand and Col. Price Weir; Royal Red Cross for
Matron Graham, who has been connected with the
Adelaide Hospital staff for more than 20 years ;
D.S.O. for Major 11. K. Fry, who was this State’s
Rhodes Scholar in 1909 ; and a nice collection of
Military Crosses. Of these one goes to Captain
Gordon Campbell, who, at Pozieres on July 24-25,
exhibited “great courage and marked ability as a
leader....He exhibited great gallantry, skill and
leadership.” Before he was a soldier Campbell was
a lawyer and well-known cricketer, and toured with
Mayne’s team in 1913. He had been booked as
manager of the Australian Eleven that was being
formed in 1914 to tour S’Afriea, but the war
changed all that and turned a good sportsman into a
soldier whose courage and work has frequently been
mentioned by returned Anzacs. Captain Walter
Fowler-Brownsworth has also won his M.C. His
wife, who was .a Miss Bilks, left for England with
her small son about a month ago.
From London comes news of the marriage of Con-
stance Phillipps, eldest daughter of our Belgian
Consul, to Major H. Greenway, son of the T. J.
Greenways, formerly of Broken Hill.

George Fowler Stewart died' suddenly on Wednes-
day. Born in Scotland he left school to go into
the London office of D. and J. Fowler, and was with
that firm, for a long while as a director, until about
three years ago. George Fowler, one of the found-
ders of the big business, was his uncle. Hence the
name.

“Dicky” Adamson, headmaster of Weslev
was as unusual as usual at the recent
speech-night ’ of his beloved college The
school kid nowadays is often a pasty-faced
long-legged, gangling, pimply object, with
an exteiioi appearance which tells a mourn-
ful tale of a flabby interior. This is the
Adamsonian explanation:—
In too many cases the Saturday afternoon, whether
wet or fine, is spent in the well-breathed atmosohere
ot a picture-theatre, instead of in the open air Too
many evenings are spent in the same way. Easy enter-
tainment, requiring no mental exertion, is becoming
more and more the fashion. The supply of pictures
seems to be almost entirely American, and while the
American stage plays acted have almost invariably
reflected the chivalrous attitude of the better-class
American to his womankind, American picture-plays
seem to cater for an entirely different audience.
Adamson throws an unpleasant picture on
the screen— of a pale, anaemic lad sitting in
germy darkness on a sunny day, probably
crunching peanuts or chewing gum, while
with popping eyes he gloats over the spec-
tacle of a perfect lady, owning an immense
bust and a large, passionate, Cupid’s-bow
mouth,. galloping gaily to destruction in a
succession of dizzy reels. It is a disagree-
able picture.
The Norman Wilsons’ second chick, Mar-
jorie, has been having an anxious time. Her
soldier husband acquired malignant malaria
in Salonika. The little wife at latest was
praying for hubby to be invalided to the
Cold Land as a tonic.
I carelessly planted C. J. Dennis at Tal-
langatta last week, and hasten to return
him to his proper locality. The poet can be
found any day hitting a typewriter on a
tree-stump at Toolangi, a gum-infested
haunt in the neighborhood of Healesville.
The inauguration of the Daylight Saving
scheme gave a shock of alarm to the theatri-
cal and picture showmen. They didn’t get
the expected New Year’s Night crowds, and
they drew sad conclusions from the falling
away. Allan Wilkie was so upset that he
promptly decided to take off “Richard III.”
and revive “As You Like It,” because it is
an out-of doors play with nothing but day-
light in its atmosphere.

THE GIVE-AWAY.
“By Jove, she's a little peach."
“She is. Always peaching on somebody."
“Rab Cilbup”: When a stockman cracks
a whip, what makes the crack? What does the whip
hit? If it hits air, why should the air make a
noise about it, especially that sort of noise? In what
position is the cracker on the whip just before and
at the moment of cracking, straight or curved ?
Finally, why should a whip crack better with a
cracker ?
“McNorth” : North Australian aborigines,
after wrapping the dear departed in tea-tree bark,
usually carry him about for months before deposit-
ing him. I have more than once dropped across a
buck carrying a parcel whose odor betrayed its con-
tents. When burial does eventually happen the
bones are placed in a sitting position in a shallow
hole and lightly covered with sand. For a reason
which I have never been able to fathom, a roughly-
built platform supported by four sticks is built
over the grave. Some dreadful burials happen at
times in the faraway places. When Jim Kane, good
prospector and better white man, threw the seven on
the upper reaches of the Mambare River (Papua),
seven days of rough mountain travel from the coast,
an orthodox coffin was out of the question. So a
cylindrical sheet of bark was obtained and held open
while the deceased’s mates gently placed him in its
smooth interior. The bark was then lashed round
with lawyer-cane and the ends stuffed up with grass.
The sorrowing mates carried their burden to a grave
they had dug on the steep hillside, and found it
half-full of water. Just as they were carefully
getting the coffin into position, with one end over the
grave, the man carrying the far end slipped on a
moss-covered stone. His end of the coffin flew up, the
grass came away off the lower end, and the dead man
slid out and went with a splash into his resting-
place.
# * * *
“Condamine”: There is another timber
that is not valued at more than a fraction of its
worth, seemingly because it is an Australian. This
is the sassafras. It grows plentifully in the deep
valleys amongst the Gippsland mountains, and re-
sembles a pine in shape. The wood takes a good
polish, and has a very agreeable scent, which it never
loses. The leaves are said to possess great medicinal
properties, and campers in sassafras gullies usually
put a few in the billy along with the tea for the
peculiar flavor they give it. Some years ago I read
that the timber had been proved a success for making
bike wheel-rims, but nothing further seems to have
been done. The bark and leaves are carried by
many a prospector in his “port” to keep the contents
healthy and sweet-smelling.
* * * *
“H.V.E.”: The incident occurred on the
South Coast of N. S. Wales, when the virgin forest
teemed with wild life. A rail-splitter killed a
large diamond snake on a rocky hill. Next day
another diamond snake was found coiled on top of
the departed. It was killed also. A few days
afterwards two more of the family were found coiled
about the bodies, and the locals considered that
now the supply of mourners was exhausted. But it
was not so. Three more snakes were killed under
the same circumstances. The diamond usually
deposits its soft-shelled eggs in hollow logs or hol-
lows of trees, and whole families are occasionally
(but rarely) discovered. In the early days, when
the now übiquitous rabbit was not, these harmless
snakes were usually destroyed at sight. Now the
diamond, much depleted in numbers by bush 'fires
and confined in habitat to the south-eastern coast of
Australia, is recognised as a natural enemy of the
rabbit and is therefore spared by sensible people.
The species is very commonly confounded with the
carpet snake, a much more sluggish reptile which
has a far wider range. Scientists aver that the
carpet snake ( Python spilotes variegata ) is a mere
color-variation of the diamond snake (Python
spilotes) ; yet the former attains a far greater size.

“Eddyson”: Gus. Petberick’s wife was a
short, -round, black-eyed, energetic woman who spent
all the time she could spare in strenuous quest of
Petherick. Gus had made a modest competence on
one of the rushes, and was now settled at Clunes in
a weatherboard house on an abrupt hill, with a
slope of garden before the front door and a sudden
rise at the back. Here he spent his days in tillage,
his chief object in life being evasion of the keen
edge of mother Petherick's tongue. He would sit
by the hour like a rabbit, hidden among the thick
gooseberry bushes, merely to avoid arguments. “She
nags wakin’ ’n’ she nags sleepin’,” Petherick said,
with a sort of bovine patience. “The worst iv it
is there ain’t never any variety in her naggin’. What
she starts with in the mornin’ she keeps up all day,
’n’, Gor bless us all, it does get a bit wearisome ’long
about tea-time. All the interest I get out iv it ever
is wonderin’ first thing what she’s a-goin’ t’ start
with. If she wakes first I don’t even get a chanst
t’ wonder.” One afternoon when I looked in on
the old boy I found him, pick in hand, working at
the hill almost at his back door, where it had been
necessary to cut the slope away to provide egress.
Gus had opened out a tunnel. “Coin’ t’ gouge out a
bit iv a dairy,” he said. “It’s the missus’s sermon
fer the day.” Petherick worked at that tunnel like
an old Trojan. He put it in about sft., opened
out to a chamber, and provided a little furniture and,
a wood stove, with the pipe directed out through
a bore-hole in the roof. At the entrance he rigged
a thick door of double slabs packed with sawdust.
He had created a substantial prison-house. It was
finished when I next called, and Petherick popped
his head out of the dairy door and beckoned me
in. He closed the door after me, and propped it.
A bright fire was crackling in the stove. The day
was wet and cold. Gus chuckled and rubbed his
hands. “Cosy, ain’t she?” he said. Then, nodding
towards the house: “ ’Er text fer the day is leavin’
muddy boots under the bed.” He spread his hands
to the fire and winked knowingly. “Matter iv fact,”
he whispered, “this ain’t no dairy. ’Tween you ’n’
me, boy, a married man’s gotter have a bit iv a
place where he can be by hisself odd times, ’n’ in
the winter months he can’t always be creepin’ under
the gooseb’ry bushes—-not comfortable, he can’t.”
•He chuckled again and winked, and. . . .the chamber
was filling with smoke! “Good lor’, look et that,”
wailed Petherick. “She’s bin ’n’ stopped up me flue !”
I dragged Gus into the open, almost a done man.
A week later I met him in the town. He was look-
ing very dour. “Hello, Petherick!” I said, “been
taking your ease in the dairy lately?” “No,” he
answered sullenly. “I take my ease in the bedroom
now —on the best bed, with me blessed boots on.
The worm’s turned. When it comes t’ chokin’, worms
turn. I ain’t unreasonable, mind yeh; I’m ez
reasonable a minded man as there is. 'I lets her nag
fer one hour a day, fair V square, but when she
goes over that I claps her in the dairy.” He hobbled
on a few steps, turned and shook his stick fiercely.
“I’ve ’eard all I wanter ’ear about snorin’ fer one
day,” said he. “She’s dam’ well in there now!”
“The Hum” : At W. L. Brockman’s home-
stead on the Warren River (W.A.) there is a pear-
tree that should easily establish a record for Aus-
tralia. It is 60 years old, and the butt is Bft. 4in.
in circumference! It has yielded over two tons of
fruit in one season, and is still bearing, though the
fruit is not so juicy as it was in the younger days
of the shrub.

“H.V.E.”: John Chinaman, includes
strange edibles in his menu. During a severe drought
some new-born c'alves were killed at a station on
which a party of Chinese fencing contractors were
at work, and one of the bodies was well salted with
strychnine to entrap the wary warrigal. Later on
the dingo-poisoners found that the bait had dis-
appeared, and inquiry at the Celestials’ camp elicited
the fact that the poisoned meat was already well
on its way to conversion : into a most savory stew.
He spoilt a boshter funeral. The savory but abnor-
mally tough mutton-fish, is another favorite article of
diet with Celestials ; so is the savage and extraordi-
narily venturesome green eel common on rocky fish-
ing grounds. Writer once, in endeavoring to detach
a mutton-fish from a rock, tore the shell off its
back, leaving only a bare mass of white flesh. But
before the prize could be landed a big green eel
dashed in and grabbed the choice morsel and wriggled
off in triumph and a cloud of language.

“Gobba^Gobba”: Re queer coffins. The
North Queensland Meat Export Co. got an advt. when
Jack Cusack died on Woodlark Island (Papua). No
other available timber being handy, a wooden over-
coat was constructed of meat cases. The top read:
“Prime N.Q.M.E. Oxford Sausage.”
“MarloO”: When the North Coast
(N.S.W.) Co. was about to erect a butter-factory at
South Murwillumbah a divining-rod manipulator was
called in to locate fresh water, the Tweed there
being salt. What he got on to turned out to be an
underground river which flows beneath the co.’s
land and empties itself into the Tweed at a known
spot beneath water-level. If a rod expert gets
a rush of energy some day he might trace
the subterranean stream to its source. Nobody so
far has any idea where it is. Anybody know of a
similar river on the coastal side of the Dividing
- Range?

KOWHAI FLOWERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the kowhais flower in Maoriland, there are
always heavy rains. The floods which follow are
called the “Kowhai floods.”
Long ago, ere you and I were
Crowing ’neath our cradle covers,
It is said, the Earth and Sky were
Warm and radiant royal lovers.
Ere the rough winds rushed betweeri them
Or the bold sun mocked their faces
(Oh ! the white stars strove to screen them
From the Spy of Dawn, who paces,
Silent-footed, down the shadows
With a lidded eye that glowers),
Earth and Sky met in the meadows
When there were no kowhai flowers.
By the winds the tale was carried,
While the night seas roared with laughter-
“ Earth and Sky have wooed and married,
Ask the gods what cometh after.”
But the gods sent swords from Heaven,
Sabres stained with awful slaughters,
And the sky afar was driven
While Earth wept beside her waters.
From her grief and love impassioned,
Brooding in her ravished bowers,
In her loneliness, she fashioned
Golden-hearted kowhai flowers.
Came her maidens, filled with wonder,
Far and wide the blooms they scattered,
Where the mountains mocked the thunder,
Where the spray the rainbow flattered.
There were rustlings in the rivers
That are Earth and Sky's true daughters,
Where the gleam of starlight quivers
There were whisperings of waters.
* From the clouds came brimming measure,
Tears that fell in silver showers ;
“Let us touch,” they said, “Earth’s treasure ;
Let us kiss her kowhai flowers.”
Once a year the grey sky hovers
Over Earth’s green-mantled shoulder
As might one, most loved of lovers,
Boldness show, yet might be bolder.
Happy Earth spreads wealth of color
Over hill and plain and gully ;
Blooms no darkling wind makes duller,
Golden clusters nought can sully.
Oh ! In gallant, country fashion
Comes her lover at the hour,
And the waters wake to passion,
For the kowhais are in flower.
MAORI LAND.
WILL LAWSON.

Mitchell climbs the Blue Mountains
on HIGH GEAR at 234 miles per gallon
Think of it —a climb of 3495 feet up the Blue
Mountains to Jenolan Caves from Sydney and back
on December 17, -by a stock Mitchell Six, with a
full load of five passengers and the gear-lever sealed
in high gear!
What a trying test of the mechanism of the
Mitchell! And what a triumph—what an indis-
putable proof of the unequalled efficiency of the
Mitchell!
This remarkable climb on high gear has not been
paralleled in Australia —possibly not in the world.
The benzine consumption for such an ordeal was
extremely low, averaging 23J miles to the gallon.
Union Red Line Benzine was used for the trip.
The most precipitous roads of the Mountains—
the sharply-ascending Lapstone Hill, the Whipcord
Hill, the Mount Victoria Pass, the five-mile hill
up from Jenolan Caves to Mount Victoria were
all conquered with ease on the high gear.
There was no laboring when the Mitchell entered
on the steepest grades—no harsh grating of gear-
wheels —no throbbing, vibrating protest like that of
most other cars when ascending hills.
Silently, smoothly, powerfully, the Mitchell went
up—and the mountains melted away before it.
Light Six; with Simms’ magneto ignition ; 120-
inch wheelbase ; 50-inch cantilever springs ; engine-
driven tyre-pump ; complete equipment.
Write for full particulars of'this master car.

“Barcoo” : The most difficult undertaking
of the overlander is the swimming of his herd over
swollen rivers. Great experience, care and intre-
pidity must be shown by the man in charge, for
once allow the string of beasts to break and they
will begin to “ring” in the water. Then loss is
SEEKING EXPERT OPINION.
THE NIPPER: “Say, Mister, do I look orl-
right? There’s a tart o’ mine cornin’
round the corner.”
certain unless the stockman and a good horse can
come at the mob from the down-side and straighten
their heads towards the landing. Special care is
displayed by the man who knows his profession to
pick a landing-place where the banks slope into the
stream. Big losses have been made by greenhorns
not taking this precaution. A few years ago one
of them put 2000 bullocks into water where the
get-out had an abrupt bank. All went well till the
mob began to take off in big batches, and half the
animals were in the wide Georgiana and jambed
against the opposite bank. The stockmen failed to
break them and 280 were drowned. An experienced
drover would have strung his cattle into the water.
Sheep will swim quite as well as cattle, but if in
running water great difficulty is experienced in keep-
ing them up stream. It is necessary to have two
or three strong swimmers to keep their heads straight.
“Pick” : Fantods or not, it is nevertheless
a fixed belief among drovers and teamsters that at
Hewitt’s Crossing, on the Bogan, camped animals
invariably stampede at night. Travelling mobs
scatter madly without apparent cause; even stodgy
old working bullocks have been known to bolt for
miles, and dogs will come cowering and whimpering
into the tents. This is so widely accepted that, not-
withstanding its excellent watering facilities, the
Crossing is shunned as a camping ground. By the
way, the ford got its name from a long-past
tragedy. In the ’6o’s a traveller named Hewitt, who
was camped there with mates, arose at night to head
back his wandering horses. He never returned, and
after days of waiting and searching his mates went
on. Many months later the skeleton of Hewitt was
found with a rusty butcher’s knife between the ribs.
“Ruby Gill” : Among the abos. every man
strove to express himself in the same way—-by
making a corroboree. The weird, barbaric Woygen
loved the gorgeous pageant of a sunset. As the clouds
rolled back, turning from gold to vivid purple, and
deepening to blood-red, he
saw the trophies of a mighty
hunt whereon the tribes might
feast for a week, and his cor-
roboree was made. He who
was funmaker, uproarious
laugh-maker and low comedian
was called Coralbyn. It is
little to our credit that bis
masterpiece should -have been
a farce depicting the drinking
habits of the whites from the
moment when, with a “Here’s
to you” the glasses clinked till
the boozed lay senseless on the
road. A well-known figure was
Mad Finigan, who won for
himself a unique position in
aboriginal drama. To the
skill of the playwright he
added that of the scenic artist,
carving from bark life-like
figures of birds and animals,
and with clay from the creeks
giving them a semblance of
their natural colors. These
he would dispose, as those who
saw his corroborees have testi-
fied, in a most artistic manner.
Georgie Quartpot—no one le-
members his real name now
was the poet of the N.S.W.
Coast tribes. His plots were
laid in the fields of romance.
He loved a lover with the
rest of the world, and all
his heroes were “sentimental
blokes.” He watched the
parent birds and their young,
and what he learned from
their tender idylls he strove
to apply to human life. In
his Betrothal Corroboree the
males and females faced each
other. Then one of the latter
wandered forth, and as she
went she trolled a melody.
This was the sign that her
man was to follow her. She
was ready for love, but in ro
other way was she prepared to
show it. Sweet as some flower
of night, hers was the right
to be wooed and won. Who
could deny it, when she had
before her the example set by the ladies of the
feathered world? When the wooer with his wooing
had gone far enough, as archly as any civilised
flapper she squeezed his hand! Then together they
sang:—-
Mah rahl go wung-i nung yah
Yengora bil-go ni-oh !
(You and I together will go away!) And beyond
the firelight, their voices wafting back on the night
breeze, they would pass into the dark.
* * * *
“H.V.E.”: “Brook-lime,” or hyssop
{ffratiola), is not uncommonly found growing in
the beds of creeks and watercourses in the temperate
climates of Australia. Of this bushmen concoct a
remedy calculated to electrify the dead. The plant
(leaves and stalks) is boiled, and the liquor mixed
with Epsom salts. Dose, a wineglassful or more.
The herb was once used by the chemist, but has
long since ceased to be “official” in the pharma-
copoeia, like many other “simples.” In appearance
brook-lime resembles watercress, but the stems are
longer, and it doesn’t choke the stream. After tak-
ing a sample the patient becomes conscious of a
species of internal earthquake, and his unwonted
activity surprises his family and friends. A blissful
calm, however, succeeds the storm, and he emerges
from the ordeal in that temper of body and mind
which lures men into lending £1 notes to absolute
and utterly untrustworthy strangers.
“Jobber” : The best way I know to deal
with bracken and blackberry is to brush ’em, burn
’em, plough like blazes and whip in a crop of spuds
before the curses get their second wind.
“Haka” : The pakeha doesn’t have all his
own way on the shearing board in Maoriland. The
other day at the Puha Tiko Tiko shed, up Poverty
Bay way, eight of the native breed undressed 1801
jumbucks in eight hours 30 minutes, and they
weren’t barebelly ewes either, but mostly big
wrinkly wethers. Here is the interesting tally:
Heriwine Rapana, 270 ; Peta Rapana, 268; Wainui
Teepa, 249 ; Huru Stevens, 232 ; Hata Stevens, 212 ;
Hoera K*ewa, 196; and te trummer and his mate,
374 between them.

“Gham”: 'Re shearing tallies (B.
10/11/’l6). On Hazelwood station, which has just
cut out, eight shearers on one day put through 1707
crossbred ewes and lambs. Average, 213. Scoring:
Ringer Gosden, 252; Fox, 250; Hamilton, 242;
Carter, 214 ; Jim Arentz, 200 ; Jack Arentz, 188
Letounder, 182; Nash, 179. Is this a N. S.'Wales
or Australian record? At Gulgo shed, Forbes, nine
men robbed 1835 complete suits. The three best
were Norton 254, Larkin 232, and Gates 231. Abo-
liars may have heard of Kimo shed, Gundagai, where
19 men put through 14,600 in a week, and in one
day 3470.

“Beeritus”: Allow me to prescribe for
broken knees and sore shoulders in horses, etc. Mix
one-eighth of kerosene, seven-eighths of lard and a
teaspoonful of salt. Wash the wound daily with a
weak solution of Condy’s and apply the ointment.
No scar will remain and the hair will grow as
thickly as ever. I used it on a racehorse which
had been, galloped on in a race. He was stripped
from hock to pastern, leaving the tendon exposed.
Six weeks' treatment effected a complete cure, leav-
ing no marks whatever.

“Bob D.”: A chap named Bergen, who
leased the once-infamous Berrima Gaol after its
demise as a penal establishment, and converted it
into a factory, used the flesh of the bunny for a
potted meat, sold the skins, converted the entrails
into garden manure, pounded the bones into fowl
food and fertiliser, and sent the tails to Germany
to be used as the manes and tails of wooden horses
for the kids. He and his family worked the plant.
“Starlight’s” cell was the storeroom; the kitchen
was in the old gallo.ws shed.

“JIGGER.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When a fellow’s thoughts get dwelling on the trite
fact that Time flies—
When he joys in looking backward, I suppose it
signifies
That his Youth’s somewhere behind him, and he’s
middle-aged at last;
For all Youth’s domain’s the Future, while the Old
have but the Past!
These and other sad reflections, shared alike by sage
and fool,
Haunt me most when I remember smoking “jigger”
after school.
It was in the timber country, and we had some
miles to go,
Past old farms and belts of forest where the winter
fogs hung low,
Paddling barefoot through the puddles, through the
slush that used to freeze
Where the road lay all the winter in the shadow of
the trees.
When the brief day turned to twilight, as its last
wan hours were spent,
We would warm our reddened noses puffing “jigger”
as we went!
Where a cutting in the hillside left the roots exposed
to view
Of the dead stumps by the roadside, there our
“jigger” harvest “grew.”
Straight and dry, the blackbutt rootlets were the
better sort by far,
And a piece the right dimensions made a natural
cigar.
Pround we were of simple triumphs as we did the
“drawback” then,
Blowing clouds like seasoned smokers —for we
reckoned we were men!
When our careful mothers, hunting through our
clothes for hole or tear,
Came across a “jigger” bumper—well, we rued it
then and there ;
And we didn’t like the system our respective fathers
had,
Which allowed us to be walloped by the other
fellow’s Dad !
(“If you ketch young Billy smokin’,” mine, would
say to Jimmy Dean’s,
“Just you ‘hide’ him good and proper, just you give
the beggar beans!”)
But parental disapproval didn’t keep us from our
bent,
And the fear of “Yaller jarnders” turned out just,
as impotent.
I have smoked a lot since those days, pipe, cigar
and cigarette,
But my mem’ry lingers fondly with that school-time
smoking yet.
Then it was a Great Adventure, fraught with pen-
alties and pain ;
To a Promised Land it lured us, and its lure was
not in vain.
Then ’twas new, and ’twas forbidden; nowadays it’s
not the same—-
It is just a daily habit use makes commonplace and
tame.
And at times I find I’m wishing I were back again
once more
In the old, barefooted school-days, smoking “jigger”
as of yore!
N.S.W.
ULOOLA.

Business, Bobbery, etc.
If wo were only as careful about humans
as we are about liens, what a race we would
be ! And how we would make the feathers
fly! Nine-tenths of the real job of the
poultry-farmer is to find out which hens are
slackers ; for that means not only a saving
in the feed bill, since the dilatory ladies
promptly go to the pot, hut a lessening of
the chances of producing a poor strain. In
the up-to-date hennery the daily performance
of every lady who is suspected of being good
enough to be kept as a mother is posted
upon her door; and a solemn deputation
waited the other day on Adamson, Vic. Min-
ister for White Leghorns, and urged him,
before the Kaiser finds out what we are up
to, to put every fowl in the public tests at
Burnley under the same personal, super-
vision. At present the ladies are in com-
munes of six, and a German spy among
them may do her bit by laying nothing
and spoiling the record of the whole
commune. She may even lead five respect-
able, hard-working females to entirely unde-
served execution. While Adamson is think-
ing about this reform a dozen ribald people
are laughing. Yet this paper has a firm con-
viction that if the rest of Australia worked
on as sound lines as its hen-growers it would
be a bit mor'e for’ard than it is.

“Edo.”: Bulletin statements(3o/11/’l6)
about the waste of stores, etc., at Canberra,
call for the remark that • if minor officials
were less under superiors’ thumbs, there
would be less cheating and blundering in
Government departments. In most Govern-
ment-owned concerns documents relating to
expenditure of money and distribution of
stores have to be countersigned by a junior
after this man’s boss has certified to their
correctness. That system is a rotten one.
I helped run a Westralian Outback concern
at which transactions were fairly large.
When my signature was needed I was never
asked for it till about five minutes before
mail-time. Eventually I discovered that my
boss’s groceries, furniture, etc., were being
invoiced to Government as tar, cement, bags
of lime and the like. Next time he wanted
my signature in haste, I declined to hurry,
on the ground that I wanted to make sure
that his arithmetic was quite correct. We
missed that mail, and I really believe that
for a while there was no wholesale cheating,
excepting in the pay-sheet, on which he put
three relatives down for time and a half
when they did not loaf the full hours. How-
ever, a couple of months later, while I was
busv thinking out schemes for checking
things, a visiting inspector smilingly told me
that, as the department had too many men,
and I was not on the permanent staff, it
was necessary to retire me. I knew that the
inspector, had not given me the real reason
for what was really my dismissal; but what
could I do? Not a doubt of it some yarn had
been trumped up about laziness or ineffici-
ency on my part. Yet I was powerless.
-* * * *
“H.M.” : Very few are acquainted with the
colloquial geography of Sydney. Poverty
Point used to be the corner of Castlereagh
and King streets, and though it has had fluc-
tuations has been for years opposite the
Criteron Theatre, Park and Pitt streets. The
Great Australian Bight is on the other side
of George-street between the Town Hall and
Market-street. The Bay of Biscay is George-
street between Ilaymarket and the Railway,
where there are many pubberies. The Water
Police Court is the Cooler. Among pubs
there is the Gluepot (King’s Head), the
Bobby (Oxford, because of the lobbying done
there), and the Quorum (Assembly, handy
to Parliament, which is raided when a
quorum cannot be obtained elsewhere). The
’Ole in the Wall, W.vnyard-lane, is the title
of a portion of another pub which Oscar
Asclie’s mother kept for years. There used
to be another Hole in the Wall in Castle-
reagh-street, a pie and coffee emporium, now
defunct. The best-known pie-palace in the
city to-day is the Cafe de Fairfax, standing
by the S. M. Herald. #
“Marloo”: When we learn how to make
gloves the flying-fox pest will cease to cause
us anxiety. I have seen the skin of the
winged rat tanned and dressed by Mr.
Broomham, of Willoughby, Sydney, and he
says that the leather is just the thing for
making gloves, having the required strength
and stretch. A pair of wings makes 5ft. of
leather, and as the cheapest of fine leathers
brings Is. 3d. a square foot each fruit-thief
would be worth at least Gs. 3d. Even if we
couldn’t make hand-wraps in Australia we
could start getting together the raw material
against the time when the war will cease.
Then we could send it to the foreigner to
make up, cart the finished article back again,
and forward the cheque wherewith he could
found more cannons to play with. r lhen
we’d all be good friends again.
“Hopbooze”: A recent cattle-lifting ven-
ture. when 600 head were removed, and re-
captured after three weeks’ hard going, was
a feat of great daring. The raiders made
west, with the obvious intention of follow-
ing the track of the North-West monsoons,
their objective being the cattle country north
of Adelaide. On the monsoonal storms sweep-
ing down from the Territory the raiders’
chance of success depended. It was a great
gamble, and recalls an equally audacious
theft brought off in the late ’7o’s. Two way-
faring stockmen put to Mr. Chapman, mana-
ger of Auburn, Burnett district, a proposal
for recovering “scrubbers” running in the
outlying ranges. Their suggestion was that
the station should buy from them the car-
cases, the vendors of the meat retaining the
hides, then, hmvever, of little value, a penny
a lh. being the recognised return. The
original object of the men may have been
honest, but when confronted with the alter-
natives of rounding up the wild beasts or the
comparatively domesticated animals on an
adjoining holding, their good intentions went
overboard. The Auburn storekeeper accepted
the beef in good faith, and all went well
until the return of the head-stockman of the
other property from a holiday. He wanted
to know the meaning of the rounding-up, and
by the time he had finished pushing his in-
quiries a couple of enterprising lads were
commencing a 10 years’ rumination on their
hard luck. # #
“Bad Australian”: Competition for South
Aiherican shipping business between Uncle
Sam and Nippon grows keener every day.
Sam has just put another fleet of freighters
in commission, and the Japs., not feeling
content with their West Coast business, now
want a cut at that of the Bast. The repre-
sentative of a Japanese shipping firm is at
present discussing preliminaries with the
Brazilian Government. The Japs, are also
trying to arrange an exchange of produce
between the two countries. This is all very
gratifying to the South American, as competi-
tion is certain to make a drop in freights,
but it is calculated to cause considerable
uneasiness among British shipping people,
who have managed hitherto to get away with
the heavy end of South American business
at highly remunerative rates.
* * * *
It isn’t always the gas meter. A family
living in a home secluded among fir-trees at
Elwood (Vic.) has had several serious dis-
agreements with the Gas Co. because of the
exceeding rapidity of the meter. 1 his pro-
gressive apparatus went right ahead with the
same passionate zeal whether the family was
at home or abroad. The family has a week-
end resort at Macedon, but the impossibility
of convincing the meter that gas was not
consumed in the Saturday-to-Monday inter-
vals provoked irritable interviews in the Gas
Co.’s office, and led to a trap being set for
the meter. The family led the meter to assume
that it had gone to Macedon. Then it re-
turned unexpectedly at 11 p.m., and found
gas flaring in three rooms, and a couple ot
tramps making the best use of the furniture.
At one Victorian seaside holiday resort
this Christinas a curious coincidence came
to light. At three different points, on three
different occasions, a small boy had been
gallantly rescued from drowning. The coinci-
dence lay in the fact that each time some
portion of the discarded wardrobe of the
brave rescuers had been stolen. Those
articles of wearing apparel that had not
been stolen had been rifled of money and
all articles of value. At this stage it was
decided to make a collection of the rescued
boys, and forthwith the coincidence deep-
ened. He was only one hoy. But the man
who had given him a shilling to fall into
the sea had already absented himself with
marked circumspection.
The Port Melbourne constable had ex-
plained the spotting of the impudent chicken
thief, and his capture red-handed in the
fowlhouse, with the stolen goods upon him.
“What did lie say?” asked the Court. “I
said ‘Them’s not your chickens,’ ” said the
officer, “and, said he, disgusted-like: ‘Why
didn’t you say so before I put ’em in the
bag?’ ”
* * * *
“Will A.” : The recent I.W.W. trial and
the long grass of a good year have caused
Cocky to develop an imagination; so, when
two swaaMes unrolled their blankets in
Temora (N.S.W.) and proclaimed them-
selves members of the rebellious clan, a
local fire-insurance agent was bombarded
with applications for policies. The anti-
climax came two days later when one of
the wild-eyed ones strolled into the agent s
office and coolly demanded a commission on
the new business. The agent burst into
hot speech and drove him out into the street,
where the Terror of the Paddocks drew Uis
pipe and set fire to some inferior tobacco.
“Jay Jay”: Before the war the greater
part of the world’s supply of potash came
from Germany. That supply having ceased,
the price of the article has soared skywards.
Yet our wool contains 5 per cent, and more
of potash salt. A small amount of this
is now being saved, but what is wrong
with recovering the lot? As the stuff is
used not only for chemical and manufactur-
ing industries but for artificial manures as
well, the demand here is bound to increase
enormously. Another hitherto neglected source
of potash' is the alunite got from alum
won at Mt. Buladelah (N.S.W.). Small
quantities of alunite have been exported to
Britain, but a mountain of it is available.
Have we got enough faith and enterprise to
move that mountain?

One of the Yankee experts who have come
over to help the Amalgamated Zinc get its
new electrolytic plant going has a yarn about
a flotation plant working at a mine in one
of the western States and surrounded by
desert and sage-brush. The Australian euca-
lyptus oil mostly used in the flotation pro-
cess came very expensive, so the manager
started experimenting with the useless-look-
ing sage-brush and found that he could get
all the oil he wanted out of it with a plant of
his own.
OUT OF IT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At a gathering of officers of the Department of
Oopazootics presentations were made to Messrs. Alpha
and Omega, who have retired on superannuation.—
Frequent news item.
Out of it!
Clear of the breadth and the length of it!
Out of the grind and the drift and the strength of
it!
Out of it —out of it all!
Done with it!
No more tO’ do with the work of it!
Cod! Old bones ache with the wrench and the jerk
of it! \
Out of it —done with it all!
Out of it!
Better young hands should have play in it.
We? We are old: we have ended our day in it.
Finished! We’re out of it all.
Out of it!
Shouldered away to the west of it,
Out of the wrack aud the rhythm and rest of it—
Out! Not a place in it all!
Out of it!
Only the ghosts of dead selves in it,
Wraiths that go haunting the orderly shelves of it.
Wraiths! We are dead to it all!
Out of it!
Yet it goes on without stop in it.
As to the sea is the tiniest drop in it,
So are we—out of it all!
N.S.W.
INVER G.

Don’t Have Grey Hair.
A simple, old-fashioned, home-made recipe will
make the greyness disappear.
Grey hair is - often a serious handicap to
both men and women while still in the prime
of life. Hair dyes are not advisable because
they are always obvious, inconvenient and
often downright injurious. Few people know
that a very simple formula, which is easily
made up at home, will turn the hair back to
a natural color in a perfectly harmless
manner. You have, only to get two ounces
of tammalite concentrate from your chemist
and mix it with three ounces of bay rum to
prove this. Apply this simple and_ harmless
lotion for a few nights to the hair with a
small sponge and the greyness will gradually
disappear. The lotion is neither sticky nor
greasy, and has been proved over and over
again for generations past by those in posses-
sion of the formula. — Advt.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Judge Moule, of Victoria, after hearing a
case in connection with the fitting out of
Australian transports, said that the whole
business was a public scandal, and that the
pilfering and stealing was appalling. This
scandal was well known to the press right
from the outset; but for two years, at any
rate, the censorship forbade any reference to
it, just as it forbade any reference to a hun-
HORRIBLE POSSIBILITY.
“It was a dreadful thing for us when Kit-
chener died!”
“Oh, I dunno! It mighter bin Charley
Chaplin!”
dred other scandals, on the simple ground
that ft was better that the public should oe
robbed than that it should think the Defence
Department was incompetent. The Minis-
ters who thus used the censorship to hide
their own gross incapacity are still in office.
Pearce is one; Jensen is the other.
It is just as well to recall now, when it
is in the balance whether Hughes shall
go to England or not, that last time he
went there he committed this country to
an expenditure of about £2,000,000 for cargo
boats and had not the faintest shadow of
Parliamentary authority for doing it. It
seems a pretty fair hint to Australia to see
that the same swollen-headed little man
doesn’t take any sort of national cheque-
book with him next time he swanks abroad.
By all means let us collar German trade.
At any rate, let us talk about it. The fac-
tories of N. S. Wales alone decreased by 70
during the year for which figures were
lately issued, and by 147 since 1913. There
were actually 238 fewer people em-
ployed. But wages naid showed an increase
of two-thirds of a million, the average for
males having increased about £lO per annum
to £l4O 11s. Id., and for women by some 14s.
per annum to £53 12s. The average wage
for both combined went up nearly £6 to
£llB 11s. Bd. The candle is burning brightly
at both ends. . *

Our overlapping and cumbersome system
of government threatens to have another in-
teresting result. The six States being still
“sovereign States,” unaffected by the Com-
monwealth in most matters, some at least
of their Premiers believe that they have just
as much right at an Imperial Conference as
the Prime Minister; and this view of the
matter is likely to be submitted shortly—if
it hasn’t been submitted already.
Weird story from a Patagonian paper :
Loose Jaw, Monday.—Two little boys named
Willie Hughes and Joey Cook went fishing yesterday,
but having only one line between them quarrelled
about the bait to be used. At last Willie tried to
put Joey on a hook and in the subsequent struggle
both kids fell into the water. They were rescued
in an exhausted condition and have been ordered to
bed. Several youngsters have come to grief trying
to catch fish at the same spot in the same way.
The Queensland Health Act Amendment
Bill contains the following provision:—
If a person suffering from venereal disease in an
infectious stage marries without giving information
to the other party, such an act shall be ground for
a decree of nullity of marriage if action is begun
within 12 months after marriage and without
resumption of marital intercourse after discovery.
The children of the marriage, if any, shall not be
illegitimate.
This is a clumsy, way of half-repairing a
wrong that there is a simple way of prevent-
ing. It would be equally sensible to abstain
from fixing a load-line in steamers, or pro-
viding that a vessel should carry a certifi-
cated captain, and instead giving the pas-
senger the right to be put ashore in a
launch if, when 20 miles ouf, he discovered
that the ark was overloaded or the captain
really a confectioner. The obvious plan, if a
clean union is aimed at, is to require a clean
bill of health from both parties before
marriage.
During the conscription campaign the
Censor’s Department succeeded in producing
one or two “instructions” which, though
quite valueless except in so far as they pro-
vided the men who elaborated them with a
job, were freely used from anti-conscription
platforms, and used, too, with telling effect.
The same absurd department has now suc-
ceeded in issuing an “instruction” which,
being barked from every corner, as all these
instructions soon are, has caused wide and
totally unnecessary alarm and some dis-
tress. This sort of thing has happened so
often that a principal result of the Depart-
ment. may now be quite fairly said to be the
parentage of vague rumors and the sugges-
tion of mythical disasters.
Sydney had another shower of forged £5
Commonwealth notes during the holidays,
and it was followed by the wholesale issue
of instructions in business places not to
accept proffered fivers. A Commonwealth
note is legal tender, just the same as a
sovereign; and this being so the tiadei
makes ' the very reasonable demand that
Commonwealth notes should be made less
easv to forge. At present they offer prac-
tically no difficulties to a skilled tradesman,
and this is not a fair thing to business
people who are compelled to accept them
or suffer serious consequences. Moreover,
when, as happened recently, a man proffers
a fiver in three or four places and finds that
he can’t pav a debt with it, he is liable to
try to get rid of it for something less than
100 s • and that will be the beginning ot
trouble. In any case, it is liable to cause
alarm. Not every man who has a fo-noie
that he can’t freely pass knows the real
reason. If the Commonwealth Treasury
wants to prevent stupid scares it ought to
talk to its printer.
“Carfax” addresses the congregation: —-
The London Imperialists have handed over a
spare drum to old Bill Massey, of Maoriland, and
if the inhabitants of the Windy Isles don’t burst
out erving it will be because they cannot stop
laughing. Bill addressing the Empire about organic
union is the weirdest thing to happen since Hughes
bolted. Someone may have pumped a few notions
on the subject into him since he reached the Smoke,
but that Bill has even a rudimentary notion of
what he. is talking about is incredible. Yet these
platitudinous remarks of his are being cabled round
the earth as if they were the quintessence of wisdom
and Bill was a modern Solomon. Poor, plain, dull
old Bill, a Cocky in a silk hat, babbling while the
world is in flames about somebody else’s dream
and wishing to heaven he was back home oiating
over the new culvert at Maungapomokomoto! But
it might be worse. While Bill has the floor the
effusive Ward has a chair. And Ward’s last scheme
for consolidating the Empire was to raise a stupen-
dous loan to start with.
The University of Hobart hands over to
Adelaide Herbert Heaton, the brilliant young
Yorkshireman —Master of Arts at Leeds -and
Master of Commerce at Birmingham—who
has been in the Island as lecturer for two
years. ' It falls to Heaton to carry out the
democratising of universities planned by the
Workers’ Educational Association. The S.
Aus. Government lias been converted to the
scheme, and gives a grant in aid; the Adel-
aide University takes up the project with
enthusiasm ; and Heaton accordingly becomes
a lecturer there in economics, with a special
eye on the new classes.

From the report of a Melbourne inquest:
The Coroner commented on such a man as
deceased having been at large. The disease was in
a most contagious state, yet he had gone about
among his fellows for weeks.
But as the deceased had not been suffering
from smallpox or leprosy or plague, he
carried his horror around with impunity,
and for all the Law knows handed on the
loathsomeness to other people. He only had
synhilis. And as 70 individuals with
syphilis were recently liberated from the
training-camps in Victoria alone, the dis-
tribution is probably going on as well as
could be expected. Notification? Absurd!
