With more forests in proportion to area
than any other Australian State, and with
magnificent native softwoods as well as
hardwoods, Tasmania imported from Puget
Sound and other distant places 12J million
superficial feet of timber in 1915, not to
mention furniture and other dressed wood
of the value of £31,000. The im-
portations would have been larger if
the war had not made it nearly
impossible to get stuff ferried from
Scandinavia. The Government has
the importing mania as badly as any
of its taxpayers. When it wanted
joists for its new buildings in Hobart
it used Oregon pine. All the while
the island’s young male population
drifts away, leaving women and old
men to pay the bill for the imports;
and Government after Government
wonders why. Since 1900 Tasmania
has been adding to its population at
the rate of only 1.19 per cent. i*?r
annum ; Canada, from which the fool-
ish little island buys the timber that
it has so much of itself, has been add-
ing 3.28 per cent.
“Kooka”: A few months back I
bought some mouse-traps, and a city
trader tried to load me with a line
bearing a characteristic Australian
brand but with the old “Made in Ger-
many” label in the least conspicuous
part. I have just noticed it, with
the identical trade-mark, exhibited for
sale in Sydney with the legend now
“Made in U.S.A.” To keep out goods
made in enemy countries and finished
or shipped from neutral countries, or
made there at enemy-owned and con-
trolled factories, the only plan is the imposi-
tion of a general high tariff, charging the
same against goods coming from neutral
countries as on goods coming direct from the
land of Kultur.
VICTORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
During 1916 the army and navy were victorious in
all theatres on land and water, and will also be
victorious in 1917. —The Kaiser.
The year was glorious. Up and down we strode,
Clanking our spurs, and making matters hum.
No matter what was standing in the road
It trembled at the shout, “The Germans come!”
It trembled, broke and faded quite away,
And horse and gun we trod it underfoot.
In every theatre the choicest play
Was the vast comedy of Prussian Boot.
You mention Verdun. Well, that small affair
Was hardly up to standard, we admit.
We lost some ground and many soldiers there,
But what a glory on our helmets lit
When we withdrew from shot-torn fort and hill,
Keeping our flag still flying further back !
The tireless heart, the fixed unchanging will
Are victory, and these we never lack.
Jutland ? Well, there we fought a stubborn fight—
A glorious victory, shall history say.
We showed that we could bark as well as bite,
As with unsilenced tongues we ran away.
We won—who doubts it ?—though they held the field
And chased us to our ports with shot and shell.
We dare not try again, but did not yield.
Oh, yes, we won, although they gave us hell.
The Somme? There triumph treads on triumph’s
heels.
Outmatched we may have been and yielded ground ;
But we were busy making peace appeals,
And our defence, though shaken, still is sound.
They pushed us farther every time they tried,
And still can push us back, we greatly fear ;
But in a blaze of glory Germans died,
And there are others left to die next year.
On, on, ye brave! The final scenes await
Your high, courageous valor day by day.
Remember, but don't sing your hymn of hate
While footling Wilson tries to call the play.
Our arms must win. (Lord, how those women wail!)
Keeping fighting 1 (How their bombs and guns
increase!)
It’s always victory until we fail;
And there is still a sporting chance of peace.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

A Solid Wall of Good Will
Nothing has ever disturbed the
demand tor this car.
Not for a month, not for a week,
not for a single day.
Having only one car to build,
Dodge Brothers have been able to
build it better and better.
But in the sense of radical
changes, it is the same car tb-day
it was when they designed it.
And still the demand goes on
growing greater and greater.
No outside conditions, no condi-
tions inside the industry, seem to
slow it up a particle.
The people want the car more
intensely at this moment than they
ever wanted it before.
This in the face of the fact that
production is at the highest peak
it has ever reached.
And in the face of the further
fact that the sales —in less than
two years—have been more than
14,000,000 pounds.
You can find out why this is so
from what people are saying about
the car all around you.
For months and months they
talked particularly of its perform-
ance.
Now, you. will hear even greater
emphasis laid on its economy.
You will hear that with thou-
sands of cars in use, Dodge Bro-
thers dealers have practically no
use for repair parts.
That rumor is true. Accidents
are the chief source of demand for
Dodge renair parts.
You will hear that the cost of
maintenance is practically nil.
This, too, is true in thousands
of cases.
The petrol consumption is unusually low.
The tyre mileage is unusually high.
Dodge Brothers. Detroit
Messrs. Dalgety & Company, Ltd.. Sydney; Messrs. Canada Cycle
and Motor Co., Ltd., Melbourne; Messrs. Cheney Motor Company,
Ltd., Adelaide; Messrs. Winterbottom Motor Co., Ltd., Perth;
Messrs. Dalgety & Company, Ltd., Brisbane; Messrs. Co-opera-
tive Motors, Ltd., Hobart; Messrs. Magnus, Sanderson & Co.,
Ltd., Wellington; Mr. George Henning, Auckland; Mr. Newton
King, New Plymouth; Messrs. HaTTkes, Ltd., Christchurch;
Messrs. Cossens & Black, Ltd., Dunedin; Mr. P. H. Vickery,
Invercargill.
You will hear that the car con-
sumes an unusually small amount
of petrol and oil.
This is literally true —of every
Dodge Brothers car, under all con-
ditions.
You will hear that it is very
difficult to find Dodge Brothers
cars for sale at second-hand any-
where.
You will also hear that the
second-hand price is only a little
lower than the first price.
Both of these things are true—
not in one city, nor one State, nor
one country, but all over the
world.
You do not hear people talking
about the price of the car.
But you do hear them talking
everywhere about the kind of a car
it is.
In short, wherever you find a
Dodge Brothers' car, you will find
it surrounded by friends.
You will find it running, from
morning to night, in an atmos-
phere of goodwill.
No illwill anywhere, goodwill
everywhere—goodwill that grows
out of its good work.
No one knows the value of this
goodwill any better than Dodge
Brothers know it.
No one counts more on it, nor
works harder to deserve it.
This is your guarantee when you
buy one of Dodge Brothers cars —
the great goodwill that all the
owners feel toward it and the high
value which Dodge Brothers place
on that goodwill.

KELLERMANN SEASON
SPORTING
NOTIONS
“0.K.”: Tatt.’s sweeps will never get
purged of blackmail influences as long as the press
continues to treat the tactics of ticket-sweating
owners as being all in the game. For instance, Mel-
bourne Argus, referring to the late scratching of
three horses for the Carrington Stakes- at Randwick,
remarked that “Tasmanian considerations probably
had something to do with their withdrawal, as the
horses were in good condition." The same journal
said of a winner: “It transpires that the owner of the
horse received a substantial consideration from a
Tasmanian source.” The effect of such allusions to
sweep “inducements” is to make it appear that no
owner can be expected to run his horse unless he has
been bribed by the person who has happened to draw
it as a chance in Tatt.’s. No mention is made of
the facts (1) that an owner lias no sort of moral
claim to a share in the good luck of a sweep in-
vestor; or (2) that if he makes a claim with the
threat of scratching his horse if the claim be ignored
he is a blackmailer; or (3) that his threat is a
mere bluff if he knows his horse to be a “good
thing,” in which case he is practically certain to
run it and back it, because he can’t afford to miss
a good opportunity; or, (4) that no “consideration”
could be demanded if Tatt.’s did not acquaint black-
mailing owners with the identity of the ticket-
holders whose luck they want to sweat, or if the
fulfilment of a voluntary promise of sweep “con-
sideration” were not guaranteed by Tatt.’s. Tatt.,
under these conditions, is in the position of a stake-
holder who sanctions and even invites the issue of
garnishee orders on the stake. It is preposterous
that any State Government should legalise horse-
racing lotteries for the benefit of the class of owners
who levy a heavy tax on the big prizes. By this
system every purchaser of a sweep ticket is robbed
of a large percentage of his potential good luck.
The Government, the sweep-promoter and the daily
press are all winking at the robbery. And the
absurdity'of the evil is in the simplicity of the
remedy which is never applied. Whv permit Tatt.’s
sweep to continue as a “consultation” associated-
with horse-racing when it could be conducted with
more virtue and less trouble, as a State lottery, quite
unconnected with turf roguery?
* * * *
Melbourne’s principal betting club —the
Victoria—is just 40 years old. The late J. S.
Butters was founder of the place where “market
prices” used to be gathered for the press ere the
publication of bookmakers’ odds on future events
was forbidden by law. There were, of course,
bookies’ haunts in Melbourne long before Butters
founded the Victoria, but they never had the “tone”
and authority of the new establishment. One of the
original Victoria Club identities died recently. Old
Macnaughton, the tall, inoffensive citizen in question,
ceased to be a bookmaker some 20 years ago, and
had never been on a racecourse since. Mac was
uniquely unconventional among men of the gambling
class. On the racecourse in his younger days he was
a Shatter —a lone-hand worker —quiet, reserved and
seemingly' mild in disposition. After his retirement
he. took up his abode at the Stock Exchange Club,
ancl remained a hatter to his last day. He was a
bachelor, with no surviving relatives, save two
ancient maiden sisters in Scotland. His only, chum
wrts Scotch whisky—-after sundown, but never earlier.
In whisky he found a congenial spirit, and in its
company he loved to talk. Two bottles a night was
hip allowance for many years. He seemed to thrive
on it. When his tongue threw off its restraint Mac
could talk to himself if there was nobody else to
converse with. Two pretty women who slept in the
next room to Mac at a country boarding-house were
disturbed by his nocturnal soliloquy'. At last they
decided to knock at the door. Even that didn’t
strike the old gentleman dumb. He merely cried
“Come in, my dears,” and went on yarning with
himself. The kindliness of Mac’s nature was beauti-
fully expressed in that “Come in, my dears!”
* * * *
Punters beware! Some small-fry pad-
dock bookmakers have felt a pinch. At least two
of them took the knock at Randwick on New Year’s
Day. Several backers went round to collect next
day but missed getting a sight of the Tommies they
were searching for. No harm would be done by
the A.J.C. holding a stability examination. The
practice seems to be to find out how a man stands as
far as pelf is concerned, grant a license and allow
him to go on until he hits something. This paper
would suggest that the A.J.C. should have a look
at existing bank balances and revise the list of
licenses.
One of the most patiently-managed gees
in the racing business is the natty seven-year-old
Aleconner, who won the Standish Handicap at Flem-
jngton on New Year’s Day. Aleconner is right at
the top of the second class, and he stays better than
most of Malster’s get. The best wins to his credit
are the Epsom at Randwick three years ago, and the
Williamstown Cup (11 furlongs) in November, 1914,
since when he had never made a mark
until he brought off the little coup in the Standish
flutter. During his long but not too arduous career
he has -scored seven or eight wins and several seconds
and thirds in races worth more than £5OO, handi-
caps or minor yv.f.a. events. His distance is any-
thing from six to 11 furlongs, though he once went
so far as to be runner-up in the Place Handicap (12
furlongs) at A.J.C. Autumn Meeting. Now that
he is back to his best, or almost his best, form, the
chestnut son of Malster and La France may bring a
little more grist to the Baillieu mill. He is appar-
ently as sound as ever he was.
*■ * ■* *
Foil, the nag which annexed the Canter-
bury Park (Sydney) Handicap on Saturday', has a
liking for that course. Several times there he has
beaten everything in events over a mile and a
quarter. But this time he had to be ridden right
out to beat Tomlinson’s imported Medley. The
people behind Foil—said to be a well-known firm
of Sydney penciUers—usually throw in for a big
win when their animal is feeling well. Foil was
put up for auction the other day, but as the bidding
stopped at £lOO he did not change his address.
Strange how often horses after being- offered to the
public come out almost immediately and win races!
■X- * * *
The Pommy keeps on doing it. The
latest to do the trick is Urney, who secured Canter-
bury Park Stewards Mile oh Saturday, One day
recently when Urney' was having a go at Randwick
in novice company he was expected to do some-
thing, and the shrewd heads made long-price invest-
ments. As. it didn’t come off the- theory that he
would do the trick on Saturday when opposed by
horses like La Balfour, Blackacre and others was not
popular. Urney’s win must have been balm to the
wounds of trainer Tomlinson, whose Medley had
been touched off earlier in the afternoon. This
latest importation to’ win came from Ireland, where
he landed a couple of races. lie may be heard
from later.

The Stead brothers of M.L. are running
a dead-heat. On New Year’s Day the Melbourne Cup
winner Sasanof was liberated in the Great Northern
Derby to beat The Toff, owned by Gerald, a brothel
of the young man who invaded Australia in the
Springtime. Sasanoff won. Later at the sariie
meeting the mokes came together again, under a
lower scale of weights, in the Auckland Plate, a mile
and a half, each with 7st. 111 b. This time The Toff
struck the line first, and the Flemington gelding
arrived next. The Derby was run two-fifths quicker
than the Plate. #

tralia a nag that was a champion 2-year-old—
Whakawatea. Age, 73.
“Mujik”: The punter who says “Well,
I’ve backed a couple of winners, and am going to
knock off,” is usually the same lunatic who keeps on
backin’ ’em when they run last. The only difference
in his madness is that it has broken out in a fresh
place. It is the wise punter who doesn't button-up
when he is on a winner or two, and who when he
strikes a losing sequence uses his money to take a
holiday. These things go in runs, for, after all has
been said about Brains, it is still true that Luck is a
lively animal. Winners come to all men like cards
at poker, or the main at hazards ; and what sort of
an ass would he be who turned up throwing 2 to 1
chances when he had done it twice? No one can
consistently win money backing horses on the level,
but the punter who refuses to follow the goddess
when she beckons him on is going to be a loser all
his life. Take your shirt ar.d boots off when your
Luck is in. Leap into a motor-car and drive 175
miles without a stop and stay there for six months
when Luck frowns.

“J.H.M.” : About the blackboard tote (B.
4/l/’l7). A Sydney “sport” was running one at a
pony meeting up Tamworth way, and was having a
rough time. In the “big” race the favorite led
coming up the straight, and the clerk used up two
pieces of chalk marking down crosses. Then Foot-
light and Nancy challenged the favorite and passed
him, to be pipped off by a local horse by a short
head. The clerk nearly sprained his wrist, and when
some of the locals came to collect they were stag-
gered by the number that had backed the local
unknown. One chap said, “There wasn’t so manv on
when I backed it.” “Garn,” replied the blaekhoarder.
“Didn’t you see Murphy, the publican, send out a
bucketful of half-crowns for it just before the start?”
This staggered the crowd for a while ; then one asked
how much they would get. “Wait till I reckon it
out,” said the" rook, looking round to get a clear
run. “Well,” he said, “you all owe me threepence
each.” Then he flew.
“Puzzle”: What should the stewards do
when a horse throws its rider after winning, and
bolts into the unknown? Technically, he can’t weigh
in ; but when it happened at the Fowler’s Gap meet-
ing, behind Broken Hill, a conference resulted in
his being allowed till 10 p.m. to recover his saddle.
And it was duly found, about five miles down the
road ; but how the horse got rid of it, and where he
has got to since, are mysteries yet to be solved.
“Toby C.”: That grassfed prad of
“Nomad’s” (B. 28/12/’l6) was no waster to cover
108 miles in 12 hours, but wait a minute. In the
early ’nineties C. C. Price, at that time school-
master at Taralga, wagered £5O to ride his grass-
fed mare 100 miles in daylight. The wager accepted,
Price and his mustang hopped "
at 6 a.m., taking a course through Goulburn to
Braidwood, 60 miles. They fed up at Braidwood, and
returned to Tarlo, another 40 miles. The wager
was won with three hours of daylight still in
hand. Price then headed his mount for Taralga and
was home and fed up again before dusk —120 miles.
Two days before the wager was made the mare was
offered for £2O. After the event she changed hands
for £4O. Pedigree—by Tatto out of some good
Australian mare.
“Ghan” : “Yarrum” enters his horse Black
Tommy (B. 30/11/’l6) for the endurance test.
Well, it will have to lick these. On Christmas Eve,
1915, a 14.2 black pony belonging to one Burke
left McCarron’s Shamrock Hotel, Grahamstown
(N.S.W.), at 7 a.m., pulling an ordinary road sulky
plus Burke and holiday luggage, and was stabled at
Kiandra at 3 p.m. That’s 75 miles. Included in
the trip was the ero sing of Talbingo Mountain.
Another Italian named Casey tried to follow, but
long before reaching the mountain had to transfer
his belongings to his fellow countryman and lag
behind. This pony is still earning his oats around
Grahamstown, and as his owner is anxious for a
holiday he will wager the expenses (full fares) of
the same journey with any unbeliever, and says his
pony will do it twice in a week. Another trip,
also by a 14.2, driven by Jack Dooley and draw-
ing three in a sulky, left Narrandera early morning,
journeying to Wagga, and thence to King’s Hotel
at Belmore —95 miles in the day. The horse that
did this was from an Angler mare and was bred by
Bob Falder.

“Carfax”; I don’t want to call anyone
names, but if “J.J.C.” (B. 28/12/’l6) isn’t fairly
close to the limit with his man from Gympie who
walked 67 miles one day and back over his tracks
the next, then he ought to copyright the story. The
world’s record for a walk of 100 miles, held by an
athlete of exceptional endurance, is 21 hours and
42min. Yet this cove who could do 134 miles inside
two days is said to have thought nothing of it. The
thing is absolutely impossible except by a man of
amazing vitality, trained to a hair, and on a road
kept clear of horses, bikes, waggons and motor-cars.
To cover 40 miles on the boot within 24 hours is a
big thing to do. I know because I did it once, and
three men who had walked. over hills after sheep for
years knocked up on the journey. It took me 19
hours, with two sleeps and five feeds, and you can
take it as gospel that the last 10 were as long as
the final stage from the Everlasting City to Eternity.
“Quinton”: The cricketing deeds of
Norman Dodds, killed in France, entitled him to a
place in the 1909 team for England. He played
with the first against the next XI, at a “trial' match
in Melbourne, which, it was stated, was to decide
whether he or N. S. Wales Gorry should go aboard
as second to keeper Carter. Dodds “kept brilliantly
and emphasised his claims by scoring 80 not out in
the first’s only innings. He was a natural wicket-
keeper. But the tour was neither for him nor for
Gorry. Carkeek, of Victoria, was chosen.
When war broke out the Midland Hunt
Club (Tas.) had 65 members. But there are only three
left in the Speck now —the other 62 enlisted. And
seven of them, at least, will never come back.
I)an Gee (Middle Harbor), whose 61
against Glebe was the highest score in Sydney pre-
miership cricket on Saturday, handled the bat when
many of the present cricket generation were in
knickerbockers. ’Way back in 1903 he and \ic
Trumper put up 423 in 2hr. 40min. for Paddington
against Redfern, and this effort still adorns the
record of batting feats. Trumpet’s share was 335.
R. Long worth’s 100yds. in 59 2-ssec. from
scratch at Rose Bay (Sydney) on Saturday, is the
best-time swim in Sydney this season. Another
of the family filled second place front the 4sec. mark.
Brother Billy, at present in khaki, who has won
most of the Australian swimming championships,
recently picked up an “Empire khaki championship”
in England; but, owing to the time for the event
being made much slower than his best in Aus-
tralia, the English sporting press has been making
nasty remarks about Australian watches. Appar-
ently military boots have slowed William down.
At the Sydney New Year sports W. J.
O’Reilly, one of the city’s bulkiest policemen, put
the 161 b. weight 36ft. 9in. and threw the hammer
88ft. lOin. Every ounce of O’Reilly was also put
beneath the caber, but the weapon fell an inch short.
There is age in O’Reilly’s whiskers now; but he is
still an impressive circumstance when the removal
of ironmongery is under discussion.
Desdemona’s sailing double in Port
Jackson on Saturday draws attention to the fact that
there have been an unusual number of doubles pulled
off this season. Each Saturday’s race is run in two
heats, and the first half-dozen in each contest meet in
the final. Although the heat winners would natu-
rally be expected to have the best chance in the final,
the’ usual thing has been for a third boat to bolt
home in front. Lately, however, heat headers have
been final winners also —a disastrous thing for the
double-event books.
A motor-car recently fled over half a mile
of Southport (Q.) beach in 21sec.—85 miles an hour.
This is whooped to be the fastest half-mile recorded
outside U.S.A., and the crowd that made the tear-
away hopes to do even better in more favorable
weather conditions. The beach gives an 11-mile
straight course. It would be an ideal place to intern
road hogs.
* * * *
“Petifi”: When 1 honoring the heroes of
Verdun pass one salute to the motor-car. For it was
there —4000 of it —and how grandly it did its little
bit we're only now beginning to learn. When, last
February, the Germans loosed their big punch the
nearest rail-head from which the French could
push up supplies was at Bar le Due, 30 miles away,
and connected with Verdun by only one roadway that
was capable of carrying heavy motor traffic. Along
that single track M’sieur set moving an endless chain
of 4000 autos., which maintained a uniform distance
from each other of 20 yards, and travelled at a
uniform rate of one yard per second. While railways
were being plunked down with ding-dong urgency,
2000 munition-and-supply cars moved doggedly for-
ward along one side of that highway, and 2000,
empty or carrying wounded, moved doggedly back to
the base along the other side. One command only
was inexorable: the chain mustn’t stop. Did a
driver find his machine getting sick he must ditch it
instantly—a repairing gang of mechanics would
physic it as soon as may be. Did the perpetual
traffic wear up the road surface a watchful workman
darted in between two machines, hastily deposited in
the rut a shovelful of soft stone, and sprang clear.
The wheels of the autos, themselves crushed the new
material into good serviceable macadam. So up and
down —up and down —dav and night—for three long
months went the Four Thousand. Salute !

For the full enjoyment of a torrid New
Year’s Day in Sydney it is best to have been born
in Argyllshire and had six months’ hard training
before the gaietv arrives. Then you get into your
breeks and your kilt and sporran and philabeg, and
your hot, exhausting stockings, decorate yersel’ wi’
a cairngorm and snuffbox, tie your leg to a dagger,
put on a few blankets, and proceed to the gathering
o’ the clans. The subsequent happenings consist of
tossing cabers, cracking jokes wi’ Sandy, consuming
usquebaugh wi’ McTavish and McKenzie and Tonald
McDuff, and dancing flings and the Reel o Tulloch
and the Seann Truibheas, and giving frantic cheers
for thb lads wi’ the pipes. Eh, mon, it’s grand, it’s
grand, to be in Sydney toun o' New Year and have
nothing to do but rush round wi’ a red face and
broil all day in the sun. They had a gathering of
Hielan’men at the Show Grounds on New Year Mon-
day, and it looked about the most violent form of
exercise the writer has seen since he encountered all
the Jocks of Otago hogmanaying at Dunedin.
*****
They still mix their jobs in the North.
At a recent race-meeting up there the sanitary man
and his'missus did the catering for the afternoon-
tea. As the last crumb was being shaken out of
the last lap the caterer yawned. “Well, I ain’t sorry
it’s over,” he said to his patronesses. “I been on
the cart three nights this week, and cutting sand-
wiches all the morning, and I’m that dog-tired I’ll
have to let things go to-night.”

The long-standing dispute between Tommy
Uren and Fred Kay about the middleweight champion-
ship was argued again before Chief .JustieeArthur Scott
at Sydney Stadium on Saturday. Thomas produced
fresh evidence of a weighty nature, and when cross-
examining the other party made him shift his ground
with great agilitv. Kay met the charges against him
in the fifth round with an argumentative glove which
threatened serious injury to ITren’s reputation, though,
generally sneaking, he relied chiefly on his remote-
ness. But it was mostly Uren, and Scott gave judg-
ment accordingly. How long Uren can hang on to
the title remains to be seen.
Jack Jannese called in at Brisbane last
week-end to discuss a punch on the nose with
Albert Locke. Albert knew all about it at the end
of 20 rounds, the young man from Melbourne im-
pressing it on him with abundant left hooks to the
dial. N.B. —Reward is offered to anyone returning
a gold tooth to Mr. Albert Locke.
“Spot” : When Harry Reeve (out in Aus-
tralia last year) won the cruiser-weight champion-
ship in London lately someone remarked that the
conflict was terribly slow and gave further proof
that “physical drill and military training have a
deleterious effect on boxers.” Well, Reeve was not
much at any time, and the word “deleterious” may
be badly chosen; but it can be taken as true that
physical drill is, as a rule, unsuitable for boxers,
runners and swimmers. At any rate, this is so where the
“drill” aims at building up hard muscle, as it
usually does in the army and navy. And that is
why a first-class boxer nyely conies out of the army
or navy. Without speed a boxer is on the slide
to start with, and no speed is possible when the
muscles are turned into granite. Thore are exercises
that will develop the legs enormously if persisted in ;
but did anyone ever see a pair of gymnasium legs
getting over the grass very rapidly? That physical
culture within limits makes for improvement in the
average man’s health is certain, but that it is much
good to a youth who wants to take a degree as a
Master of Biff is very doubtful. We will
see later on, if the Fates are good, how much Car-
pentier and Billy Longworth have benefited by it.
“Fiat Lux” : This is my experience with
a pull-through stuck fast in a .30'3 rifle. Finding a
ramrod useless, I loaded up with a blank cartridge,
tied a string to the trigger and pulled. Nothing
happened. On opening the breech it shot back, and
the empty shell whizzed past my ear. Another try
was* successful in shifting the obstruction. But
don’t experiment with a shotgun. My brother, out
after parrots, accidentally stuck the muzzle of his
gun into soft mud while crossing a creek. There was
a burst barrel lying alongside a stunned yfltoigster
10 minutes later. •
“Bang”: When a piece of rag got situck
in my .44 Winchester Thompson advised me to “stick
a cartridge into her and let her go.” I did. The
result was a burst barrel and gladness that I had tied
50 yards of fishing-line to the trigger and hidden
behind a stump. On another occasion I tried the
same dodge with a .22 Winchester. The bullet
stuck hard up against the rag. Whereupon I took
the bullet out of another cartridge and reinforced
the charge with about a dram of powder. It
shifted the obstruction all right; but the recoil split
the stock, and the rifle thereafter wouldn’t shoot worth
tuppence.
* * * *
“C.H.S.”: Re obstructions in barrels of
firearms. I have seen two which were removed with
powder, and in each case it was disastrous for the
weapon. A Binghi had a hard wad stuck fast about
half-way down a breech-loading gun, and attempted
to blow it out with a cartridge. Result: Both
barrels broken off short at the point Of obstruction,
Binghi laid on his back, a piece of his ear and most
of his hair missing. No. 2: A volunteer had a. pull-
through fast in his rifle, and fired it out with a ball
cartridge, fastening the rifle to a fence and attaching
a string to the trigger. The pull-through evas
ejected, but where it had been the barrel was bellied
to about twice its proper size for a comb of inches.
Only the good-quality, steel in the barrel saved it
from a fracture.

‘“Bad Australian” : Does anyone care to
hear about another shooting experience? I was out
after kangaroos one morning just before sunrise and
had sneaked to the top of the red-box ridge Overlook-
ing the “lick” on the swamp, where the marsupials
were usually found about that time o’ day. A couple
.of does and a joey were feeding about 200 yards
lower- down the flat. I picked the biggest of the
bunch and fired at the widest part of the hind-
quarters. This is the only place for a sure shot at a
marsupial, as you get a leg or the pelvis or backbone,
the most vulnerable parts, except, of course, the heart
or brain, which no experienced kangaroo-shooter ever
aims at. On skinning the animal I found that the
Martini bullet had gone right through. Just. a.s I had
worked my foot in over the loin preparatory to
giving the final heave which lifts the skin to ears
and tail tip, I happened to look across the flat, and
there, about 25 yards away, lay an “old man” with
the blood flowing from a wound between flank' and
thigh. When I skinned and dissected him I found
that a flake of lead about the size of a shilling had
cut an artery and was partly embedded in one of the
joints of his backbone. On Sherlockholmsing the
matter I found the mark of the bullet on the moss-
covered granite outcron immediately behind the
corpse of-the first animal. The bullet had struck, the
rock, and flying off killed the “old man.”

“Cynicus”: An Anzac, whose discharge
showed two years and 97 days’ service,
reckons Brisbane Medical Board is too cau-
tious. This Billjim was at Gallipoli from
start to finish, but before he could get to
Fiance lost a thumb and a finger, a piece
of shrapnel plugged him within an inch of
liis heart, one leg was badly shattered, and
his stomach got more hard tack than it
could digest. Altogether, he had 18 wounds.
The Board would not accept him for further
service. “What the ’s wrong with me?”
he asked. “We don’t encourage gluttons,”
was all the reply he got. Yet the Antis
would have let him go willingly. In fact, he
was the Antis' strongest argument when they
enthusiastically declared that the volunteer
system had not failed.
“Six-and-Eight”: Re the outrageous cost
of passing on Commonwealth inscribed war
stock in a small deceased estate, “W.A.M.”
(B. 28/12/’lt>) begs the issue. I did not
question the correctness of his figures if the
executor paid a solicitor to do the work. I
only pointed out that in N. S. Wales there
is no necessity to employ gent, one, that
any agent of the curator will do what is
necessary for less than £l. If no provisions
exist in Queensland similar to those in force
in the Ma State, “W.A.M.” may be right.
If, on the contrary, similar provisions do
exist, then “W.A.M.’s” dead friend was unfor-
tunate in appointing an executor who chose
an expensive course where a cheap one
existed. “W.A.M.’s” suggestion that provi-
sion be made for small amounts to be paid
on the executor making a statutory declara-
tion is unexceptionable; but if in Queens-
land it be still practically necessary to em-
ploy a solicitor, how about first getting the
beam out of his own State’s eye? Copying
the N. S. Wales provisions ought not to b?
a job beyond the Ryan Government’s powers
of statesmanship.
In from the sea comes another little
troopship print, The Anesthetic, which
is unlike its contemporaries in that it in-
cludes a “ladies’ letter” “written at ruinous
expense by a dress expert from one of the
howitzer batteries.” For example :
My Dear Blitherette, —At the concert on Friday
night I noticed Sister T in a ravishing confec-
tion of grey madapolam, the rather rococo lines
of which were relieved by a scarlet over-piece or
top-hamper of priceless old shantung, the whole
being- set off, not to say emphasised, by a massive
toque of the purest white calico, or again it may
have been (but how about a fresh sentence?). Near
her was that indefatigable squire of dames, Captain
A . lie was wearing his second-best uniform,
the one that the Quartermaster made for him on
the Peninsula.
But it’s a pretty dangerous thing, all the
same, for a soldier to poke borak at
woman’s passion for dress details. Nothing
that a fashion-writer ever wrote goes so
minutely into details as some orders and
regulations relating to the millinery of Billy
Anzac and his moderately-good friend
Atkins.
“Oliver” : There is a pearl of great price
in the conscientious report of the Vic. De-
partment for Neglected Children. “The
infants that pass through the depot are,
perhaps the most difficult problem,” says the
visiting committee, “as many are practi-
cally at death’s door when they enter, and
it is impossible in so many cases to pull
them through .”
Geelong favors the brand which produces
the fighting drunk. A par. in the local
Advertiser gives publicity to a storekeeper's
“revised price list of wines and spirits for
the New Year holidays,” and proceeds tri-
umphantly : “A special line is old Scotch
cream whisky at 4s. 3d. and Bs. per battle.”
“Itan”: The flying-fox in Bananaland is
rapidly changing its habits. When the vermin
descended on the Queensland coast in millions
last summer they did not bother about spend-
ing the night in secluded spots in the moun-
tain scrubs as they used to. They just hung-
up in the nearest tree, even if it happened
to be in the city street. Since then they
have grown still bolder, and great numbers
did not return to northern breeding haunts
this year, but just hung about the fleshpots.
While waiting for the fruit to ripen they
have been filling in nights squealing and
fighting over- the scanty “figs” available on
the Moreton Bay trees in the parks.
“J.J.C.” : No need for O’Malley’s culvert
to be laid through Torres, because just as
much water falls on the Queensland side as
in Papua. Geraldton, now Innisfail, has
done 6ft. per month on several occasions,
and falls of lOin. and over per day are quite
common. The greatest washing-day ever re-
corded in Bananaland was at Crohamhurst,
on February 2, 1893, when 35|in. was
emptied down. Some of the islands off the
coast exceed even this, _ but there are no
official records of their doings.
A recent shuffle makes Captain Patrick,
lately in charge of the Melbourne excursion
boat Weeroona, “captain supervisor” of his
co.’s fleet. This grizzled old commander,
who must know Port Phillip as most men
know their homes, used to do some remark-
ably accurate weather forecasting. Once a
wharfside joker told him of a suburban
barber who, despite his everlasting indoor
job, was as slick as any mariner in his
prophecies. Patrick later on called at the
little shop for a haircut and, during the
usual yarn, casually asked the razor-artist
how he worked out his forecasts. “Oh,”
said the barber carelessly, “there’s some
captain-cove called Patrick who does a bit
of forecastin’. He’s always wrong, so, what-
ever he says, I says the opposite.” It was a
minute or two before Patrick realised that
he had been decoyed ashore.
“A.R.”: Met a merry little Cockney ves-
terday with a tale to tell. He had been >n
guard duty a few days before, and a fussv
English sergeant, who hadn’t been long
enough at the game to know the unwritten
rules, ordered Private Thomas London, alone
and unaided, to clear a room of noisy Aus-
tralians, Private London’s sole weapon for
the suicide consisting of a stick. “The
blinky sergeant, ’e says to me,. ‘London,’
he says, ‘you clear out these blighters!’ I
ups and says, ‘What! me go in amongst
them wild Horstralians! Not ’arf! Them
chaps can punch wuss nor’ ’orses kick. You
tell them to git out! ’Corse ’e clinked me
for it, but Pd rather be clinked nor have a
funeral.”

Having passed through the firing-line in
Egypt and Gallipoli without being once
punched, a blue 2d. ticket issued by the Mel-
bourne Tramway Co. has returned to Vic-
toria. It left Australia tucked in the uni-
form of Sergeant S. A. Round, in October,
1914. It saw the Sphinx and the Pyramids,
and covered on desert marches about 700
penny sections. It went into action near
Ismailia, and rolled back the first Turkish
attack. Later, it landed at Anzac, survived
the occupation and evacuation, and spent
Christmas, 1915, in Egypt. Thence it passed
to London, where tram- and ’bus-conductors
refused to honor it. It saw its owner
through an appendicitis operation, and when
Round joined the Army Pay Corps he sent it
back to his mother, who keeps it as a
souvenir.
Young George Patterson, a Melbourne
advertising agent who joined the A.M.C.
early in the war, writes of food at the
Front: —
We were supposed to have just plain “guts,”
rather than stomachs, when the Egyptian days were
on; but only a High Commissioner would complain
of the menu here in France. The quantity, quality
and variety are wonderful. Personally the worst
food I’ve met on active service was home in Aus-
tralia. Of course it may have improved now, for 15
months have gone by. It needed to, badly.

BANANA LAND
This is the third time Clermont has been
devastated within white-men’s days! In the
first big flood (1864) Charles Hardie Buza-
cott, the veteran journalist, had to forsake
journalism and take refuge in a tree.
Toowong Anglicans kept up Christmas by
baptising a' Jew. The Hebrew convert is
so rare a bird that the event was given all
possible publicity. Headed by a cross-bearer
and choir, the congregation marched down
to the river at the old St. Lucia ferry, and
while a lady violinist played hymns rector
Walter Scott (wearing amice, alb and
stole, which are among the vestments that
keep Archgaiters Wright awake o’ nights)
took the backsliding child of Israel in for
a dip. After priest and neophyte had
changed into dry things in a tent on the
river bank, the procession went back to St.
Thomas’s Church, where Bishop Le Fanu
was waiting to confirm the new Christian,
who had thus publicly notified his intention
of eating pork -whenever he pleased.
The gentle shepherd who looks after the
Anglican collection plate at Mackay is wor-
ried about the frequency with which crook
thrums appear therein. He writes in the
local Church Messenger :
I should be glad of any suggestions as to what
ought to be done with the big and growing stock of
bad coins which have been found in the collection
plate. Perhaps someone could think of an appro-
priate use that might be made of them if they
were melted down. In the meantime those who put
them in the plate might note that they are of no use
to the church, and that God does not accept the
sacrifice of what is of no value either to the giver
or to anybody else.
The Deity’s refusal to accept bad coins might
not matter so much if bankers and trades-
people didn’t follow His example so enthusi-
astically.
Crawford, who went out in the Clermont
flood, was a gritty fellow pursued by hard
luck. Some eight years ago he won the
Mt. Morgan seat in the Labor interest.
Within two years the party expelled him for
accepting the referendum verdict on the Bible-
in-State-schools question, as he had pledged
hi mself to do on the hustings. Thereupon Craw-
ford, in place of cadging for a Government
billet, studied for the law, qualified as a
barrister, and on his defeat at Mt. Morgan
settled down to practice, first at Mt. Morgan,
later at Clermont. He was just building up
a respectable business in the latter town
when the sluices opened and obliterated Cler-
mont —and Crawford, too. He has a brother
in the N. S. Wales Parliament.
“Boro-Belle” : There’s money in egg-shells.
A southerner who came to live up North
bought a small cutter and spent his week-
ends and holidays cruising amongst the
islands between Mackay and Bowen in quest
of birds’ eggs. At the end of a year he had
over a thousand blown shells in a variety of
delicate shades and sizes—from the tiny
sandpiper’s to those of- the great sea eagle,
whose nest consists of a ton and a half of
firewood stacked to the height of the tallest
tree on some water-washed fragment. An
egg-collector, stepping lightly, bought the lot
for £3OO.
The Dead Hand stretched out over Cler-
mont the other day with dreadful conse-
quences. The tow r n, born in the ’so’s, was
AN INSPIRATION .
VOICE ( from above): “Is that you, William ?”
BROWN (with simulated emotion): “Falsh pershured woman —you expecter ’nurrer
fell'r ”
built between a sandy creek and a lagoon.
The pioneer bark and calico palaces were
planked down anywhere on this low-lying
ground. Gradually the bark and hessian
changed to wood and iron, and then to wood
and brick, and the few fossickers became a
mixed community of' 2000. But the site of
the main part of the village didn’t change—
it still roosted, in foolish defiance of possi-
bilities, between Sandy Creek and the lagoon,
where the Dead Hand placed it over 60
years ago. Then the rain fell as though an
ocean had been turned upside down, and the
bewildered inhabitants, jambed in between
the flooded and overflowing creek and lagoon,
found themselves engulfed” before they knew
what was happening. It is certain that at
least 60 of them were swept away ; and the
casualty-list is still incomplete. “Exception-
ally heavy monsoonal rains,” say the meteor-
ologists ; but it was the Dead Hand that
turned Queensland’s annual salvation into a
curse.
“M. Jay” : When the deluge tumbled upon
Clermont the luckless town was woefully
short of coffins. Which recalls an incident.
Over 20 years ago a well-known Brisbane
P.M., who was then chairman of the Cler-
mont hospital commitee, prophetically fearing
that some dreadful scourge would overwhelm
the town and carry away a big batch of souls,
recommended that at least six coffins be kept
on hand to combat the unforeseen visitation.
The Rip Van Winkles on the committee
laughed the loud laugh of scorn, and told the
foolish chairman that Clermont would never
want as many as six boxes at a time, and
that, in any case, it was an extravagance that
could not be tolerated. So the undertaker
was never approached. What sort of a shoe
would have been flung at Mr. Chairman had
he suggested a reserve supply of 60 instead of
six ?
“MacTolga”: Up North I can show a
cedar just 24ft. in diameter. Not a mile
away from this sapling there is another
acting as an umbrella fer half an acre of
ground. When the .Lake Eacham Hotel is
started by the State, this giant will form
■ one of the scenic, marvels of the Eacham
funicular railway.
The old coolabah tree which Leichhardt
marked over 70. years ago at Taroom —it is
right in the centre of the main street—has
so far survived the vandal. The “L” is
still visible, but part of the “1844” is over-
grown. Somebody chopped a niche to sig-
nify the height of the last flood, and a sur-
veyor cut a bench-mark on the trunk; but
the limit was reached when a council
employee started to hack down the tree
altogether. The Lands Department has
now promised an iron rail to protect it.
Apropos of the Police Commissioner’s re-
signation, Home Sec. Bowman’s death was
due in some measure to a caucus row on the
question of forcing Cahill’s retirement. Bow-
man, who had already had more than one
seizure, attended the party meeting, got terri-
bly excited over the issue, and fell down on
his way back to his office. By the way, a
grant of £lOOO is being made to Bowman’s
widow.
Unforgiven.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
‘Come close to the roots of the grass, dead
mouth,
For I have a kiss for you to-day ;
Look up from the sun-warmed earth, dear
eyes,
That almost fretted their hue away !
“The bluebells spring from their wells, I
think.
Listen awhile, O small ghost-ear,
And grow to your shell-fine curve again.
For I have a tale you would joy to hear.
“Lift up, sweet breasts, with a quickened
breath ;
Pulse, little throat, in your grave-clothes
deep;
You will listen to me at last, I know,
When I bid you dream through your grass-
bound sleep.
“Stretch out, slim arms, from your clovers
there,
Moisten your lips with your small red
tongue;
Let us beckon the bannered hours again,
The march of days when we both were
youngJ
“Oh, believe that I loved you all the time!”
A moth swoops low in the quiet grass;
There is no reply where the daisies grow—
Only the shadow of wings that pass.
Then the green grass shivers, the daisy
rocks.
“Does she heed at last, to my love grown
wise?”
And a ripple comes from the grave of her
(For the dead , can laugh) as she answers
“Lies!”
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, January 6, 1917.
My Dear Thargomindaii, —
The tragedy at Clermont lias cast us down
into gloom. It is heartbreaking that after
having been baked by droughts for the last
few seasons, Central Queenslanders should
now be so stricken with the other hand of
Fate. Death and destitution ! In one case a
whole family, mother, father and four chil-
dren, were drowned. Food is being distri-
buted, but clothing is urgently needed, and
the Government will carry free of charge
parcels addressed to the Mayor of Clermont.
We are going to have another Button Day
to help along recruiting, and every patriotic
citizen who hands over 12d. for a decoration
is expected to at the same time part with his
or her name and address, accompanied by a
promise to work for some war cause. The
promoters mention that “naturally they will
not care to dispose of buttons to eligibles not
in khaki.” So, if you are a returned soldier,
Thargomindaii. wear your colors, or if you
have been turned down be sure you stick in
your hat an affidavit to that effect. Several
returned soldiers who do not plaster them-
selves with their battalion badges have been
urged to “get into khaki” by well-meaning
flappers. Come to think of it, well-meaning
flappers are a wearisome item in the furni-
ture of life.
Hylda Davenport, a Brisbane girl whost
fine soprano voice was trained in Europe, has
been in town for a few days, looking up old
friends. She is in love with the Blatherskite
of Berlin. Ilis war swank jolted her from
her chosen path, and instead of touring
Europe in grand opera she has been appear-
ing in musical comedy. However, if pluck
and brains count for anything, as of course
they do, the career is a winner when the dove
cooes.
Two more engagements. Miss Lucy Dick-
son, daughter of a late ex-Premier, has
accepted a binding ring from solicitor Alex.
McNab. She is clever and charming, and the
gent, is extremely popular. (Which doesn’t
mean, my dear, that he isn’t clever and
charming, too.)
Also, Commander R. Bowen has booked a
promise from Corinne Bruce Nicol. She has
done good work for patriotic movements since
the war began and is a clever artist and a
good musician. Some men have all the
luck !
Major and Mrs. Pendlebury leave us by
mail train on Tuesday. Yesterday Mrs.
Malcolm Cameron buttered the farewell
buns, and there was a little farewell gift in
between tea-sips.
Allies’ Garden City has been closed as far
as patriotic funds are concerned after mak
ing a profit of £4500. The Mayoress of Bris-
bane subsequently urged that the “City”
should be opened again for the benefit of
the sufferers in the Clermont floods. Good
souls who were due for a rest cheerfully
agreed to carry on for another 10 days. There
are more than military officers who should be
saluted in these strenuous times.
Mrs. Adolph Feez has gone to Sydney to
say good-bye to a son who sets out Hun-
strafing soon. A cousin of the youthful sol-
dier, Capt. Conran, is a bright particular star
in the fliers’ brigade. His mother was
Minnie Mole, a former Queensland belle.
Dick O’Hara, who is married to the
youngest of the Fisken trio, has been fight-
ing an attack of typhoid on his Irish acres,
where he settled after selling his Banana
sheep run. Worse still, the O’Hara infant
also contracted the complaint. So the
missus has had no time left over to tend
sick soldiers.
“Rosna’s”, note from London :
The Vernon Redwoods, of Toowoomba, gave a vocal
recital in the .Rohan Hall the other night and
struck the stiffest fog seen in this city for years. It
was so thick the taxis climbed lamp-posts and the
motor-’buses got lost and went ambling sorrowfully
round and round Trafalgar-square looking for Criekle-
wood and Tooting. Nevertheless a good many people
nobly risked their lives to pass some flora at the
musical ex-member for Toowoomba and his wife,
who have polished their top C’s in Italy and else-
where.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

BEAUTIFUL hair adds immensely to
the personal magnetism of both men
and women. Actresses and smart women
are ever on the look-out for any harmless
thing that will increase the natural beauty
of their hair. The latest method is to use
pure stallax as a shampoo on account of
the peculiarly glossy, fluffy and wavy effect
which it leaves. As stallax has never been
used much for this purpose it comes to the
chemist only in sealed original packages,
enough for twenty-five or thirty shampoos.
A teaspoonful of the fragrant stallax gran-
ules, dissolved in a- cup of hot water, is more
than sufficient for each shampoo. It is very
beneficial and stimulating to the hair, apart
from its beautifying effect.
* * * *
IT is astonishing the number of women
who suffer from the humiliating afflic-
tion of superfluous hair, and I am sorry to
say that through ignorance or wrong treat-
ment the majority of them have made matters
considerably worse by causing the hairs to
grow more quickly and stronger than before.
It only requires a little thought to realise
the folly of it all. Removing the surface
growth for a few days may give temporary
relief, but in order to obtain permanent
results the hair roots must be killed. Caustic
depilatories are worse than useless, and on
no account should the hairs be pulled out.
Apart from the electric needle, which is _ a
costly and painful process, any chemist will
tell you that the best home treatment is
pure powdered pheminol, which has a de-
vitalising effect upon the hair roots, in addi-
tion to which it removes all trace of the
objectionable hair in a few seconds, and the
recommended treatment gradually, but
surely, dissolves out the hair follicles, which
is the only method of permanently ridding
oneself of this terrible disfigurement. [6

WOWSER ROSES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rich and rare are the wowser’s roses.
Not a bud on these trees of mine!
But when he in his bed reposes
I climb into his garden fine!
Here and there do 1 glide, acquiring
Beauties heavy with scent and dew ;
Soon a lady will be admiring
Flowers of glory that 1 grew.
Old and plain is the wowser’s missus,
And he daren’t give one away.
These will gain me a dozen kisses.
“Bring them round to the flat to-day !
Oh, how sweet of vo to remember !
What a lot of them! Dear Old Boy!”
She’ll be petting me till December !
Wowser roses will bring me joy!
Long and late in his garden toiling,
Never dreaming where roses go,
When the midsummer sun is broiling,
Working with spade and rake and b:,e.
In the dusk with the cool hose spraying,
Keeping the beauties fresh for me —
Lord! I can hardly keep from saving:
“Thanks! I'll call for them after tea.’’
X.S.W.
KODAK.
■The Hindenburg worship must be bitter
medicine for the Kaiser. The rough soldier
was in disgrace for years because, disregard-
ing all the rules of the game, he beat the
Kaiser at some military manoeuvres, and
later took no trouble to soften the humilia-
tion. When the Kaiser tried to explain
away the disaster, Hindenburg growled : "If
it hadn’t been only manoeuvres, I’d have
driven your Majesty into the Baltic.” Then
Hindenburg went into retirement and re-
mained there drinking till he was wanted.
"D.F.” : The “Rev. Professor” John Howard,
now healing the afflicted in Hobart (Tas. /
and receiving donations from 2s. (id. upwards
(no fees), is none of your one-trick men.
According to his advt. he is a clairvoyant
healer, disease reader, magnopatb, officiating
minister of the Spiritual Church, and
licensed to solemnise marriages. Besides
that he observes that he is going to contest
the Franklin seat in the Reps, at the next
n ederal election and win it.
This was the answer of John Thurstans,
of Dundas (Tas.), to the usual question, put
to him the other day as a witness, about
his trade, occupation or prbfess'on:
“Licensed victualler, storekeeper, butcher,
baker, postmaster and banker.” He didn’t
bother mentioning that he was a farmer
and stockowner as well.
Flight-Commander Ball. D. 5.0., of the
British air corps, is an artist who makes the
deceased Immelmann and Boelke look small.
Recently Ball brought down his 27th Him
machine, and he has participated in over a
hundred air-scraps. He is an uninteresting-
looking youth with the cheerful rectangular
countenance of the average English athlete.
Father Bernard Vaughan, who is leading
the agitation against “race suicide” in Eng-
land, is one of 14 children, and his father
was one of 20. Archbishop Vaughan, form-
erly of Sydney, belonged to the prolific
family.
“Exile”': By police decree the people of
Berlin now have to go to bed at 11.30 p.m.,
when all the pubs close. When I was last
there they never closed day or night all the
year round, nor did the drinking cafes or
the eating restaurants. Six years ago I
went by the Nord Express from London to
Berlin. My associate, a leading electrical
London engineer, and I stayed at the
Ivaiserhof Hotel. It cost us £3 a day each,
but it was worth the money. No hotel in
London can compare with this glittering
palace. Strictly subject to the War Pre-
cautions Act, I _ venture to say that both
Berlin and Vienna are architecturally
superior to Paris and London. They pull
down the aged, soiled, moss-grown buildings
in Berlin and Vienna, while they seem to
cherish fusty, musty, gloomy structures in
London and Paris, and prop them up so as
to keep them open for ever. We went out
to dinner in a music-hall and cafe combined.
Seated in a low gallery running round three
sides of the big hall we dined and watched
a complete vaudeville performance on the
stage. There must have been 3000 people
present. Our dinner of eight courses, witli
wines ordered by my companion, began at
8 o’clock and finished with the last turn
on the stage about 11.30. Then we did a
gin crawl round Berlin from one magnificent
cafe to another for the next three hours. At
3 a.m. I asked the maitre (V hot el of an
enormous cafe-restaurant when he closed the
place, and his proud reply was “Never!”
Burglars who broke into a .South Mel-
bourne grocer’s shop the other night put the
stock of butter to a novel use. They dumped
the large squares of cowfat out of their
boxes, dabbed them all over the office safe
till it was just a huge mound of butter, and
then exploded a charge of dynamite and
blew the door out. The report, muffled
in butter, • was not heard in an adjoining
bedroom, and the thieves got away with £7O
in silver*. Some three years ago burglars at
Ballarat put a tub of butter to a similar
use, but it was the grocer himself they
muffled, sticking his head into a 201 b. mass
when he refused to be still. Perfidious
grease !

marm’s merry-Christinas note :
Did yous send Susie home fer them little pimpels
on her hand? It is notheng. She straches them and
I have stuff from the kemist and put on them she
won t let it stop on 2 minets. Don’t you send her
home becos you get me into a row with her father.

“Gellibrand”: The 8.1. boat Itonus, re-
cently sunk in the Mediterranean, has done
her share of carrying horses and stores for
the A.I.F. Luckily she was empty when her
end came. Before the 8.1. got her she was
owned by old Currie, of Melbourne, who
adopted a curious form of christening. The
names of his vessels began with successive
letters of the alphabet and all ended with
“us.” Starting with the Argus, he built the
Clitus, Darius, Euryalus, Fortunatus
(burned at sea), Gracchus, Hymettus.
Itonus. Janus. Then the 8.1. bought him
out. I forget the B boat, but it wasn’t
Bacchus.

Though a few “Live to eat,” the great
majority “Eat to live,” but when your
digestion is faulty, eating causes your life
to be a misery. To propei’ly nourish your
body your food must be thoroughly digested
and its various constituents pass to the
organs for which they are intended.
When this is not done the food does you
no good, but rather harm, for it lies in
your stomach, causing poisonous gases
and disorders of the stomach —causes con-
stipation, which means that the food, in-
stead of being expelled through the bowels,
lies there, and the poisonous matter is
absorbed into the system. All this can
be avoided by taking a course of Cham-
berlain’s Tablets. They aid digestion,
cleanse the stomach, stimulate the liver
and bowels to perform their work, and in-
vigorate the system in so doing. Cham-
berlain’s Tablets are the safest Cathartic
to take, for they perform their work so
gently and naturally that there is no fear
of inflammation following.

get when you buy an eigliteenpenny box

and liver. They eliminate all impurities
and tone the system at the same time.

of trouble. When the stomach fails to
perform its functions the bowels become
deranged, the liver and kidneys congested,
causing numerous diseases, the most fatal
of which are painless and therefore the
more to be dreaded. The important thing
is to restore the stomach and liver to a
healthy condition, and for this purpose
no better preparation can be used than
Chamberlain’s Tablets.
If you were able to live just right all
the time there would be little need
for medicine, less need for doctors. But
some little thing is constantly coming
along to put us out of balance —exposure,
change of diet or drinking water, irregu-
lar meals are little things that will at
times upset the best of us. Nothing
particular the matter, but we don’t feel
good. At such times the wise among us
will take a few doses of Chamberlain’s
Tablets with certain knowledge that they
will put us right, right away.
Many women habitually suffer from
headaches which make life a daily purga-
tpry, _ Frequently they are caused by in-
digestion, and to relieve them permanently
you must remove the cause. For indi-
gestion, biliousness, constipation, disorders
of the stomach and liver there is nothing
better than Chamberlain’s Tablets. Head-
ache cannot exist when Chamberlain’s
Tablets are taken.
Back up a sewer and you will poison
the whole neighborhood, clog up the liver
and bowels and your system is full of
undigested food, which sours and fer-
ments —this means indigestion, headache,
bad breath, yellow skin and everything
that is horrible. Chamberlain’s Tablets
quickly stop fermentation in the stomach,
and they make the liver lively, tone up
the bowels and set the whole machinery
going.
Much ill-health is due to impaired diges-
tion. If the stomach fails to perform its
functions the whole system becomes de-
ranged. A few doses of Chamberlain’s
Tablets is all you need. They will
strengthen your digestion, stimulate your
liver and regulate your bowels, entirely
removing that miserable feeling due to

WHEREAS by an Order of the High Court of
Justice Chancery Division dated 17th July 1916
made in the matter of the Estate of GEORGE
POCOCIv deceased and in an action of PITHIE and
another v. POCOCK and others (1916 P. 821) the
following inquiry was directed namely:—
“An Inquiry whether FRANCIS ALDERSEY
TURNOUR POCOCK (a son of GEORGE PEARCE
POCOCK) is living or dead and if dead when he
died and whether he died under the age of 21 years
and if he attained the age of 21 years whether he
died testate or intestate and who is now beneficially
entitled to his share under the Will of the Testator
GEORGE POCOCK.”
Notice is hereby given that the said FRANCIS
VLDERSEY TURNOUR POCOCK or any person or
persons . claiming to be entitled under the said

TURNOUR POCOCK is the son of George Pearce
Pocock and Fanny Pocock. He sailed from England
for Adelaide Australia in July 1863 being then of
the age of 16 years. He is believed to have gone

of he was going to New South Wales.

l N DIVORCE. IN THE SUPREME COURT OF
1 NEW ZEALAND. NORTHERN DISTRICT. No. 991.
Between ELIZABETH MACKENZIE, of the City of
Auckland, wife of Alexis Greig Mackenzie, Petitioner,
and ALEXIS GREIG MACKENZIE, late of Auckland,
Canvasser, Respondent.
To ALEXIS GREIG MACKENZIE, formerly of
Auckland, Canvasser, but now of parts unknown.
TAKE NOTICE that a Citation bearing date the
18th day of November, 1916, has issued out of this
Court citing you to appear and answer the Petition
of ELIZABETH MACKENZIE of Auckland, praying
for a dissolution of her marriage with you on the
grounds that you have deserted her for 5 years and
upwards without just cause. TAKE NOTICE that
by an Order of this Court dated the Bth day of
December, 1916, it was ordered that personal ser-
vice on the Respondent of the Petition and Citation
be dispensed with, and that service be effected by
advertising an abstract of the Petition and Citation
to be settled by the Registrar of this court at Auck-
land twice in the Sydney Bulletin and .twice in one
daily newspaper published in the City of Auckland,
and that an appearance and answer be filed within 90
days after the date of the insertion of the last of
such advertisements and that the trial be at the
First sittings of this Honorable Court in Divorce, to
be held at Auckland after the expiration of 110 days
from the last appearance of such advertisements as
aforesaid. If you wish to enter an appearance you
must within the time above-mentioned attend in
person or by a Solicitor at the Registry of this
Court at Auckland, and there enter an appearance
in a book provided for the purpose. In default of
your so doing the Court will proceed to hear the
said Petition, and you will not be allowed to
address the Court.
Dated at Auckland this 18th day of December,
1916.
(Sgd.) R. G. THOMAS, Registrar.
This abstract is published by Archibald Peak, of
Wyndham Street, Auckland, Solicitor for the Peti-
tioner. A copy of the Petition may be inspected at
the office of Messrs. Walker & Peak, Wyndham Street,
Auckland, or at the Auckland Registry of the
Court. Friends of Respondent are requested to for-
ward this advertisement to him.

The Inky Way.
“Copy Buy”: Fred Whitehead, of Mel-
bourne Herald, one of the best of Yarraside
pressmen, is exchanging his fountain-pen for
a bayonet. During the conscription campaign
lie was lent by the Herald to the National
Referendum Council and battled heart and
soul to secure a "Yes” vote. If conscrip-
tion hau been carried, as a married man he
would have gone in his turn ; when it was
defeated he decided that it was no longer
desirable to stand upon the order of going,
but to go; so he waited only to adjust his
private affairs and then enlisted. Whitehead,
who used to be a public-schoolmaster, has
adorned many parts of the Inky Way. He
was. chief-of-staff of Adelaide Daily Herald
in its early days, and was one of the
Argus’s Parliamentary men before joining
the office round the corner. He has always
been an active member of the A.J.A., and
edits the Australian Journalist when
secretary Brickhill is away on an arbitra-
tion or such-like job-
* * * *
“Weka”: Done for and “dissed” at Alex-
andra (M.L.), John Johnson Ramsay, editor
of the local Herald. The luck that has
made many a lesser light shine brightly was
never his. As a lad, his first job in M.L.
was driving a team on the Otago Goldfields.
He next shouldered an encumbered farm,
and after a drought booted it to Dunedin.
The dredging boom was in full swing, so
T.J. stuck out his plate as a sharebroker.
Subsequently he took the first bridle-track
to the Taieri, and ran the Taieri Advocate.
Having no success, he hoofed it to Omakau,
the terminus of the Otago Central Railway,
and just got a forwarding agency going when
the Government pushed the line'on to Clyde.
Ten minutes after the railway arrived at
its new destination .T.J. was on the premises,
but he soon headed for Wellington, and a
little later was appointed manager of the
Weraroa Experimental Farm. Then he
went to Te Aroha, had three consecutive
shots at Parliament and missed. A few
years ago he returned to the goldfields and
purchased the Alexandra Herald, which he
was editing when Peter sent his record to
press.
* * * *
1v.8.M.”: The hustle of Mclntosh on
.Sydney Sunday Times has started the Sun
people'moving. The management has seen
that the Sunday Sun, if it is to keep where
it wants to be, must have special ideas and
special brains shoved into it. The wise men
of Goshen can tell you all the shapes the
ideas will take, of course, but all that is
actually apparent to date is that papa Deni-
son has switched all the time and grey
matter of editor Monty Grover over to the
Sunday edition. Campbell Jones remains
Lord High Managing-Editor, Adam McCay
is editing the daily edition, Del McCay gets
into brother Adam’s shoes as associate-
editor, and Bill Lyne, as soon as his hoppy
leg permits, will be news-editor.
Laurie Taylor, the Sydney poster-artist
now in London, whose black-and-white
work is known to Bulletin readers, has a
Picture, “Peel Harbor, 1.0. M.,” in the
Autumn Exhibition at the Walker Galleries,
lie is also running a series of war drawings
in the Bystander, and designed the cover
for the Christmas number of Pearson’s.
* * * *
Will Hope, a Maoriland black-and-whitbr
and contemporary of David Low, is pushing
himself under the notice of Yankee editors.
The other day his persistence secured him a
post on a St. Louis daily, for which he is
now doing three cartoons a week.
A FELLOW-CRAFTSMAN ENLISTS.
(To F.J.W.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A pressman once a pressman aye—of most of us
’tis true;
But you have done with black and white: the Bed
God calls to you.
The column that your headlines bore another’s job
becomes —
The column now that gives you space is headed by
the Drums.
For all you played the game so well, ’twas merely
make-believe;
Fate meant you for a sergeant, with a chevron on
your sleeve;
Though copy sagged the pockets of the coat you
used to don,
We knew we’d see you, some day, with your dusty
khaki on!
Your old employ is ended well; the new is well
begun ;
Of all assignments booked to you not one was badly
done ;
The files you filled bear witness that your work was
found worth while—
’Twill prove its value now you fill another kind of
file.
Though featly you employed the steel wherewith
your pars, were writ,
The nib sufficed not for your need : a broader blade
more fit
You found to reach the hearts of men, more worthy
to be met;
The wit and grit that sped your pen shall speed
your bayonet!
We place beside the much you did the little that
you said—
The nonchalance that oft deceived, the silence that
misled,
The thoughtful taciturnity, the unresponsive mood
Beside the vigor of your work —and know that it
was good.
Henceforward must the formes be locked wherein no
longer lurk
The stories where the tradesman’s touch bespoke
your handiwork;
Henceforward copy shall be spiked which other
hands must write ;
Henceforward you shall live the things you put in
black and white.
0 fellow-craftsman, take this wish for all that it is
worth—■
A pledge of fair remembrance to the lads of all the
earth!
Your comrades rise to watch you go.... Right well
they understand
That he must have a soldier’s heart who has the
craftsman's hand.
VICTORIA.
G. I.
