Folly Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE played for stakes we could not
win.
(God bless the Devil!)
The peach was ripe on the tree of sin
Vnd the lawns of Eden lush and level.
tvl saw the winking planets spin ;
To a Columbine world we were Harlequin.
(God bless the Devil!)
The choir-boys passed in pure array.
(God bless the Devil!)
Would they sing us white as the bough ot
May, ■ , . e
Whose souls were red with the wine ot
revel? , . .
But a brown maid tripped through the
orchard way, , , ~
And a white maid’s feet in the dusk would
stray.
(God bless the Devil!)
A priest to fill the spicbd bowls.
(God bless the Devil!) _
Mirth treads her stockings to skin-pink holes I;
But the wine in the cask will keep its level.
Drink high! Drink low ! Though a kiss be
coals i
To burn a harvest of sin-glad souls —
God bless the Devil 1
Oh, shrive ine clean and let llic go !
(God bless the Devil!)
There’s a joy tihchased in the Woods below
Where Pan is piping all foois to revel,
with sin for a shaft and song for a bow,
1 follow the way that the glad knaves know.
(God bless the Devil!)
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
Petroleum Hunting,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WITH thi-ee Fijians, Saifo, Beni and
Another, With an Arab engineer
and a returned Australian of
Gallipoli, We left Suva on a good tide
through Lahthala Bay to the delta country
of that noble river, the Ilewa, as like the
Clarence as Viti LevU is like North Queens-
land. It is navigable 80 miles, and the
great caiiefiolds that feed Nausori Mill and
the mud-fat cattle standing in the high grass
made it the more like the rivet from Har-
wood towards Grafton. There is a great
exteiit Of this delta country Of the ReWa
and its tributaries cutting up the country
frOnl the rivet eastwards to the sea with
Small rivers sUch as the Wainibokasi And
the Nakelo. So past Butata to the sea at
NavUlOa, tlieh Nambua and the island of
Bail, once the throiie of Cakobau. Leaving
the thatch houses on their coral patch _we
went to sea again or rather aground. Saito,
refreshed by eating great wads of food what
time his eyes rolled their whites in ecstasy,
ieaped overboard. Beni followed, and the
other dragged the launch through the shal-
lows, The retreating tide left acres of
coral trash uncovered; in the shallows
around Rau natives walking, as in the sea
far from land, dragged their flat-bottoms
over the shoals. Thus at last we came to
Verata, Beni’s village on the mainland.
The cliffs of tufas and mudstone hid _ a
village of long thatched houses, breadfruit,
banana, mango and dracenas; plump women
hUrging chocolate babies; yellow brown girls
With frizzled hair; small boys with their
hair drawn to topknots and a frizzled ring
of hair at the neck; decrepit old natives,
thin in the shanks; a woman bent double
and hobbling on a stick to the village spring.
As we walked up the steep shell-paved track,
a small boy and a small girl, three years
old or so, both pot-bellied from much yam
and taro and both naked, each snatched up
a great glossy leaf of the breadfruit and
covered themselves as did Adam and Eve.
Who had told these babies that they were
naked? Nobody before the native women
began to wear slop-chest skirts. So pro-
bably the snake that destroyed Eden was a
draper. Certainly many a home has dated
its ruin from a bargain sale.
In the chief’s house a little girl of four
—one Mariseana —captured me quickly, and
thereafter the children followed us. Thank
God, children are always the same, no mat-
ter how maturity dirties or the foolishness
of old men makes miserable the world;
black, brown or wdiite, children are the same
the world over. So Mariseana stood by
while we were there-—my little friend, who
is the color of a well-polished tan boot. In
the house, while the rain of afternoon fell
and brought the dark, the women at the
mat-making spoke with a fine softness of
voice, the complement of these grave, sensi-
tive gentlemen of Fiji. I wanted to get to
work at once, but the soldier warned me of
the dangers of precipitancy with these
people. Beni broke the matter to the Chief
Tavite, who liked our mission not at all.
So we made presents of kava, and the kava
stone began crushing—to have few intervals
of silence before half-past two next morning.
Fowls shrieked without and died for our
dinner; Sairo, bored because he wasn’t eat-
ing, told the assembly that the soldier had
been four times wounded at Gallipoli; the
soft-voiced women became shrill and the
courteous men eager. So by-and-bye they
had his shirt rolled down from the shoulder
to see the places where Sir Bullet made his
exits and his entrances; and some with-
drew to bring others of the village and see
the man home from the wars. They must
put their fingers into the cicatrices too —a
natural thing, Doubting Thomas being not
so incredulous as inquisitive.
Mariseana, although four years old, knew
already how to use her eyes, and squatted
by me holding my hand. The lollies arrived
and the children sat in heaven ; but the one
half-caste child in the lot showed his greedy
breeding, always with his hand out and
lifting the spoils of the gentlemanly full-
bloods. Then the kava came with all cere-
mony and presented on the bended knee;
to be drunk in a draught to handclaps and
cries of “Bula” and “Vinaka,” as the drink-
ing in one draught shows good health and
good wind.
While the kava was prepared they brought
us another honor —the singing of the kava
song, the man presenting the bowl, held on
a level with his eyes, almost crouching as
he offered it to the cardinal points of the
compass. Followed the presentation of the
whale’s tooth to the chief, the long, grace-
ful thanks like a prayer, the eloquence of
the gratified like an invocation. I drank
the astringent peppery and rather soapy-
flavored water —a drying of the mouth fol-
lowed, and a coolness as when the forehead
is rubbed with menthol, and a cleaning of
the mouth as by olives. The yangona, or. to
give it the name mostly used in foreign
parts, kava, is a great crop these days, for
the Fijian doesn’t grow the root now—h§
buys it of the white man, the Indian or the
Chinese storekeeper. It has the property of
making iridescent the tanoa or wooden kava-
bowl after long usage, and the cocoauut
shell used as drinking cups. These are
scraped very thin and are of much beauty of
form for the chiefs; the inside of the shell
long used shines with a pearly iridescence,
deepening into the bluish purple on the
skin of passion-fruit. I do hope I am not
iridescent in the interior,
The lights made that beautifully-built
house of round timber and walls of orna-
mented reed and heavy thatch more decora-
tive ; the big cowries, denoting a chief’s
home, hung from the guests’ entrance. The
moneyed Indian in Fiji is not content With
one of these native houses, because they are
too cheap. lie builds a galvanised-iron
horroi*, having apparently learned that fool-
ishness of a missionary, who knows that
God can be happy only in a tin house.
Meantime the kava went Up and down
and they talked war and Occasionally of my
business there, but skating with much deli-
cacy around the talk of a petroleum seepage
in the village. Then they sent the commoner
men away, and an old woman who talked
out of her tUrtt, saying that in her days
the “karaseni’’ in the spring had been so
thick that it crackled ih the fire when it
burned, Was told that women must be
silent in the presence of men, and was sent
out of the house. They got back to the
war, and the returned soldier described a
fight with the Kai Germans and the Kai
Turks, in which he Used the term “Kai
Scotchmen”; whereat one old man asked
the Big Chief who were the “Kai Scotch.”
To that the Chief replied : “The Scotch are
the teeth of the English.”
Rut all we could learn of the petroleum
seepage Was the story which had brought us
there. Up to thirty years ago it was visible,
and then a missionary named Lindsay, either
to protect the native from losing the thing
he couldn’t use or to leave it as an asset
for the missionary when his missionising
should be overpast, commanded his flock
to fill in the bathing-place and never to
show the seepage to a white man lest the
ghost responsible for the petroleum should
bring a curse to the village, causing them
to lose their land. For this had they filled
the hole in and their superstition showed
in their faces when they demurred to dis-
covering to us the site of the old bathing-
place.
Now, a bigger chief than our host had
ordered the village to respect us and serve
us and do all we should desire; none should
laugh at the white chief, all should do
him honor; the freedom of the city was his
and the people. Wherefore the golden yel-
low girls of the meke cast down their eyes.
Te Deum laudamus.
But when the talk got on to “karaseni”
the chief’s name became oyster, and next
morning he went bush, so that we did not
see him again. He believed in the ghost all
right.
And there were others. After about eight
drinks of kava, had with all due ceremony,
a dignified gentleman, full of the grog which
is non-alcoholic, but has some drug quality
such as cocaine, so that it leaves the head
clear and numbs the legs, arose and went to
the guest door. Outside the rain fell thickly
and the gentleman in the blue sulu was very
careful of his hair; keeping his head in
cover of the thatched eaves, he shouted
words that were certainly insulting and full
of bad language. He then spat twice
viciously at the rain, and returned to his
seat near the kava bowl, accepting the
applause carefully and as if it were his due.
“To whom did he use the bad language?”
I asked.
“To the ghost. He called the ghost filthy
names, spat on him and sent him to
Navilua.”
“Why Navilua?”
“This town used to fight the Naviluans,
and our boys have no respect for them now.”
“And will the ghost stay where he is
put?”
“No —he’ll come back, and that chap will
spit on him and send him somewhere else.”
Twice in the next hour the dignified
gentleman, more and more filled with kava,
arose and avaunted the ghosts. He sent
them among the horned cattle once, bidding
them, with great contempt, to live with the
bulumakau. Once he spat on two ghosts at
a time, and yet again he spat so wonderfully
that the applause sounded as if he had
knocked seven bells out of the spooks that
time.
Kava and presentation and speech and the
kava song—for which the drinker must
drink slowly—and more long draught and
the beating of the lali for rhythm, old men
still arriving to see the soldier’s wounds, ana
a hen bright-eyed in the lamplight, bringing
forth her clutch of chickens in the chief’s
house to escape the mongoose which has
eaten all the ground game. It is a terrible
insult to call a Fijian a mongoose. Another
speech that none were to be rude to the
white men (unnecessary with this fine
people, the richest in the world because they
have enough, and so well fed they are too
happy to be impolite) and that all we did
was to be respected.
They brought the girls to dance —a dance
of posturing while sitting, a melancholy
song for accompaniment; melancholy as the
lives of women in poverty. They were young,
pretty, copper-colored and well oiled with
cocoanut, as the chiefs were with kava ; bare-
backed, their bosoms covered, flowers at the
wrists and in their hair. That bare-backed
ballet in “So-Long, Letty.” came from here.
Their music was beautifully harmonised, and
seemed to be all about death and sorrow.
And after midnight they brought in the
young bucks for a meke of war; their fine
strong faces full of insult and menace;
masters of pantomime moving as one. They

m
should be the most easily-drilled soldiers on
earth, and they made an experience unfor-
gettable—their beautiful bodies glistening in
the lamplight, their war chant and the
hissing of the leaves carried in bunches at
their wrists.
.The ghost-destroyer now arose, half-numb
with kava, went to the guest door, shrieked
insult at the ghosts and tried to drive them
out of the island with one comprehensive
spit. Alas, his mouth was dry, he could
not spit, and came back to his seat not to
applause but to derisive laughter. Rut his
stupid ghost was keeping us away from a
perfectly good seepage, and I am glad the
kava dried him up. So “Samothe” and
“Good-nights,” and the chief took me to a
bed of mats with a fan on the pillow—the
pillow embroidered with “Good-morning,
my dear.”
And now we had to break superstition
with a miner’s right.
Red-eyed and smooth of mouth from the
kava, Bulumakau, whose spittle failed before
the ghosts, made back to the kava bowl with
a sore head. Sairo’s face lengthened to 2ft.
Gin. as it seemed that the trip had been for
nothing. Superstition had made the morn-
ing full of difficulty; they believed that the
“karaseni” in the bathing-hole was old
Nick, whom they had set on fire.
Rut the kava stone was going again, and
by-and-bye kava was handed by the maker
on his knees; though, for all the respect, we
were still up against the same granite super-
stition.
There were still curious old men who
called to probe the returned soldier’s wounds
with their fingers, breaking into monosylla-
bles of wonderment followed by long in-
drawn breaths.
Our man Beni began talking softly to the
chief of “karaseni”; old grey-haired men sat
around talking in whispers after we had
eaten ; while that confident, cheerful ruffian
Sairo sat eating everything in sight with a
greed learned of the white man, all his early
etiquette forgotten. After he had eaten all
and in pursuance of an arrangement that he
should go prospecting among some of the
older men, he left us with the whispering
old men and the kava bowl.
After much struggling with the supersti-
tion of Old Nick they led us down the
beach—as the event proved, in the wrong
direction —to a seepage of iron; and we
made westerly along the beach under the
cliffs of mudstone towards the village again.
They showed us a small hole which seemed
to hold nothing but, stagnant water, and a
most fearsome smell, plainly the result of
vegetable decomposition ; then at last to give
them time we said “Good-bye” and
“Samothe,” and waded out over the coral
shoal to the boat, and so to sea, leaving them
more than half-ashamed of their supersti-
tion and liking us well.
After that away to Namenua—to get news
of home by going away from it. At Namenua,
in a house in a clearing on the seaward side
of the town, we found a mentor —a white
man, Rill, whom the natives call “Missa
Viliame,” otherwise the comi of Never Mind.
A comi is a lord of the manor, as differenti-
ated from an hereditary chief of a village,
and the comi of Namenua is a grey-haired,
grey-bearded, spectacled, studious old man,'
very light in color for a Fijian and grave
enough to be a professor of biology.
From Verata to Namenua the dip of the
mudstones is more north-easterly, or sea-
ward, the strata being tilted inland.. Here
on a mud-bottomed pool was an unmistak-
able scum of petroleum, and on this evidence
the comi of Never Mind took me along to
the comi of Namenua. Fie is a man of much
intelligence, and after a smoke and a shell
of kava he told us that the seepage of petro-
leum by Verata had showed in the bathing-
hole of Sara Tarnbua. So after a decent
wait we engaged three men of Namenua as
miners and went to sea again.
I awoke early to the sound of the conch
blown by the mayor of the village to tell
people to wake up. It is the best job a
mayor can have,
Down at the hole sunk last night there
was a plain seepage of petroleum scum com-
ing from seaward, and making quickly from
the dense mudstone at the toe of the cliff.
To be more than certain that no tricks had
been played overnight, I had the hole baled
out, a sump sunk and rebaled and then
watched for three hours till the hole filled
again and the petroleum scum showed
thickly enough for a sample. They led us
to other seepages then. The ghost being
broken, I might now see all ; but most of
these others were plainly oxide scums seep-
ing out of the cliffs and surfacing the reef
pools.
A long yarn ensued which inquiry told
me was a discussion over the compensation
to be paid for a diseased cocoanut-palm
should we cut it down, and then a long
burlesque business conversation, with the
kava stone going again. Also the comi of
Never Mind had a burlesque row with me
as to whether 1 was to pay him a million
or merely five hundred thousand’ and this
aroused a native who was anxious that I
should not be displeased, and in honest and
disinterested collusion with me plied the
comi with kava, and every time the emptied
cocoanut shell was thrown down begged him
to sign the paper for the half-million.
The fine choir of school children began to
sing under a bread-fruit by the school as
we pursued superstition for its hidden spoil,
they singing with a single mind while men
become, colder and skins like rhinoceros hide
grow one over the other and around the
heart.
And there was Jonni. the big. handsome
half-Tongan servant of the comi, about 25
years old, and not yet beginning to be
spoiled. With one good oar and one broken
one he went to Levuka in a flat-bottom and
back, a distance of 56 miles of open sea,
for 65.. and the last night I was at Namenua
be walked 10 miFs to a store to bring me
some white man’s tobacco.
The children, the least spoiled of all, sang
under the bread-fruit—
Me da songoni le a muri!
Ma na tai ne awai,
which is “Shall We Gather at the River?”
—the tune being my interpreter of the words,
and then—
Ni langa sere mai na angelosa
Ka ni sai liemuni na kena dau
No_ sere me yacova tuna siga
Qai tuba vala noda maniarau.
which is to say—
Darker than night life's shadows close around us
And like benighted men we miss our mark ;
God hides Himself and grace has scarcely found us
Till death finds out his victims in the dark.
All the children followed us to the sea edge
over the flood of mudstones bared by the
tide. With the samples in the bottles and
the ghost of 30 years laid or departed to
Navilua or the Bulumakau we raised anchor
and ran between the reefs to Namenua —
being later bound for Ban, the Wainibokasi
and the Rewa, Suva, Sydney and other
foreign parts.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Dago.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WAS hungry —actually empty. Unfor-
tunately I had no money. On the
morrow —well, to-morrow I was to dine
with somebody’s rich uncle. But to-night—-
I pressed my hands over an aching void
and strove to stifle self-pitying sobs.
Just then I caught sight of a lovely red
lobster —such a beauty! It reposed on a
bed of oysters, and a little Dago, with well-
oiled curls and goo-goo eyes, was arranging
its limbs.
I knew that little Dago. Once I bought
some prawns and he gave me extra measure
because I smiled at him. I wondered if his
heart might not perhaps be very susceptible
and a little judicious flirting provide me
with something to eat. Surely Carmen
I fore’s smiles were worth a lobster!
Nothing like trying, so I went up to the
window and gazed longingly at the fish and
then at the Dago. Naturally, being a man
—and a Dago—he thought 'lie was the centre
of attraction. lie couldn’t see it was my
stomach and not my heart that was empty.
He ceased playing with the lobster and
fidgeted with the oysters, looking at me
under his black lashes and rolling his goo-goo
eyes.
After a few minutes of this I slowly
sauntered into the shop.
“(food evening,” I said, placing my elbows
on the counter and gracefully resting my
chin on my clasped hands.
“G'ood ev’ning, Mees,” said the little Dago,
giving me a bewitching smile with his moist
red lips. “What can I do for you?”
“You can make me happy,” I said, soul-
fully.
The palpitation of his heart became more
evident, and he smiled lusciously.
“Ah, you maka fun of me,” he said, in a
deep, fruity voice that reminded me of the
love scene in an Italian opera.
“You are cruel to think so,” I said, sighing
deeply. “Show me your nicest lobster,
please.”
lie brought one and placed it on the
counter.
“Dis is da beaut,” he said, stroking its
back tenderly.
“What is its name?” I asked, tickling it
under the ear, or where its ear should have
been.
“Just da cray,” he said, showing a double
row of white teeth. Then, leaning over the
counter he added in a soft whisper:
“Don’t often I see you go past, eh? You
live near, perhaps?”
“Perhaps.” I said, straightening one of
the long feelers that hung despondently from
the lobster's brow.
“I know you well,” breathed the little
Dago. "I tiuk you da.bonzer Mees.”
“You funny little Dago,” I said, laughing.
Then, remembering I "was hungry: “You
don’t mind me calling you that?”
“You call me what you please—l love it!”
“Ah.” I said, shaking my head at him,
“you are like all your countrymen—brave,
but fickle.”
“Brave?” he said. “Yes, we are brave—
some of us—but fickle? No! You are
wrong ! I have a bruzzer he love one woman
all hees life! He is twenty-five and she is
forty. Both are devoted”
“Then she must be very wealthy,” I said,
sceptically.
. “But no —it is my bruzzer has da money.
She gave it to him !” he said triumphantly.
“Ah, we can love —we can die of love.”
“And I,” I said, lifting the lobster’s claw,
“I could die of hunger.” I touched it with
my lips, thinking the flavor might stay my
ravening appetite.
Like a flash the little Dago seized the
claw and pressed it to his lips, kissing it
fervently. Then he broke it off and placed
it in a pocket of his vest, over his heart.
I was touched—and I began to fear he
was also.
“Ah,” he said in his vibrating baritone,
“you are da beaut Mees.” He flung out his
arms and caught the lobster in a tight
embrace. A loud crack rewarded his fervor.
“There,” I said, “you have split his shell.”
“Never mind,” he said, wiping some mois-
ture from his shirt sleeve. “I will get you
anuzzer—a better one. I will give it' to
you”
He paused to roll his goo-goo eyes. I hung
on his words with breathless interest. ' A
mournful gurgle trilled from my expectant
gastronomic apparatus.
“Go on!” I said. “You will give it to
me?”
“Yes, I will give it to you if you promees
to go to Manly with me soon—my first night
off.”
“Will you really?” I said with genuine
eagerness.
“Really,” he said solemnly. “We Italiano
never break our word.”
“Ah,” I said, “you are like your noble
Horatio. You are a peach!”
“An’ you,” he said, delighted, “you are da
bonzer Madonna !”
He went to the window and got the large
cray that had evidently only been intended
for show purposes. Then I knew I had
really touched his heart.
“You will tink about da trip if I give you
thoes?” he said as be carefully wrapped it up.
“Think about it!” I cried enthusiastically.
“Why, if you throw in half a dozen oysters
I’ll dream of it, you incomparable Dago !”
It was too easy, but I pay for it in having
to walk a long way round so as not to pass
the little Dago’s shop. In a month, perhaps
I shall be safe. He will be dead then—or
have forgotten.
